	RELIEF SOCIETY REPORT.
		BOX ELDER STAKE.
	Convened Nov. 16th, 1912, Pres. Sarah P.
Stohl presiding. Present, besides Relief So-
ciety stake officers, Pres. Oleen N. Stohl,
Bishop Johnson of Mantua, Sister Sarah
McLelland of general Relief Society board,
Sisters Sarah M. Taylor, Goddard, Jost, and
Coles of the Ogden stake Relief Society.
	Singing “Did You Think to Pray.” Open-
ing prayer by Sister Sophia Jensen. Sing-
ing by the Relief Society choir, “Angry
words, oh let them never.” Roll call showed
two hundred and fifty present. Minutes of
previous forenoon conference read and ap-
proved.
	Pres. Sarah P. Stohl was pleased to see
so many sisters out this morning, and was
very happy to have so many visitors with
us; said we hoped to make this the most
profitable conference we had yet had. May
the Lord bless us with His holy spirit to-
day.
	Sister Julia A. Richards gave a good talk
on genealogy; thought it very necessary to
keep a record of our families; said it was a
work in which there ought to be more in-
terest taken; thought if we wanted our chil-
dren to take interest in this work we would
have to take interest ourselves; said she
thought it was a work which draws out our
love for our relatives and one another. May
the Lord bless all.
	Sister Daisy Madsen gave a report of her
labors in the mission field; said she worked
in the Relief Society in Chicago, where they

[Column 2]
had quite a large society; thought if the
sisters here had the difficulties the sisters
have in the world we would appreciate the
work much better. Reported the work in
Bloomington, Ill.; said the sisters have the
spirit of the Lord with them when they
meet; spoke of the sacrifices some of them
have to make when they join the Church,
and could contrast the difference between
those who had been raised in the gospel and
those who had not; gave some of her ex-
perience in tracting; said her heart was full
of gratitude for the little she had done in
the Relief Society; paid a tribute to the
mothers; said if every Latter-day Saint
would live up to the requirements made of
her, we would be a better example to the
people.
	Pres. Agnes Owens reported the Willard
Relief Society, also Pres. Annie Peters re-
ported the Perry association. The choir
sang the selection, “Daughters of Zion.”
	Sister Sarah McLelland of the General
Board said it was with very much pleasure
she came to visit this conference, and give
the greeting of Sister Wells to the sisters
here; referred to her laboring in the mission
field, with some of the sisters from this
stake, and knew them to be good worthy
sisters, whom she had learned to love; did
not wonder at their goodness in being as-
sociated with such good mothers; had very
much enjoyed the spirit here this morning,
and the good attendance; spoke in
regard to the temptations that are before
our children, and that they did need the
faith and prayers and counsel of the fathers
and mothers, that they may retain the faith.
Referred to genealogical work, and her priv-
lege of visiting the Temple at St. George;
said a few words to the teachers, and said
any position that you hold is worthy of your 
best efforts.
	Sister Coles of the Ogden stake was very
pleased that our president had been kind 
enough to invite them to attend this confer-
ence; had been well paid for coming, in
listening to the talks of the sisters; spoke on 
genealogy work, was very much impressed
with this work, and the greatness of it, and
how the spirit of Elijah was working with
the people of the world in this direction;
her whole heart and soul was in this Relief
Society work.
	Pres. Sarah P. Stohl invited all the sisters
to remain and have lunch.
	Singing, “Oh God, we raise to Thee.”
	Prayer by Bishop Johnson.
	2 p. m.—Singing, “We Thank Thee, O
God, for a Prophet.” Opening prayer by
Bro. W. C. Horsley. Singing by choir,
“Sweet is the Work.” Minutes of previous
afternoon conference read and approved.
	Pres. Sarah P. Stohl was pleased to see
such a good congregation this afternoon,
and hoped all would be able to hear.
	A paper on “The Ideal Home” was given
by Sister Minnie H. Jensen.
	Sister Sevena Madsen gave a report of
her labors in the Northern States Mission.
It had been her privilege to bear her testi-
mony first in a Relief Society meeting. It
was a pleasure to go and do all she could to
assist in Relief Society work; they studied
the “Parent and Child;” was transferred
from Chicago to Des Moines; they had a
mothers’ class there and studied the prin-
ciples of the gospel; reported the work at
Council Bluffs; reported her visit with Pres.

[Column 3]
Horsley and others at the Park Valley and
Rosette wards, this stake; was very much
impressed with the visit.
	Duet and chorus by the choir, “Truth Re-
flects Upon Our Senses.”
	Dr. R. A. Pearse gave a talk on “Public
Sanitation,” and how our committees can
be a help to the Board of Health; spoke on
the inspection of school children for the eye,
ear and throat trouble, and the detriment
these defects were to the child through life;
spoke on the milk supply, and to watch for
contagious diseases; gave ways of keeping
down disease germs; said the people should
demand certain laws that are for the best
good of the people.
	Pres. Sarah M. Taylor of the Ogden
stake was very thankful for the invitation to
come to this conference; had enjoyed it very
much; spoke in regard to the small attend-
ance of young mothers, also in regard to
the work of the teachers; gave a brief report
of the work done in the Ogden stake; was
pleased with the talk on sanitation; 
thought how necessary it was for us
to be organized that we may carry on this
work, and thought we could plan our home
work so that we could get out to Relief
Society meeting; said a few words on gene-
alogical work, also on the history of the
Pioneers. Prayed that the spirit of the
Lord would rest upon us in such a way that
we will do our duty.
	Sisters Daisy and Sevena Madsen ren-
dered a duet, entitled, “God is Love.”
	Sister Sarah McLelland said we are
always glad to have the priesthood with us,
as it always has its good influence. The
paper on “The Ideal Home” and the talk on
sanitation had been very good; paid a trib-
ute to the choir, and had enjoyed the beau-
tiful singing; said there was such a broad
field for the Relief Society; felt that the key-
note for the future was in what Pres. Joseph
F. Smith had said in the last semi-annual
conference in regard to setting our aim 
high, and in regard to immorality in dress;
spoke on honoring the Sabbath day, form-
ing of habits, card playing, and cigarette
smoking; thought if we prayed for those
boys who do these things, the Lord would
help us; spoke on the great work of women,
and felt there was not another people who
had as much to live for as the Latter-day
Saints. May God bless you in your work in
this part of the Lord’s vineyard.
	Stake Pres. Oleen N. Stohl felt the most
important thing before us now was to im-
press our children with faith; thought we
ought to talk more to them, and speak more
of the beauties of the gospel; spoke of the
receiving of the Holy Ghost and the bless-
ing s that follow it; the mothers taking
more interest in the Parents’ classes in the
Sunday School, and participating in the
lessons taught there; felt to ask a
blessing upon the mothers and said they
could do much in instilling good principles
in the lives of our children.
	Pres. Sarah P. Stohl felt we had had
an enjoyable time, together, and thanked
the sisters who had visited us and given us
such good talks and counsel; felt we ought
to be friendly and neighborly.
	By request, the choir sang “Daughters of 
Zion.”
	Benediction by Sister Emelia D. Madsen.
			ANNIE H. LITTLEWOOD,
						Stake Secretary.

SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH,	-	MARCH, 1913


RELIEF SOCIETY DAY.
	The 17th of March is now pretty well
conceded to be Relief Society Day through-
out all Zion. Scarcely a ward or branch
of the Church but on that day holds
a festival of some kind. It may be a party,
a concert, a meeting or a feast, at all events
it is a day of rejoicing. “I now turn the key
for woman,” were the ever memorable
words uttered by the Prophet Joseph Smith
when on the morning of March seventeen,
eighteen hundred and forty-two he organ-
ized the Relief Society. There were present
eighteen women and three men in a room
over the Nauvoo store called the Masonic
Hall, when the prophet uttered the strange
sentence. The society had been duly organ-
ized with a president, two counselors, a sec-
retary, assistant secretary and treasurer; the
name had been decided upon and some dis-
cussion as to the work to be performed, when
Joseph proceeded to give instruction and in
a brief way outline the duties of the society,
during which instruction the words above
quoted were uttered. Remember, in eigh-
teen forty-two not a college in the world
had opened its doors to women. There were
no clubs, societies or organizations of wo-
men any where. Women’s education was
almost entirely along lines of fine accom-
plishments such as fitted her to grace a ball
room or a family parlor but to unfit her to
meet life and its issues without the aid
of man to lean upon. She was trained
much on the theory of the clinging vine and
hothouse notion and not at all fitted for
the strength of self-support or the throes of
adversity; all of which training is charm-
ing and quite sufficient when a sturdy oak
in the shape of a prosperous well-disposed
husband can be guaranteed for ever and for-
ever. But alas for the wars and other con-
ditions that hew down the sturdy oaks and
leave mothers, wives, sweethearts to stand
and fight alone life’s battles!
	Glance back seventy-one years; what a
marvelous change for woman—professors,
doctors, lawyers, bachelors of art, literature,
science: states women, police women, may-
ors, members of city councils, juvenile
judges, and numerous other positions held
by woman while in matters of education
she claims her place on the same basis as
her brother, the basis of intellectuality.
	The key was indeed turned for woman.
That glorious thought vibrated throughout


the earth, over mountain and sea, and the
daughters of the universe have received its
benediction. Written words could no more
tell the magnificent work of the members of
the Relief Society than they could describe
the perfume of a flower or the song of the
meadow lark, for like ministering angels
they comfort the sorrowful, relieve the dis-
tressed, feed the hungry, clothe the naked,
wait upon the sick, relieve the distressed,
scattering glad news and cheer along their
daily walk, giving in the true Christian
spirit, letting not the left hand know what
the right hand doeth.
	One sweet day of celebration and re-
membrance is surely and justly theirs, the
seventeenth of March, Relief Society Day.
		ANNIE WELLS CANNON.


[Column 1]
		THE LEGISLATURE.
	The tenth session of the Utah legislature
has closed and for the fourth time since
statehood women took part with men in the
framing of the laws.
	There have been all together nine women
elected to the legislature of this state; more
than any other state has had and foremost
of all the states in such procedure.
	Very much has been published this past
winter in the different magazines about Col-
orado’s woman senator, claiming that Mrs.
Robinson of that state is the first woman in
the United States to hold that position; and
it has been with some surprise that we have
noted the press of Utah has not contra-
dicted the statement, when it is well known
that in 1897, Dr. Martha Hughes Cannon
was elected on the Democratic ticket to the
legislature, as state senator, and the same
year Mrs. LaBarthe was elected state repre-
sentative. The next session Mrs. Alice M.
Horne of Salt Lake City and Mrs. Anderson
of Ogden were elected representatives, while
two years later Mrs. Coulter of Ogden
graced the lower house with her presence.
After a lapse of five years women came once
more to the front in this connection and the
result was the election in 1912 of five women
on the state ticket, one presidential elector
and four state representatives. The elector,
Mrs. Margaret Zane Witcher; Dr. Jane W.
Skolfield, Mrs. Anna H. King, Mrs. Edythe
Ellerbeck Read and Mrs. Annie Wells Can-
non, representatives. In the beginning of
the session Mrs. Read, who had been ill all
winter, died without even qualifying, and
the women felt they had sustained not only
a severe loss in companionship but the loss
of an able and brilliant co-laborer in the
many lines of their work.
	That the legislature just closed has been
a memorable one for some splendid work is
recognized as a review of the measures
passed will testify.
	The interests of the state at large were
well considered, and while roads and
bridges, and cattle and sheep, and wild ani-
mals and irrigation projects, and revenue
and taxation, mines and smelters and dif-
ferent industries, fish and game, all received
much attention, there was also more thought
and time given to the educational interests
of the state and more splendid measures en-
acted along that line than any previous leg-
slature; also considerable attention was
given to social legislation, and while all the
measures along this line were not success-
ful, many of them were. Among these lat-
ter were the widowed mothers’ pension and

[Column 2]
joint guardianship, the minimum wage, the
woman deputy, the widows’ property tax
exemption bills, for all of which the women
worked most earnestly, also the new juve-
nile court law, which is a most excellent
measure. It creates a system of juvenile
court judges throughout the state, adding
greatly to the efficiency and support of an
already admirable work.
	That some worthy measures did not suc-
ceed is due not so much to a lack of earnest
work as perhaps a lack of knowledge on the
part of some of the legislators. New ideas
require, like other things, time to grow, but
when the seed is once sown the plant is
pretty sure to thrive and those things hoped
for this time will, we trust, be realized the
next.
	For the first time in the history of the
state the legislature adjourned in the allotted
sixty days, a precedent which it is hoped
other sessions will follow.
						A. W. C.


SUFFRAGISTS AND SUFFRAGETTES.
	It seems almost impossible for the public
to distinguish the difference between the
two organizations of women known as the
Suffrage organization and the Suffragette
society.
	The first, the suffragists, is that notable
body of women who have firmly but quietly
and decisively carried on an educational
campaign for the right of franchise; a
noble and notable army of women with live
leaders like Susan B. Anthony, Elizabeth
Cady Stanton, Lucretia Mott and others,
who have held congresses and conventions
in nearly all the large cities of the world;
who have proclaimed their gospel of equal
suffrage from the platform and through the
press but have never resorted to any dem-
onstration beyond the dignity of the most
refined, although at times they have them-
selves been boisterously assailed and most
unkindly and unjustly ridiculed and mis-
represented through the press.
	The second, the suffragettes, are a mili-
tant organization of women who endeavor
to gain the right of franchise through force
rather than education, through demand
rather than persuasion; claiming that what
is the right of men is equally the right of
women and needs no argument.
	That the end hoped for is the same need
not imply that either organization neces-
sarily justifies the other in its mode of pro-
cedure. Whatever the suffragettes may
think of the educational plan, it is quite
certain the suffragists do not approve of
the militants when they go so far as to
destroy property, threaten life, interfere
with the mail and incite to panic and riot.
The law is the law and when one breaks a
law, man or woman, then is he or she sub-
ject to the penalty of the offense. It is this
misunderstanding among the masses of the
people, this not being able to distinguish be-
tween the two, this thinking that if a woman
wants to vote she must necessarily be a
militant law-breaker and window smasher,
that has retarded and hurt the suffrage
cause more than anything else. In all prob-
ability it was this same misunderstanding
which caused the trouble in Washington
during the suffrage parade the day before
President Wilson’s inauguration. The lower
element of the city, the hoodlums and igno-
rant masses, assailed without resistance from


a likewise ignorant police force, a most rep-
resentative and intelligent body of AMerican
women and not only destroyed the effect of
a classical and beautiful procession but in-
jured their bodies and insulted their souls. 
	For months preparations were being
made for this procession. Letters had been
written to the prominent organizations of
every state in the Union. The designer’s
art had been called into service for unique
floats and telling costumes. Banners, flags
and pennants had been painted and em-
broidered. Horses, carriages and automo-
biles had been engaged, while bands and
drum corps had been making ready for some
time. An imposing and magnificent array
which might have told in symbol to the
people gathered at the capital from all over
the United States for the inaugural cere-
monies, something of the numerical and in-
tellectual strength of the suffrage cause,
had not that brutal, ignorant, seething mass
of humanity been allowed to break the ranks
and scatter and hurl the columns of march-
ing women into confusion. Such proceed-
ings are a shame to American manhood and
especially to a city government which can
offer no protection when protection most is
needed. It is to be hoped that the investiga-
tion into the inaction of the police depart-
ment will be thorough and the findings will
be adequate for such dereliction. The suf-
frage cause is advancing at any rate stead-
ily and surely, and no amount of brutal and
ignorant opposition can stop the progress of
so just and good a movement. 
					A. W. CANNON.


WOMAN ’ S EXPONENT.


THE RELIEF SOCIETY.
	(Copied from the Original Record.)
	ELIZA R. SNOW, SECRETARY. 
	By invitation of the President of the
Church, a number of sisters convened in
the Masonic Lodge Room on the 17th of
March, 1842. President Joseph Smith,
Elders John Taylor and Willard Richards
were present. 
	President Smith stated that the meeting
was called for the purpose of making more
complete the organization of the Church,
by organizing the women in the order of
the Priesthood. 
	John Taylor was called to the chair and
Willard Richards acted as secretary. Af-
ter singing and prayer a vote was taken
to know if all were satisfied with each sis-
ter present, and willing to acknowledge
them in good fellowship and admit them
to all the privileges of the institution about
to be organized. All being satisfied, the
names of those present were taken as fol-
lows:
	Emma Smith, Martha Knight, Elvira A. 
Cowles, Sarah M. Cleveland, Phebe Ann
Hawkes, Margaret A. Cook, Desdemona
Fulmer, Elizabeth Ann Whitney, Sarah M. 
Kimball, Elizabeth Jones, Leonora Taylor,
Eliza R. Snow, Sophia Packard, Bathsheba
W. Smith, Sophia Robinson, Philinda Her-
rick, Phebe M. Wheeler, Sophia R. Marks. 
	President Smith then explained some of
the duties that would devolve on members
of the Society, said they could provoke the
brethren to good works, look after the
needs of the poor, and perform charitable
acts. 
	Woman must assist in correcting the mor-
als, and strengthening the virtues of the
community. Said it is now in order for the
sisters to elect a President to preside over
the Society and let her choose two coun-
selors to assist in the duties of her office. 
He would ordain them and let them pre-
side just as the Presidency preside over
the Church, and if they needed his instruc-
tion, they could ask and he would be pleased
to give it from time to time. Let the Pres-
idency serve as a constitution and their de-
cisions become precedents for you to act
upon.
	If officers are wanted to carry out the
designs of the institution, let them be ap-
pointed and set apart as Teachers, Deacons,
etc., are among us. 
	Elizabeth A. Whitney moved and So-
phia Packard seconded the motion that Em-
ma Smith be elected President of the So-
ciety; the vote was put by the chairman
and pronounced unanimous. 
	The President Elect made choice of Sar-
ah M. Cleveland and Elizabeth Ann Whit-
ney as her counselors. 
	Eliza R. Snow was appointed secretary,
and Phebe M. Wheeler assistant secretary,
and Elvira A. Cowles, treasurer. 
	President Joseph Smith read the revela-
tion to Emma Smith, from the Book of
Doctrine and Covenants, and stated that
she had been ordained at the time the reve-


[Column 1]
lation was given to expound the Scriptures,
to teach, etc. He then read Scriptures to
show that an elect lady is one elected to
preside.
	By request of President Joseph Smith,
Apostle John Taylor ordained Sarah M.
Cleveland and Elizabeth Ann Whitney to be
counselors and assist President Emma
Smith in the duties of her office and share
in the blessings pertaining thereto.
	He then confirmed on Emma Smith her
former ordination, and blessed her to be a
mother in Israel, a pattern of virtue, and
to possess all the qualifications necessary
to enable her to preside with dignity and
give such instruction as may be requisite
in her calling as an Elect Lady.
	Elder Taylor vacated the chair and Em-
ma Smith and her counselors took the stand.
	A lengthy discussion followed on the
question, “What shall the Society be
called?” Mrs. Cleveland and Whitney ad-
vocated the appropriateness of the name Re-
lief Society. John Taylor and Joseph
Smith spoke in favor of the word Benevo-
lent. Emma Smith and E. R. Snow ar-
gued in favor of Mrs. Cleveland’s choice.
The gentlemen withdrew their objection and
a motion to adopt the name Relief Society
was unanimously carried.
	President Joseph Smith then said, “I now
declare this Society organized with Presi-
dent and counselors. All who shall here-
after be admitted must be free from cen-
sure and admitted by vote.” President Jo-
seph Smith laid down a five dollar gold
piece to commence a Relief Society fund,
and said, “What I do hereafter for char-
ity I shall do through this society.” Sarah
M. Cleveland gave 12 1/2  cents; Sarah M.
Kimball $1.00; Emma Smith, $1.00; Eliza-
beth Ann Whitney, 50 cents; Willard Rich-
ards, $1.00; John Taylor, $2.00.
	At the third meeting of the Society,
March 31st, President Joseph Smith said
the members of the Society must act in
concert, or nothing can be accomplished.
To move acording to the ancient order of
the Priesthood this must be a select So-
ciety, separate from the evils of the world.
	Sixth meeting, April 28th, said he was
present to speak upon the Priesthood; as
his instructions were intended only for
members of the Society, he wished those
not members to withdraw. After which
he called attention to the 12th chapter of
Corinthians, “Now concerning spiritual
gifts,” etc., said the passage which reads,
“No man can say that Jesus is the Lord
but by the Holy Ghost,” should be trans-
lated, “No man knows,” etc. Continued the
reading of the chapter, and gave instruc-
tions respecting the different offices and the
necessity of every individual acting in the
place allotted to him or her, and filling the
office to which they were appointed. 
	Spoke of the disposition of some to con-
sider the lower offices in the Church dis-
honorable and to look with jealous eyes on
the position of others; said it was the non-
sense of the human heart, to aspire to posi-
tions not appointed of God, that it was bet-
ter for individuals to magnify their respec-
tive callings and wait patiently till God
shall say to them, “Come up higher.” Said
it was talked by members of the Society
that some were not doing right in laying
hands on the sick. If they had common
sympathy, they would rejoice that the sick
could be healed. The time had not been
before when these things could be in their

[Column 2]
proper order. The Church is not now or-
ganized in its proper order and cannot be
until the temple is completed. He referred
to the commission given to the ancient
apostles, “Go ye into all the world,” no
matter who believes, these signs, such as
healing the sick, casting out devils, should
follow all that believe, whether male or fe-
male.
	If the sisters have faith to heal the sick,
let them do so, and let the good work roll
on. It is no sin for anybody to administer
to the sick that has faith, or if the sick
have faith to be healed by the administra-
tion.
	Reproved those that were disposed to
find fault; spoke at some length on the
difficulties he had had to surmount ever
since the organization of the Church, on ac-
count of aspiring men; said the same as-
piring disposition will be in this Society
and must be guarded against.
	As he had the opportunity he would in-
struct the Society how to act according to
the will of God. He did not know that he
should have many opportunities of teach-
ing them, they were going to be left to
themselves, they would not long have him
to instruct them, the Church would not
have his instruction long and the world
would not be troubled with him a great
while, according to his prayer God had ap-
pointed him elsewhere.
	The congregation was melted to tears.
	He exhorted the sisters to sustain with
their confidence, their faith and their
prayers, those whom God had appointed to
lead. Said the keys are about to be given
to you, that you may be able to detect any-
thing false, as readily as the Elders. If
members of the Society become corrupt, you
must deal with them; the sympathies of the
heads of the Church have induced them to
bear with those that are corrupt, in conse-
quence of which all become contaminated;
you must put down iniquity and by your
good example provoke the Elders to good
works.
	If you do right there is no danger of go-
ing too fast, resist evil and there is no dan-
ger, neither God, man, angels or devils can
condemn those that resist everything that is
evil. As well might the devil seek to de-
throne Jehovah as that soul that resists
everything evil. You are now placed in a
situation where you can act according to
those sympathies which God has planted in
your bosoms. If you live in accord with
your privileges, angels cannot be restrained
from being your associates. Women, if
they are pure and innocent, can come into
the presence of God. If we would come
into the presence of God we must be pure.
The devil has great power, his ways are de-
ceptive. Do not be contentious, but let the
weight of innocence be felt, which is more
mighty that a millstone hung about the
neck. Meekness, love, purity, these are the
things that should magnify us.
	Iniquity must be purged out, then the
vail will be rent, and the blessings of heaven
will flow down.
	After this instruction you will be more
responsible, it is an honor to save your-
selves, all are responsible for themselves.
	He then read the 13th chapter of Corin-
thians. “Though I speak with the tongues
of angels,” etc.; said do not be limited in
your views with regard to your neighbors’
virtues, do not think yourselves more right-

[Column 3]
eous than others. You must enlarge your
souls towards others, if you would do like
Jesus and carry your fellow creatures to
Abraham’s bosom. He had manifested
long-suffering and we must do the same.
Said, “Though we have the spirit of proph-
ecy,” etc. Though one should become
mighty and do great things, overturn
mountains, and then turn to do that which
is evil, all former deeds would not save
them, but they would go to destruction. As
you increase in innocence, virtue and good-
ness, let your hearts expand, let them be
enlarged towards others; you must be long
suffering and bear with the faults and er-
rors of mankind. How precious the souls
of men! The Society is to get instruction
through the order which God has estab-
lished, through the medium of those ap-
pointed to lead, and I now turn the key to
you in the name of God, and this Society
shall rejoice and knowledge and intelli-
gence shall flow down from this time; this
is the beginning of better days for you.
	When you go home never give a cross
word, but let kindness, charity and love
crown your works. Do not envy sinners,
exercise mercy towards them.
	Let your administrations be confined
mostly to those around you. As far as
knowledge is concerned it may extend to
all the world. But your administrations
should be confined to the circle of your
acquaintance, and more especially the mem-
bers of the Society. Those ordained to
lead the Society are authorized to appoint
officers as the circumstances shall require.
	If any have a matter to reveal, let it be
in your own tongue, do not indulge much
in the gift of tongues, or the devil will take
advantage.
	You may speak in tongues for your com-
fort. But I lay this down as a rule, that if
anything is taught by the gift of tongues,
it is not to be received for doctrine.
	President Smith then gave instruction
respecting women administering to the sick
by the laying on of hands, said it was ac-
cording to revelation, he never was placed
in similar circumstances, and never had
given the same instruction.
	At the ninth meeting May 26th, the
Prophet read the 14th chapter of Ezekiel
and said the Lord declared by that prophet
that the people should each one stand for
themselves and depend on no man or men;
in the state of corruption in the Jewish
church that righteous persons could only
deliver their own souls. Applied it to the
present condition of the Church of Latter-
day Staints. If the people depart from the
Lord they must fall, they were depending
on the Prophet, hence there was darkness
in their minds.
	He had two things to recommend to the
Society. Be guarded in your speech; no
organized society can exist without giving
heed to this caution. All organized bodies
have their peculiar evils, weaknesses and
difficulties, the object is to make those not
so good equal with the good, and ever hold
the keys of power which will influence to
virtue and goodness.
	When you chasten and reprove, never
mention it again, then you will be estab-
lished in power, virtue and holiness and
the wrath of God will be turned away. God
designs to save the people out of their sins.
Jesus said, “Ye shall do the works which
ye have seen me do;” these are the grand
key-words for the Society to act upon.


Tenth meeting was addressed by Bishop
N. K. Whitney, President Joseph Smith be-
ing present. 
	Eleventh meeting, June 11th. Presiden[-]
Joseph Smith. It grieves me that there is
no fuller fellowship, if one member suf-
fers all feel it; by union of feeling we ob-
tain power with God; Christ said He came
to call sinners to repentance, and to save
them. Christ was condemned by the right-
eous Jews because He took sinners into His
society, he took them upon the principle
that they repent. Nothing is so much cal-
culated to lead people to forsake sin as to
take them by the hand and watch over them
with tenderness. The nearer we get to our
Heavenly Father, the more we are disposed
to look with compassion on perishing souls. 
I say to this Society if you would have God
to have mercy on you, have mercy on each
other. How mild the Savior dealt with
Peter, saying, “When thou art converted
strengthen thy brethren.” At another time
He said to him, “Lovest thou me? Feed my
sheep.” The Society is not only to relieve
the poor but to save souls. 
	Sixteenth meeting, held in the Grove,
Aug. 31st, 1842. After relating recent diffi-
culties President Joseph Smith had encoun-
tered while being hunted by his enemies,
he said the Relief Society has taken an
active part in my welfare in petitioning the
Governor in my behalf. These measures
were necessary and your influence helped to
bring about my redemption from the hands
of my enemies. I have come here to bless
you. The Society has done well, their prin-
ciples are to practice holiness. God loves
you and your prayers shall avail much; do
not cease to let them ascend to God in my
behalf. The enemy will never get weary. 
I expect he will array everything against
me, expect a tremendous warfare. He that
will war the Christian warfare will have
Satan and all the powers of darkness con-
tinually arrayed against him. 
	A few things had been manifested to him
during his absence, respecting baptism for
the dead, which he would communicate next
 Sabbath if nothing should occur to pre-
vent it. He then offered a fervent prayer to
God in behalf of the Society. After the
transaction of some business in the adjust-
ment of which President Smith offered
counsel—he then said he had one more re-
mark to make respecting baptism for the
dead which must suffice for the present and
until he had the opportunity to present the
subject at a greater length. 
	All persons baptized for the dead must
have a recorder present, that he may be an
eye-witness to testify of it. It will be nec-
essary in the Great Council that these things
be testified of. It should be attended to
from this time. If there is any lack it may
be at the expense of our friends. 
	This was the last Relief Society meeting
that President Joseph Smith attended. 
	During the two years administration of
President Emma Smith and her associates,
visiting sisters were appointed to search
out the needy, to find work for the able-
bodied, to receive contributions and to re-
port conditions. 
	The labors of the Society were extend-
ed to the four wards of the city. 
	One thousand two hundred and seventy-
five names were enrolled as members. The
recorded receipts were $415.24.


[Column 1]
		THE LIVING WAGE. 
	The members of the vice investigation 
committee of Chicago came across so many
shocking and surprising facts connected
with the downfall of girls that they have
referred the matter to President Wilson, be-
lieving that only by national legislation can
existing conditions be rectified. There have
already been some splendid results from the
publicity of the investigations. The Na-
tional Harvester company has increased the
salaries of its women employees, and other
large institutions are taking steps to do the
same, while the states which have legisla-
tion pending connected with the “living
wage” subject are likely to pass some
measures of benefit to the working woman. 
	The Utah legislature enacted a minimum
wage measure which has received the gov-
ernor’s signature and will go into effect im-
mediately. To the credit of the state, be it
said that Utah is the first state to fix a scale
of wages for the working girl. The mini-
mum is low, it is true, and not what the
friends of the measure desired, but it is a
step in the right direction, and will raise the
standard and respect of the girl herself to
know she has a law to befriend and protect
her. 
	One other excellent measure enacted by
the Utah legislature connected with the
working girl, was the one which provided
for a woman deputy on the Commission of
Immigration, Labor and Statistics. It will
be the duty of this woman deputy to see
that the state laws are enforced in regard
to the employment of women and children
in the different industries in the state. When
one considers the large number of women
employed in the different mercantile estab-
lishments, the laundries, the hospitals, the

[Column 2]
biscuit and candy factories, box factories,
hotels and restaurants, not to mention
schools where about ninety per cent of the
teachers are women, one wonders that so
long a time should have passed in a suffrage
state without such an officer.
	With a good, competent woman holding
this position, the working girl will feel
greater security in her work; she will know
that there is some one with authority look-
ing after her interests. The industries need
not suffer in consequence of these measures,
in fact they ought to prosper, as all things
do, where a proper understanding exists be-
tween employer and employee, on a basis
of something like equality. It may not, per-
haps, be very well known that many estab-
lishments in Salt Lake City employ young
girls at the rate of fifty-three and a half
cents a day, and some even less, expecting
a good nine-hour day’s work. With these
two measures enforced, such a condition is
now impossible. 
	While probably there will always exist
evil and girls will fall from the path of
rectitude, yet with good laws on the statute
books of the state and good officers to en-
force the laws, there is protection for the
working girl and safer conditions around
her. The low wage and poor environment
may not be the greatest or only causes of
immorality, but they are undoubtedly im-
portant factors in driving girls to lives of
shame. 
	The fact that when a girl lives at home
she does not need such high wages as the
girl who comes from the country and has
to pay for her room and board as well as
clothes, is one argument the employer makes
that he always inquires if his apprentices
live at home and the wage in such cases
ranges from two dollars and a half to four
dollars a week. Now, does it stand to rea-
son that parents would send their girls into
the whirl and sorrow of the factory or the
shop if her wage was not to become a factor
in the support of the home? One does not
need to follow the investigations of “vice
committees” in Chicago or any of the big
cities; all one needs to do is to look around
at home in one’s own town or city and learn
something of the conditions of the labor-
ing class; that as soon as children are
old enough they are taken from school and
forced into the “madding crowd” in pursuit
of money to help pay perhaps the house
rent, or the interest on the mortgaged home,
to help clothe and feed those still younger
and the work worn mother. Many of these
working girls are underfed and life in youth
becomes care and burden when it should be
a joy and delight. When the nation or the
individual states have legislation to amelior-
ate these conditions then are we progressing
towards the plane of universal brotherhood;
when the manufacturer or the merchant can
realize that those whom he employs are en-
titled to a reasonable and sufficient wage,
in fact when he will concede that he does
not need ninety-five per cent of his profits
while his employees divide the remaining
five, then perhaps will begin the solving of
that perplexing national question—“Labor
and Capital.”
	The women of the country have agitated
legislation along these lines for some time
past and now when women are beginning to
have a hand and an influence in the making
of the laws, the fruit of their labors is being
realized. In four of the suffrage states,
Colorado, Utah, Washington, and Califor-

[Column 3]
nia, women sat in the legislature this win-
ter, and in all of these states some splendid
social legislation has been passed, while
committees of women have worked for sim-
ilar laws in states where suffrage does not
yet obtain. So rolls on the good work. 
						A. W. C.


WOMAN ’ S EXPONENT.


WOMAN ’ S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
		ST. PATRICK.
	“Toast America’s goldenrod, 
	Toast France’s lily fair.
	Toast bonny Scotland’s thistle,
	And England’s rose so rare.
	They all deserve our praises,
	And our blessings too, I ween,
	But give to me the shamrock
	From the little isle so green.”

	It would not be inappropriate if we gave
a few minutes thought to St. Patrick, as our
festival day by coincidence falls on the same
day as his. St. Patrick was a true saint of
God. A saint is a person endowed with a
sublime spirit of prayer, purity of heart,
and perfect charity and humility.
	Such people God chooses to act as his in-
struments on earth.
	During the close of the fourth century, in
a little town in Scotland, a boy was born
called Patrick. His parents were of a good
Roman family. He was tenderly nurtured
and reared in the Christian faith. He lived
on the farm with his parents until he was
sixteen years of age, when a band of
pirates visited that part of the country and
carried off a great many of the young peo-
ple, among them the boy Patrick. He was
taken into Ireland and forced to tend cattle
and swine. He went through many hard-
ships, such as danger and cold. He endured
these trials for six long years, and it was
during this time that his intense religious
nature was developed.
	He fasted and prayed day and night to
be able to stand these persecutions. He
made as many as one hundred prayers in
a night and the same during the day. One
night he had a dream in which it was made
known to him to return to Scotland. He at
once planned to escape, which he succeeded
in doing and made his way on foot two
hundred miles to the sea coast. When he
arrived there he found a ship ready to sail;
having no money he begged the captain to
be allowed to work his way, but the cap-
tain sternly refused. He turned to go back
with a sad heart, and prayed earnestly to
God to help him. He had not gone far when
he was suddenly called back by the sailors
who told him the captain had relented.
	During the voyage he preached a great
deal to the sailors and taught them to have
faith in God. When they reached land they
found themselves in a barren waste of coun- 
try and had to travel for many days on foot.
They were often without food and at one
time actually faced starvation. The captain
asked this boy preacher why he did not call
upon God to help them. He replied if they
would turn to the Lord in faith their prayers
would be answered. This they did, and that
very same day they came across a herd of
swine, and were never without food again.
	Patrick finally reached his home and was
received with great joy and gladness. After
a few years he had a vision in which God
told him he was to perform the great work
of converting the Irish. He immediately
began to prepare himself for this mission.
After studying hard for four years he
gained permission from the Pope to set
about this work. So he departed once more
for Ireland.
	He found the people to be a lot of bar-
barians worshiping idols; he worked un-
ceasingly among them day and night. He
traveled from one end of the island to the
other exposing himself to great danger. In
a short time his labors were rewarded and

[Column 2]
he baptized many, ordained clergymen and
established monasteries, and in time all of
the people were converted and Ireland be-
came a garden in the Church.
	St. Patrick performed many miracles,
such as healing the sick, restoring sight to
the blind, and it is said he even raised the
dead. He labored in this way for 40 years
and then he passed away.
	During the early part of his ministry he
was preaching to a barbarian chief, trying
to explain the Trinity. This the chief could
not understand, how it could be possible for
one to be in three. St. Patrick picked from
the green sod a shamrock and holding it up
bade him behold “one in three.” The chief
was so impressed with this illustration that
he at once was baptized.
	The shamrock became the national em-
blem of Ireland and is symbolized all over
the world where there are any Irish by “The
Wearing of the Green.”
	St. Patrick we know and esteem
	By wearing a little knot of green;
	But our hearts best wishes will always be 
	With our own Relief Society.
			WlNNIFRED C. ELLERBECK.


ANNIVERSARY DAY.

			Provo, April 3, 1913.
	The 17th of March anniversary exercises
of the Utah Stake Relief Society were held
in the stake tabernacle, President Martha
A. F. Keeler presiding.
	After the opening exercises President
Keeler delivered a greeting from President
Emmeline B. Wells; spoke on the past his-
tory of the Relief Society, and stated that
the program had been arranged to do honor
to Sisters Marilla Daniels, Percia Bunnell,
and Agnes Farrer, who have labored faith-
fully for many years in this organization.
	A vocal solo was rendered by W. D. 
Roberts.
	Sketches of the lives of the three guests
of honor were given by Mrs. Pearl Daniels
Thomas, Mrs. Melissa Bunnell Williams,
and Mrs. Zella Farrer Stubbs.
	Sisters Daniels, Bunnell and Farrer were
each presented with a post card album, and 
were showered with post cards bearing sen-
timents of the greatest esteem. The sisters
thus honored responded briefly, and related
many incidents of their experience in the
early days of Relief Society work. The 
musical exercises, in charge of stake Relief
Society choir director Mrs. Salt, were a
very pleasing feature of the program. 
Stake President Joseph B. Keeler added
a few words of appreciation of the labors
of Relief Society workers. After the bene-
diction refreshments were served.
			MARY E. ASHWORTH,
			Corresponding Secretary.

	MARILLA MILLER DANIELS.

	Read by Mrs. Pearl Daniels Thomas.
	Sister Marilla Miller Daniels was born
October 12, 1830, in Haddam, Middlesex
County, Conn. Her father Aaron Johnson
and her mother Zerviah Kelsey, joined the
Church in 1836, and in the following spring
left their home, associations and all ties of
kindred for the gospel sake, traveling over-
land through central New York to Kirtland,
Ohio; this being the first place appointed


for the gathering of the Saints, and here
their first Temple was built. The first time
she ever saw the Prophet Joseph Smith was
at a fast meeting held in the Kirtland Tem-
ple in 1837, when he addressed the Saints. 
One would have to see him in order to un-
derstand the glorious inspiration that over-
shadowed him. The impression that he
made upon her mind at that time has never
left her, although she is now in her 83rd
year. And many times after that she en-
joyed the privilege of hearing him speak
and has never doubted the divinity of his 
mission. Her family were quite intimate
with the Prophet Joseph, and his family, as
neighbors, living only across the street from
each other. In the early spring of 1838 her
father’s family arrived in Far West, Mo.,
but they were only permitted to remain
there until March of the following year, on
account of the exterminating order of Gov.
Boggs, for them to leave the state. In the 
spring of 1839 they again left their home
and moved to Commerce, Illinois, which
was afterward called Nauvoo. Here her
father built three houses and they remained
there seven years. She has a distinct recol-
lection of the martyrdom of the Prophet and
his brother Hyrum, and the terrible scenes
enacted at that time. At the age of four-
teen years she was teaching school in Nau-
voo to about thirty pupils, and our President
Joseph F. Smith, also others of Brother
Hyrum’s family, were among them. Here
she met and married William Miller, the
ceremony being performed in the Nauvoo
Temple. In the spring of 1849 she left for
Utah, her husband being captain of the com-
pany. They arrived in Salt Lake in Sep-
tember of the same year, remaining there
one year. Her husband William Miller
founded the town of Springville and they
in company with her father’s family settled
there, living there ten years. Her mother
and only brother died of cholera on the 
plains in 1850 and were buried by the road-
side. This was a terrible blow to her as
she was anxiously awaiting the arrival of 
her mother and other relatives. In 1860 her
husband was called to act as President of
Utah stake, which necessitated their mov-
ing to Provo, where she has since resided.
Her husband died Aug. 7, 1875. Some
years later she married James E. Daniels.
She has been identified with the Relief So-
ciety ever since its first organization in
Utah. She has led a very active life and 
devoted much time to public work, having 
been a teacher in the Theological class of 
the Sunday School for a period of nineteen
years; was president and also a teacher of
the Primary association in her ward; was
also first counselor to Mary John in the 
Utah Stake Relief Society for many years.
In September, 1892, she was appointed as
president of the World’s Fair commission
for Utah county, and worked most assidu-
ously to make a success of the World’s Fair
exhibit from Utah, traveling and organiz-
ing clubs throughout the county. For sev-
eral years she acted as vice president of the
Woman Suffrage Association of Utah
county, and was sent as a delegate to the
27th annual convention of the National
American Suffrage Association, held in At-
lanta, Ga. After the death of President
Mary John, Sister Daniels was honorably
released from the presidency of the Relief
Society and was appointed as an honorary
counselor and member of the stake board
which position she now holds.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
		PERCIA BUNNELL.

	Read by Mrs. Melissa Bunnell Williams.
	Sister Percia Grover Bunnell, daughter
of Thomas Grover and Caroline Nickerson,
was born December 27, 1841, in Nauvoo,
Ill. She lived there with her parents until
she was five years old, when they left for
the Rocky Mountains with the first company
of pioneers. While crossing the Mississippi
river February, 1846, in a ferry boat, one of 
her grandfather’s oxen jumped overboard.
In jumping around he knocked a board
from the bottom of the boat, which sank im-
mediately. Grandfather signalled another
boat which came to their rescue. They were 
all soaked through—bedding, provisions and 
everything in the wagon. Notwithstanding
this misfortune not one received any ill
effects. The journey was continued as far
as Pisgah, but after arriving there, on ac-
count of the few teams and heavily loaded
wagons, it was decided that the families be
divided and part sent back to Nauvoo.
Grandmother and her children were among
this number. The following September
they started again with mother’s grand-
parents, Grandmother Grover having a
young babe but twenty-four hours old. After
traveling two hundred miles sickness befell
them and great-grandfather and three sons
died, leaving grandmother and her mother
with five small children to care for. They
resumed their journey as far as Council
Bluffs, when they were again detained for
want of means. So grandmother stayed
there, taking in washing, sewing, nursing
the sick, or anything she could get to do.
They stayed there until 1850, when grand-
father sent means for them to come to the
valley. They arrived in Salt Lake City in 
September, 1850, having suffered many
hardships on their journey. They lived
there two years, grandmother teaching
school for support. In 1852 she moved to
Provo, where she has since lived. Mother
and father were married in 1856; they had
twelve children, four of whom are living.
Mother suffered all the hardships incident
to pioneer life. She attended the first Re-
lief Society organized in Provo, acted as
teacher in the Third ward seven years; also
was appointed president of the teachers in
Lake View ward. In 1878 was chosen pres-
ident of the Lake View Relief Society,
which position she held six years. In 1901
was chosen second counselor to Sister Mary
John and labored as such until Sister John's
death, when she was honorably released and 
chosen an an aid, where she still labors. She 
has always been ready to help the poor,
nurse the sick, and although she is 71 years
old, continues in this good work. 

		AGNES STRONG FARRER.

		Read by Zella Farrer Stubbs.
	Agnes Strong Farrer is the daughter of
John Strong and Agnes Miller. She was

[Column 2]
born October 3, 1837, in Kendal, Westmor-
land, England. In 1846 she was baptized a
member of the Church by her father John 
Strong, who was president of the branch.
	Grandma with her parents left England
and walked across the plains and landed in
Utah October 28, 1855. In 1856 they moved 
to Provo and in 1857 she married Roger
Farrer, and they have resided here ever
since. Grandma is the noble mother of nine
children, six of whom are living. She has
always been a Church worker in different
organizations; a teacher in the Sunday
School and counselor in the Primary for
fifteen years.
	The first Relief Society meeting she at-
tended in 1870 she was called upon to sing
and pray, and this was the beginning of her
interest in this organization. She was set
apart as teacher and labored in that capacity 
many years. She was appointed president
of the teachers, also an aid on the stake 
board. She was for the last seven years a
member of the Utah Stake Relief Society
Board, acting as chorister for that organiza-
tion.
	She has always been a regular attendant
of Relief Society gatherings and an ardent
worker therein. Her labor has been one of
love and pleasure, and her influence for
good is always felt wherever she goes. She
is a strong, most beautiful character and 
can see the good that is in each person.
	By her smile, how soon the stranger and
friend knows, it is not of herself she is
thinking, not of herself but of others—an
example for us all. She is a Saint of cheer-
ful spirit, Saint of days and nights. She 
shall sit in heavenly places and have her 
heart's desire.
	God bless our dear, sweet and soul-inspir-
ing mothers and grandmothers whose di-
vinely human interest and love have given
us our glorious nation.


NOTES AND NEWS.
	Mrs. Luella B. Johnston, of Sacramento,
Cal., is believed to be the first and only
woman yet chosen as a commissioner by
any city which has adopted the commission
form of government. Sacramento is proud
of her, and prominent citizens put up the
money to have in the California section of
the Washington parade two banners, one
reading, “Sacramento, the Heart of Cali-
fornia and the Capital of the State,” and
another, “Women of Sacramento voted
commission plan and elected only woman
commissioner in world. Under her admin-
stration as commissioner of education,
school, health and sanitary conditions have
become ideal.”—Exchange.

	“Something has really happened in Cali-
fornia. The men have learned that the .
women have waked up.”

THE LOVERS AND THE BOBOLINK.
Idly adown the green lane's shade,
Happy and silent two lovers strayed,
Perfume and sunshine over all
And 'round them the myriad songsters' call.
Moss and ferns crept down to the sedge
Where the wild rose hung o'er the water's edge,
And through the green aisles of the rushes rang
The chorus of joy the blackbirds sang.
Listen! From throbbing breasts of gold--
Mellowest music ever rolled--
Where, rocking on spray o'er the cascade's
	brink,
Wooing his love, trilled the bobolink!

Morning of life! and bright June day,
What could they do but onward stray,
And following close as some sweet dream
Hovered the brookside's minstrel's theme.
Then, as too near their footsteps pressed,
A lark sprang up from her wee home nest--
And, in a pool, (as in legend old),
They saw the secret their faces told.
Brighter along their homeward way
Did the flowers bloom and the sunlight play,
For their hearts were bound in that sweet link
Sung far and wide by the bobolink!
			AUGUSTA JOYCE CROCHERON.
March 6th, 1913.


AM I QUEEN IN MY HOME?
Do I reign as queen in my household?
	Oh! how much does that question mean?
Can I ever with all of my striving,
	Gain that worthy title "Queen?"

Am I queen in my home to my children?
	Are they subjects loyal and true?
Is my reign full of justice and honor?
	Are they anxious my bidding to do?

Do they come to me with their sorrows?
	Do they let me share their joys?
Is "Mama" always the confidant 
	Of my heaven-sent girls and boys?

Do they entwine their arms about me
	And tell me of their fears?
Do I know their disappointments?
	Do they let me dry their tears?

Do I patiently list to their prattle,
	And join in their games at play?
Do I study out their lessons,
	And direct their efforts each day? 

Do I seek to know their meaning?
	Do I strive to ever be wise.
And bring myself back to childhood,
	And see things through children's eyes?

Am I a queen to my husband,
	My Lord, my Priest, my King?
Do I help him fulfill his calling 
	Do I with him God's praises sing?


In the right do I sustain him?
	Do I comfort, cheer, and bless?
Do I stand side by side with him equally
	And try with him to progress?

With unswerving heart do I counsel,
	With sweet intuition from heaven?
Plead and advise if there’s error,
	Let him feel I’m a “queen” God given?

Oh, show me a queen that's ideal;
	Let me get inspiration from her;
Let me absorb her best aspirations,
	In her highest convictions concur.

Oh, God, give me strength and wisdom,
	Give me light, knowledge and power;
That I to my husband and children,
	May be truly a “queen” every hour.
					VINCY R. BARKER,
						7th Ward R. S.


[Column 1] 
Harriet Amelia Snow.
A SKETCH.
Harriet Amelia, daughter of Aaron and
Elizabeth T. Squires, born 13th September
1819, in Aurora, Geauga Co.Ohio. Her hon-
orable lineage is best established by refer-
ence to the fact that her parents were na-
tives of New England, one of her grand-
fathers served in the Revolutionary war,
and that her progenitors came to America
in the Mayflower.
At twenty-five years of age, she embraced
the Gospel, and gathered with the Church
at Nauvoo. In January, 1846, she was
married to Elder Lorenzo Snow, and in
the February following with her husband
and his three other wives, crossed the Mis-
sissippi and joined the encampment of the
Saints who had preceded them. Thence
to Salt Lake Valley her story is not dissimi-
lar to that of the majority of the Saints,
except in personal incident and circum-
stance.
A praiseworthy act of hers, during the
trip across the plains, deserves historical rec-

[Column 2] 
ord. A woman had died on the way, leav-
ing three little children—one of them a
helpless infant. Sister Snow was so
wrought upon by the pitiful condition of
the infant, that she weaned her own child
and nursed the motherless babe. By a stupid
blunder of her teamster, she was one night
left behind, alone, with two little children
on the prairie. Luckily for her, a wagon had
broken down and had been abandoned by
the company. Depositing the babes in the
wagon box, she made search, and found that
some flour and a handbell had been left in
the wreck, and with this scanty outfit she
set about making supper. She first took
the clapper out of the handbell, then stopped
up the hole where it had been fastened in.
This now served her for a water pitcher.
Filling it at a brook some distance away,
she wet up some of the flour, then, with
some matches that she had with her, started
a fire, and baked the flour—cakes, herself
and thirteen months old child making their
supper upon them. She then ensconced her-
self in the wagon with her babes, and slept
till early morning, when her husband found
her and complimented her highly for her
ingenuity and bravery. (Extract from
Women of Mormondom.)
After living a few years in Salt Lake
City, her husband was called to settle in
Box Elder, and preside over thaf part of
Zion. In 1857, one of her husband’s wives
died, leaving a little girl three years old,
and a baby boy. “Aunt Harriet” (as she was
lovingly called,) took the little girl into her
heart and home, another wife took the little
baby boy, but some years later, she also
took this little one, and they shared her
mother-love, together with her own children,
until they grew up and were married. She
was a blessing in the family, in times of
illness, they all depended on her greatly,
for she was skillful in understanding the
use of herbs and nursing the sick, and her
faith and childlike trust in the Heavenly
Father was a power, and inspired the broken
hearted with calmness, courage and faith.
In May 1868, the Relief Society was or-
ganized in Brigham City, she was called to
the position of 1st counselor to President
Leonora Snow Morley. After the death of
Sister Morley, in February, 1872, Aunt
Harriet became the President of the Relief
Society of Brigham City.
In those days, there was need of a great
deal of practical labor for the Relief Soci-
ety, poor emigrants came every year to
town, sometimes whole families came next
to empty-handed, needing everything. Aunt
Harriet used to say in Relief Society meet-
ing, “let your hearts and minds expand,
let us divide and share what we have with
the newcomers,” working days were kept
every month, where quilts and carpets were
made, ready to sell or give away according
to needs.
In the first years of the Society, Aunt
Harriet led the sisters in making many over-
alls, shirts and knitted socks, which were
sent to the men who worked on the Temple
in Salt Lake City, very little money was to


[Column 1] 
be found here then, but Aunt Harriet was
resourceful, many calls were made of the
Relief Society to assist, building up the
commonwealth. Aunt Harriet made chick-
en raising a study. When the call for the
first Tabernacle in Brigham City came, she
inspired the members of the Society to
gather Sunday eggs for a certain period of
time, and a fine sum in hard cash was rea-
lized, which was quite an item in those days
when money was so scarce.
	She was an inspiration in building up
home industry, she organized a class in
braiding straw, with Sister Carrie Smith as
teacher, fine hats with homemade trimming
were made in this department. Through
her management the Society helped build
the woollen factory and the Co-op Store;
when the days of prosperity arrived, the
Society owned a goodly amount of stock
in both institutions, and when poor families
were in need, the sisters would get the splen-
did homemade cloth, call extra working
days, cut and sew until the needy were com-
fortably provided for. She herself always
dressed in home made as much as was pos-
sible. Although money was so scarce,
Aunt Harriet managed through the Society
to emigrate a number of people—several
families from England and others. But
very little if any of this money ever was re-
turned. She was prompt in filling every
call, never shirking work. The following
incident will serve as an example. On July
24th, 1875, the United Order held a Grand
Fair. Every interested man and woman
was busy in assisting with preparations.
The committee called on the Relief Society
to make thirteen flags—the Stars and Stripes
—one for each department of Home Indus-
try, and one extra large for the Courthouse.
The request was made just a couple of days
before the 24th, but Aunt Harriet was equal
to the occasion. She gathered several sew-
ing machines to her house, requested a num-
ber of the best needle women to come and
work, and proudly they turned out the full
number just in time for the great celebra-
tion.
	In 1876, Sister Emmeline B. Wells met
with the Society in Brigham City, May 22nd
she came to explain the call from President
Brigham Young, that the Relief Societies
should gather and store wheat, this wonder-
ful call Aunt Harriet took up with her whole
soul, full of energy and determination she
led the sisters into the fields to glean wheat
like Ruth of old, this was the beginning of
this work, but little by little the members
gathered means for buying wheat. In the
beginning it was kept in the basement of
the Court House, this was found impracti-
cal, so Aunt Harriet gave one of her up-
stair rooms to keep wheat in, until a granary
could be built. She planned and worked
hard for a granary. She asked all children
to bring to her all the glass pieces they
could find, these she crushed herself, and
had it mixed into the mortar used in build-
ing the granary, this substantial cool rock
building was completed in 1880. This was
the first granary among the Relief Societies
in the Stake, but she had the joy to see a
number more before her death. She was
anxious and zealous in storing wheat.
	When the Relief Society was organized
into a stake capacity, March 13th, 1878,
she became President of the Relief Society
of Box Elder Stake.
	A few years before, however, President
Brigham Young called upon the Relief So-

[Column 2]
cieties to send a number of good women to
Salt Lake City, to study and qualify in ob-
stetrics, that we might have good midwives.
Aunt Harriet with the assistance of Presi-
dent Lorenzo Snow, organized several clas-
ses. These sisters were called and set apart
for this mission, by President Lorenzo Snow
and his counselors, and Aunt Harriet obliged
herself to see that they did not suffer for
anything needful, during their schooling.
She was a mother to these and raised means
to pay the tuition for those who did not
have the means on hand, she also bought
a number of books as a medical library for
their benefit in their work.
	When the call for raising silk came, she
took up the work in earnest, herself raising
cocoons. She called upon ward societies to
buy land and plant mulberry trees. For
some years this industry prospered, the three
wards in Brigham City also the Willard as-
sociation had splendid mulberry tree
groves. Aunt Harriet, although along in
years, would hitch up a horse and buggy and
drive to the groves for loads of leaves for
her silk worms.
	In 1884, June 11th and 12th a conjoint
Fair was held in the Court House, by the
Relief Society and Y. L. M. I. Association,
the Relief Society had a splendid exhibit
of sericulture, including seven silk dresses,
four silver grey, three changeable royal
purple, big skeins of beautiful reeled silk,
many cocoons, twilled silk cloth, crocheted
ladies mitts, bright colored netted neckties,
skeins of sewing silk, also the waste from
the silk worked together with wool, showing
what could be done in this useful industry.
	When the Deseret Hospital was erected,
July 17th 1882, Aunt Harriet raised liberal
contributions yearly, of good quilts, sacks of
clean clothes, boxes of canned fruit, and sent
down to help sustain it.
	How dignified, happy and proud she
looked, when telling us about the first call
towards the Logan Temple. We had felt
happy and proud to assist building the Tab-
ernacle, but to help with the Temple,—well
it was done with a spirit of joyful reverence.
	As Stake President Aunt Harriet had to
travel a great deal, Box Elder Stake was far
reaching, taking in Snowville, Park Valley,
Grouse Creek, Malad and into Cassia Co.,
Idaho, when Aunt Harriet, and Sister Eliza
R. Snow, in President Lorenzo Snow’s com-
pany, which included many of the prominent
brethren, had traveled through the Stake and
finished organizing, she found herself Presi-
dent of twenty-four associations. The wes-
tern trip took about three weeks in those
days, but she enjoyed her labor and brought
her wonderful influence to the different
wards, she was a tower of strength to her
co-laborers; she was noble and wise in coun-
seling and directing, and her strong spiritual
mindedness coupled with her sweet humili-
ty, made her sublime. The branch presi-
dents would look forward to her coming,
and if there were any sick sisters or chil-
dren in their midst, they would take her to
them, that she might bless and comfort them.
	The nearby settlements she would visit to-
gether with as many of her board as could
go, sometimes in one big three seated wagon,
sometimes another smaller buggy. These
trips were thoroughly enjoyed by all includ-
ed.
	Aunt Harriet was very conscientious, al-
ways, duty first, in fact, her husband’s trib-
ute that he paid her the last Relief Soci-
ety meeting he attended before leaving for

[Column 3] 
Palestine 1872, gives a plain index to her
character. He delivered a spirited farewell
sermon to the Society concluding with these
words: “I leave you now in the care of
Aunt Harriet, for I know, even if the Devil
stood in the door, she would push him aside
and go to perform her duty.”
	Aunt Harriet was a tall, large woman,
dignified in appearance, plain and frugal in
tastes and habits, modest, unassuming, in-
dustrious. Her very presence inspired re-
spect and confidence in old and young, she
always wore homemade clothing as far as
could be had, continually urging the sisters
to be self-sustaining and patronize home in-
dustry, not follow the fashions of the world.
The following incident may show her integ-
rity to principle even in small things. One
bright summer day she traveled with a num-
ber of her board to visit a near by settle-
ment. Aunt Harriet wore a new dress, per-
fectly plain, princess cut, but instead of the
old fashioned invisible pocket, her daughter
had put on a new mode, a placket pocket,
At the meeting the local president reported
her ward and spoke very forcibly on the
follies of fashion, among other things, she
said: “Brigham City lies on a hill and it
ought to be a light to the whole Stake.”
Naturally the Stake officers present won-
dered if any of them had offended in some
way, so returning home in their big three
seated wagon, being all women, Counselor
Mary the teamster, they began to criticize
each other’s dress like happy school
children determined to find the culprit; with
much merriment they accused each other,
until one declared it must be Aunt Harriet’s
new pocket, and there she sat, never saying
one word but her shoulders shaking with
suppressed laughter and her eyes twinkling
with amusement. (Let me state here, that
I have never heard Aunt Harriet laugh
loud, but always a silent laugh, so to
speak.)
	Next morning the same company went to
Bear River City, arriving at the president’s
home, removing their dust coats, helping
each other with little tidy-up touches, sud-
denly they all stood transfixed looking at
Aunt Harriet for lo! she had picked off
the modern pocket and inserted an old
fashioned one in its stead, blessed Saint as
she was, always telling us, that example
speaks louder than precept. She enjoyed
the company of the young, and attended the
Young Ladies’ and Primary meetings con-
stantly.
	In looking backward through the long
vista of years, it seems impossible to give
a perfect sketch of her many-sided labors,
spiritually and temporally, she firmly be-
lieved and carried out the instructions of
the Prophet Joseph, to the Relief Society,
that they should strive to save souls and
provoke the brethren to good works. The
many peculiar calls that came to our women
in her time, she always accepted and magni-
fied her calling, loyal and true to her peers.
Her intimate associates at headquarters
were Sisters Eliza R. Snow, Zina D. H.
Young, Emmeline B. Wells, Phoebe Wood-
ruff, M. Isabella Horne, Jane Richards,
these leaders in woman’s work often visited
and enjoyed the progress of home indus-
tries in Box Elder County.
	She was a true mother in Israel, always
willing and ready to go among the sick.
She was sent for a great deal, because
the sisters loved her and her administration
in the sacred ordinances because of her sub-


lime faith in the Gospel. She always
taught and inspired her sisters to live up
to every principle of the Gospel. Being
among the first to enter Celestial Marriage,
she often testified to the greatness of this
principle, saying, “I know this is a true prin-
ciple, or I should never have stayed by it,
it is a pure and sacred principle, calculated
to exalt and ennoble all who honor and live
it as revealed by the Prophet Joseph Smith.”
	She taught and urged the sisters to gath-
er genealogy and do Temple work, herself
setting a strong example. In 1889 her
health failed more or less, she became rather
feeble and her eyesight became quite dim,
but spiritually she was full of energy to
finish her genealogy. In October she went
to Salt Lake City, attended all the meetings
of the Relief Society also the General Con-
ference, then she traveled all alone down
to Manti and worked in the Temple till her
record was finished. A month later she re-
turned home unannounced, in the afternoon
she came and surprised us in our home,
bringing her lantern; she was in the habit of
doing this, when she would stay and spend
the evening, and we would take her home
at night. She told us all her experience
during the trip and her work, told how she
had now finished her own record, also had
taken some genealogy of one of her hus-
band’s wives, fearing that she would not
attend to it, Aunt Harriet had also this
finished. Under the recital she looked so
earnest and grave, and we sat gazing at her
with eyes brimfull of tears, with a feeling
of awe, our hearts full of love and tenderness
towards her, but the still small voice plainly
whispered to us “Aunt Harriet will soon
leave us, she has finished her work.”
	Next year, May 12th, 1890, she passed to
the great beyond.
	We thank Our Heavenly Father for hav-
ing known, lived and labored with our moth-
er, “Aunt Harriet.” 
				EMELIA D. MADSEN.





WOMAN ’ S EXPONENT.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
	AN AUDIENCE WITH THE POPE.
	Our letter to Cardinal Martennelli pro-
cured us our audience with His Holiness
Pope Pius X.
	On Nov. 25th 1909 at eleven o’clock in
the morning, we drove through the streets
of Rome in the brilliant sunshine, over the
beautiful arched bridge of St. Angelo, across
St. Peter’s Square to the entrance of the
Vatican.
	When we arrived the Swiss guard in their
picturesque costumes of yellow, blue, and
black stripes, all saluted.
	We wore the customary black dresses,
black veils, and no gloves and carried rosa-
ries which the Pope might bless for our
Catholic friends at home.
	We were conducted up the spacious stairs
past a line of more saluting Swiss guards
into a lofty waiting room where probably
ninety persons were assembled. The walls
of the room were hung with beautiful car-
dinal brocaded silk, there were silk curtains
at the door and windows, large paintings
on the wall, and at one end of the room
was a gold throne with the papal coat of
arms and a dove on the back and the figures
of St. Peter and St. Paul on the sides. The
canopy was of cardinal velvet.
	The men waiting here were in evening

[Column 2]
dress, although it was not yet noon. The
women were in black, while the young girls
were in white with white veils. The veils
are draped on top of the head and fall
over the shoulders with the ends fastened in
front, but do not cover the face. There were
a number of monks and nuns here too.
	After a time a chamberlain, in a gorgeous
costume of cardinal brocaded silk, and wear-
ing cardinal silk hose, black shoes with car-
dinal buckles, ushered us out in groups of
about thirty. We traversed interminable
distances it seemed, through a series of
broad and spacious apartments similar to
that which we had left. Finally we passed
through a smaller room where a number
of persons were standing evidently waiting
to receive a greeting and blessing from the
Pope. We were taken into the next room
and stood in a circle around the wall. It
was an exquisite room. The walls were hung
with cardinal moire silk, the carpet of car-
dinal velvet with the papal coat of arms in
the center and behind us was a large gold
throne. The windows were draped with
white silk curtains, long and softly sweeping
as transparent as lace.
	Two officers in full uniform with helmets
and swords kept guard. One lady had for-
gotten to remove her gloves so she was
asked to take them off. In a moment or so
another officer came in and inspected each of
us from top to toe to see that all was correct
and probably to see that there were no suspi-
cious characters present, for we heard that
at that time the Vatican feared anarchists.
	There was a little rustling movement in
the next room, the guard signaled us all to
kneel and the Pope, accompanied by an at-
tendant robed in purple, entered. His Holi-
ness was dressed entirely in white cloth, a
beautiful, fine, soft fabric, and he wore
hanging on his breast a magnificent emerald
cross. He came in in the simplest fashion,
walked around to each one and extended
his hand in blessing and that each might
kiss the ring of St. Peter which he wore.
After he had passed us, one woman, over
devout, I suppose, bowed to the floor to
kiss his feet, but he jumped back as though
very nervous and did not allow it. The at-
tendant and guards came up quickly, but
the Pope recovered himself, spoke to her and
placed his hand upon her head. After each
had received his greeting he arose, then
when the Pope had been all around the room
we all knelt again, and the Pope, standing in
the center, gave the blessing for all in melo-
dious Italian. He was a man of fine phy-
sique, with white hair, and a ruddy com-
plexion, large kindly Italian eyes and a
simple, gracious manner. His personality
spoke a kindly sympathy for human nature,
and one felt in his presence that a strenuous
life out among his people, helping them in
their struggle in the world, more strongly
appealed to him than an existence which re-
quired him to shut himself away in appar-
ent seclusion.
	After the Pope had finished the blessing
he went into the next room, when he re-
turned we formed a passage from one door
to the other, standing on each side, and as
he passed through, all knelt again, and the
audience, simple but impressive, was over.
	Then we walked back through all the
gorgeous cardinal rooms, past the saluting
Swiss guards and bowing footmen and left
the Vatican.
						G. B. W.


TO A DEAR YOUNG GIRL, EMMIE. 
Slowly with the changing seasons
	In loneliness the years had passed,
Heated summers, autumns somber,
	And the winter ’  s chilling blast;
Dear ones sleeping past earth ’ s waking,
	Dear ones toiling far away;
And my heart still listening, waiting,
	For their coming, day by day. 

Far away the salt lake glistened;
	Fair days, when its waves were blue;
But when white, rude storms descended
	And isolation sterner grew. 
Mountain ranges, gray clad stretches
	Where no streams ran, nor green trees
Broke the monotone of landscape
	With the wild birds ’  melodies. 

Thus the years passed, pioneering,
	Hoping time and toil would bring
Round our dwelling bloom and beauty,
	And the valley yet would ring
With the sounds of busy labor,
	Shine with streams from depths below,
And the desert gray yield harvests,
	As foretold so long ago. 

So the heart fed on its mem ’ ries
	Of dear friends and days gone by;
Ah, how many have passed from us,
	Happier now than you or I. 
Then, at last, almost a stranger,
	Came I to old haunts of mine;
Scenes may change, but true selves ever
	Changeless keep their countersign. 

“Sister Crocheron, you ’ re remembered,”
	 ’ Twas a girl ’ s sweet voice that spoke;
Ah, the long years ’  shadows vanished
	And glad feelings, long hushed, woke. 
Snow was still upon the mountains,
	But the spring, with flower and bird,
Filled my being with its joyance
	As her welcoming I heard. 

Grace and sweetness were thy dower,
	Never may their beauties fade;
Ne ’ er across thy happy pathway,
	Shadows cold, or thorns be laid. 
May the sunshine and the music
	Of thy face and voice endure;
As thou gav ’ st, to thee be given,
	Heart gifts true and sweet and pure. 
			AUGUSTA JOYCE CROCHERON. 
Salt Lake City, Nov. 19, 1912.


BOOK REVIEW.
	We have received from Dillon, Montana,
a new book entitled “Poetic Gems to the
World I Love,” by Mrs. Louise Coulson.
The author’s picture is the frontispiece. The
collection is somewhat fragmentary, con-
taining poems that breathe sweetness and
purity and touch a sympathetic chord in
the hearts of those whose minds tend to the
spiritual side of life, thus bettering human-
ity and the home.
	Mrs. Coulson resided here in Utah for
some years, and became well acquainted
with our people. She both spoke and 
wrote in their behalf.
	We trust this little volume will prove a
blessing to its readers, and bring success to
its author.

	Idaho women sent their suffrage banner
to New York for the parade.  On one [--]
of it is the design of the State s[--] which
was executed in 1890 by M[--] J. G. Green.
This is said to be the only State seal ever
designed by a woman. The pin of the State
Federation is also formed from the seal.
