SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH,	-	MAY, 1913


MOTHER’S DAY IN MAY TIME.
	It is a beautiful season to have chosen in
which to keep a festive occasion, in remem-
brance of the mothers through whom the
earth has been peopled—the mothers, who
knowing the suffering and the responsibility
have accepted it all to perpetuate the human
race—mothers of prophets, seers and reve-
lators; mothers of doctors, lawyers, minis-
ters; mothers of doctors, lawyers, minis-
ters; mothers of kings and princes; mothers
of great men in all the walks of life. We
only read of one man who had no earthly
mother, and he was the first man Adam.
He was alone in the new world; and since
it was not good for him to be alone, he was
given an Eve to be his companion and help-
meet. She was the mother of the race that
has peopled the world.
	There is no joy equal to that of the moth-
er when the babe is born—a new life, an
immortal soul that will exist forever, a part
of her own life! Would it were possible
that all the women in the world could realize
the importance of this great blessing and
could comprehend the vastness and magni-
tude of motherhood—there would be more
rejoicing and less unhappiness.
	Some of the most vivid word-
pictures of the supreme joy of motherhood
are drawn in connection with the birth of
Isaac and of Samuel; and still more intensi-
fied in the royal birth of Jesus. Those who
read these wonderful passages in the Scrip-
tures can scarcely realize all that these births
meant to the mothers, who were really and
truly in direct communication with divine
messengers; we marvel at these manifesta-
tions given to women of ancient times and
while we can hardly understand it all, the
simplicity of the narration appeals to us with
the utmost sense of its sublimity.
	In the case of Sarah, we wonder how
she could laugh when the Lord spoke with
her. To us who have such reverence for
even a prophet or an apostle, it seems in-
credible that one should make light of con-
versation with the Lord.
	The month of May is typical of all the
tenderest and most beautiful expressions.
It is the month of delicate flowers, bright-
colored birds, and budding and blossoming
fruits. It seems indeed fitting that Mothers
day should be observed in May, for it is
also the month in which the graves of our
country’s heroes, and of loved ones gone be-
fore, should be decorated with choicest flow-
ers by the hands of women who love the


cause for which they laid down their lives;
and so we say on each May day: O happy
mothers to have borne illustrious sons who
fought in freedom’s cause and whose graves
are dear to every mother who loves her own.
	As we look backward, we can find many
wonderful mothers of men and women. In 
the nineteenth century, there was Lucy
Mack Smith, mother of the great Prophet
of this dispensation, and of his brother the
Patriarch. If we could but look into her
eyes and hear her relate the story of her
life, we would certainly class her with the 
greatest mothers who have been permitted
to bring forth men who have stood for the 
highest ideals of honor and spiritual up-
lifting that have ever been revealed, since
the world began.


EDITORIAL NOTE.
	Interesting programs were rendered
March 17 by nearly all the Relief Societies
in the Church, so many indeed it would be
impossible to publish them all; but it is with
much pleasure that we read of the renewed
interest in Relief Society work these occa-
sions arouse and we trust the Latter-day
Saint women will always appreciate their
membership in this splendid organization.
There are reports from Payette, Cannon,
Liberty, Roy wards and many others.


[Column 1]
GENERAL RELIEF SOCIETY CONFER-
			ENCE. 
	The Conference of the General Relief So-
ciety of the Church was held in the Assem-
bly Hall, Salt Lake City, Utah, April 2nd
and 3rd, 1913, commencing Wednesday,
April 2nd, at 10 a. m., President Emmeline
B. Wells presiding. 
	The morning session opened with singing
“We Thank Thee, O God, for a Prophet.”
Prayer by Miss Sarah M. McLelland. Sing-
ing, “Softly Beams the Sacred Dawning.”
	President Emmeline B. Wells extended a
loving and tender greeting to those assem-
bled. She regretted the fact that the weather
was so stormy, but felt that this would not
mar the good effect of the conference, if we
all have sunshine in our hearts. She re-
gretted the fact that some have departed
from us since our last meeting, but felt no
sorrow for them; the only real sorrow we
should have is for those who go into outer
darkness. President Wells expressed the
hope that all the members of the Relief So-
ciety love, uphold, and sustain each other,
and that all are united in this great work of
love. 
	The work of the Lord is progressing. It
is a source of joy to know that another
temple is to be built, and this in Canada.
The growth in this direction will go on and
on, so that the salvation for the dead may be
more readily accomplished. 
	Mrs. Sarah P. Stohl of Box Elder Stake
responded to the President’s greeting. She
expressed joy and satisfaction in being per-
mitted once more to see and hear our be-
loved president. Felt that all should appre-
ciate the fact that our president knew per-
sonally the Prophet Joseph Smith, and knew
of his great desires to aid and uplift the
women of our people. She was pleased to
report Box Elder Stake in good condition. 
Weekly meetings have been inaugurated,
also department work, the older sisters

[Column 2]
meeting in one class and the young mothers
in another, each department taking up work
particularly fitting to its various needs. The
evolution of women has been discussed and
time has been spent on genealogical work. 
	Sister Verona B. Redd, secretary of Tay-
lor Stake, reported that much good is being
accomplished in this far away stake. The
people are intensely interested in their work,
and are steadily progressing. 
	She was followed by Mrs. Sarah McLel-
land, a member of the Board of Bannock
Stake. There are fourteen societies in this
stake, scattered over a large area of coun-
try. It is necessary to travel seventy-five
miles to reach one ward. The members are
all united, and the work is steadily growing. 
	Sister Alice Boomer represented Bingham
Stake, where there are ten societies and a
membership of four hundred. The class
work is especially good, being conducted by
competent class leaders. 
	Bro. George Pyper sang a tenor solo, “My
Native Land.”
	Benson Stake was reported by Mrs. Re-
becca Rawlins. The distinctive feature of
her report was that in this stake two sisters
have been appointed especially to call on all
the aged and sick and infirm, for the purpose
of giving them comfort and cheer.
	Mrs. Eleanor Squires reported Liberty
Stake, where the organization is new. In
this stake preparation meetings for officers
have been instituted and are very success-
ful. Among other things “Home Econom-
ics” has been taken up, and actual demon-
strations in cooking given. 
	By special request Bro. George Pyper
gave another solo, “O, My Father.” The
President expressed gratitude for this cour-
tesy, and asked that the audience give a ris-
ing vote of thanks to Bro. Pyper. 
	Mrs. Annie Wells Cannon, President of
Pioneer Stake, and a member of the State
Legislature recently adjourned, then deliv-
ered an address, “Women in Social Ser-
vice.”
	Mrs. Cannon stated that outside of her
family and the Gospel nothing is so dear to
her as the Relief Society work, with all that
it has accomplished in its various fields. The
great opening for women in the world had
its beginning with the Latter-day Saints in
the organization of this powerful society,
which was the opening wedge. 
	The status of women today is very differ-
ent from what it was fifty years ago, great
changes have taken place and much progress
made.
	Woman has always had one opportunity
—the opportunity to work; this one thing
has never been denied her. Man has never
considered that she was out of her sphere
when she toiled in the fields and factories;
he has never criticized her for leaving her
home for any kind of drudgery; but when
she began to enter the professional field and
when she began to compete with men in the
great work of the world, she was reminded
that she was going out of her own sphere
and was neglecting her home. 
	After the key was turned for woman by
the Prophet Joseph Smith she began to look
around and to think; she felt that if she were
not out of her sphere in the working world,
she would not be out of her sphere in public
life; so she turned her eyes to education and
to general development, and little by little,
step by step, she has gained a prominent
place in the world’s work. 
	Last year much good was done in Amer-


[Column 1]
ica by the women, not only in suffrage
states, but in other states as well, and legis-
lative work has been greatly influenced by
them indirectly by the intelligent interest
they have manifested in public questions;
and, as this interest has grown, women have
had less time for novel reading and frivolous
amusements.
	There have been women in our State leg-
islature from time to time, and in the session
just closed we had three women in the
House of Representatives, all deeply inter-
ested in such legislation as would help to
uplift and benefit humanity. Some of the
social legislation worked for was secured,
and while some failed, it was only the be-
ginning of a campaign for better things
which will be ultimately successful.
	When it was recommended that the Juve-
nile Court be done away with because it
was only partially effective, the women rose
to the occasion; they were on the alert and
through their efforts the subject was recon-
sidered; and as the result the State of Utah
has one of the best Juvenile Court laws in
the world today.
	Another measure for which we are all
pleased is the minimum wage for women.
Utah is the first state to establish this law.
The least wage that can now be paid a girl
apprentice over sixteen, is seventy-five cents
a day, or $4.50 per week. Much was said
against this law by paid lobbyists, and it was
predicted that it would kill home industry.
This same prediction was made two years
ago with regard to the nine-hour law, but
no evil result has so far been seen. The
State Vice Commission in Chicago has been
discussing this same question and trying to
sift it to the bottom. They have found that
women do not receive a living wage and
girls sell their souls to live. They feel that
the question is beyond them and have ap-
pealed to President Wilson to call a con-
gress of governors to settle the question.
All thinking people may know that some-
thing is materially wrong when 95% of the
profits in a manufacturing business goes to
the manufacturer himself and only 5% goes
to the workers—the people who make the
business and put their whole lives into the
work.
	It is a great satisfaction to know that a
woman deputy is to be appointed in the State
department of Immigration, Labor and Sta-
tistics, and this seems highly appropriate
since 99% of the employees in the state,
aside from mining, smelting, and railroad-
ing, are women.
	We are extremely proud of the widowed
mother’s pension law, and proud of the men
and women who helped to bring it about.
It is much better, too, for the state to help
the mother care for her little ones and allow
her to train them when young than to have
in later years perhaps to support them in
penal institutions.
	Equal guardianship of children is another
good law accomplished through the efforts
of the women.
	Several good measures introduced by the
women failed, and among them the bill pro-
viding for the establishment of a girls’ dor-
mitory at the State University. This is the
first thing the women of the State in a
united body have asked for, and because of
the great need of such a home for outside
girls it is a matter of keen regret that the
measure failed.
	In the face of all these things, we can see
that women have a sphere beyond the home,

[Column 2]
and it is to be hoped that in the future the
women of the Relief Society will interest
themselves in all questions of social legisla-
tion and avail themselves of the opportuni-
ties they have to help bring about important
and needed reforms.
	Announcements were made and the ses-
sion closed with singing, “O Ye Mountains
High,” and prayer by Mrs. Sarah Jenne
Cannon.
		AFTERNOON SESSION.
	At 2 p. m. the conference was resumed.
The congregation sang “Hail to the Bright-
ness of Zion’s Glad Morning.”
	Prayer, Dr. Romania B. Penrose. Sing-
ing, “Earth with Her Ten Thousand
Flowers.”
	President Wells announced that the after-
noon session would be devoted to public
health problems. The speakers are all spe-
cialists in their lines and will no doubt give
very valuable information and instruction.
	Mrs. Alice M. Horne introduced the
speakers and their subjects.
	The first speaker was Dr. S. H. Paul,
Health Commissioner of Salt Lake City,
who took for his theme, “Saving the Babies
and the Value of Milk Depots.”
	Dr. Paul said he was especially pleased to
be able to talk to such a large audience, be-
cause to a great extent the efficiency of the
health department depended upon the un-
derstanding and co-operation of the public.
Many things the health authorities had at-
tempted, he said, had failed partially at first
because the reasons and necessity for certain
rules and regulations were not understood.
In illustrating this, Dr. Paul referred to con-
ditions of the city’s milk supply, which be-
fore the enactment and enforcement of the
present regulations were deplorable. At
first, he said, there was decided opposition
from milk producers, but more recently,
through co-operation of producers and dis-
tributors, Salt Lake’s milk supply compared
favorably with that of other cities.
	Dr. Paul spoke of the concerted attempts
of other cities to lower the rate of infant
mortality. In some cities he said that one
infant in five died before reaching one year
of age, and although conditions in Salt Lake
were vastly better than the average, at least
75% of the deaths of infants were from
ailments preventable through proper feeding,
and a large proportion were from milk unfit
before it reached the home. Dr. Paul said
that it was the purpose of the health depart-
ment to establish a milk depot where proper
milk could be furnished for infants, and to
engage trained nurses to instruct mothers
in the care and preparation of food for in-
fants.
	In providing proper milk for infants, he
said, the first requirement was to get good
milk of proper chemical composition, and
next to see that it was kept pure after it
was delivered to the home.
	Mrs. Emma Ramsey Morris gave a vocal
solo, “Fear Not Ye, O Israel,” and by spe-
cial request sang “Come, Come Ye Saints.”
	Prof. Jakob Bolin of the school of physical
education of the University of Utah, then
gave an entertaining and instructive talk on
the necessity for proper posture, explaining
the injurious effects of improper carriage
and describing exercises by which improper
posture may be corrected.
	He asserted that many women live in a
state of constant invalidism because of care-
lessness in this respect.
	Dr. W. Brown Ewing, nerve specialist,
was the next speaker. His address in full
appears in this paper.
	At the close of each lecture questions
were asked from the body of the house and
were answered by the speakers respectively.
	Congregation sang, “Come, Come Ye
Saints.”
	Closing prayer, Mrs. Emma A. Empey.
			(To be continued.)


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


THE CHILD—BORN AND UNBORN.
By W. Brown Ewing, read at General Re- 
		lief Society Conference.
	When a member of your committee asked
for this address I confess I was at a loss to
know just what ground to cover and just
what would be the most useful.
	As my work has been, for so many years,
entirely along mental and nervous lines I
naturally take up that phase of the subject. 
	It has often been said that “the hand that
rocks the cradle rules the world.” The
proper way to train a child is to begin with
its grandparents. The problems of caring 
for the fit and the unfit are entirely separate
and distinct. The education of children is a
vast problem that has not been fully worked
out.
	Environment, good or bad, has a wonder-
ful influence on the future mental welfare
of the child. But no surroundings however
good they may be, will compensate, in any
adequate degree, for a bad heredity and a
vitiated nerve cell formation. He who dis-
regards nature’s laws will surely pay the
penalty—sooner or later. Like will produce
like, just as surely as night follows day, in
the palace of the king or the hovel of the
pauper.
	If we remember that genius is essentially
abnormal, and direct the teaching along gen-
eral lines rather than toward fostering any
special bent, we will surely more nearly
reach the desired goal. Yet how often we
find the opposite course persisted in and the
precocious child encouraged along the one
line of his peculiar aptitude—a one-sided
brain development—and the abnormal ten-
dency strengthened rather than retarded. No
amount of cultivation will give good growth
when the nerve cells are few and ill-nour-
ished; but careful culture can do much
when there are those with strong inherent
impulses toward development. On neurol-
ogical grounds, therefore, nurture is to be
considered of much less importance than
nature, and in that sense the capacities that
we most admire in person’s worthy of re-
mark are certainly inborn rather than made
(Donaldson).
	To be efficacious, physical and mental
training should go hand in hand, so
that the mentality may not be ham-
pered by a weak body. Would you
wish a child to become an accomplished
musician or a lightning calculator and
yet need a guardian in the ordinary 
affairs of life? From such material are the
world’s feeble-minded and criminal classes
recruited.
	Every child is a separate and distinct
problem. The treatment that will strengthen
one nervous system may utterly ruin an-
other; and only by careful study and obser-
vation can you decide what course to pur-
sue. Children like or dislike intuitively and
their confidence may be secured or lost in a
moment’s time. Their confidence may be


[Column 1]
secured by a kind word or thoughtful act,
or lost by a careless word or deed. The
child has not yet been born whose love and
affection was gained by falsehood or deceit.
Study the child, even more assiduously than
the “alfalfa weevil,” for really the subject is
more worthy and more important. A “sane
mind is a sound body” is more important
than monetary consideration, for in the end
the difference may be the sorrows that ac-
company the little white casket and the beau-
tiful flowers. It costs no more to “raise” a
man capable of living for eighty years than
it does to “grow” one who has not the capac-
ity of living to be forty years old (Fisher.)
	To begin a child’s training with its grand-
parents simply means that children sprung
from unhealthy and abnormal parents will be
abnormal, and that they will start the battle
of life under a handicap. The strenuous-
ness of this life is hard enough on the here-
ditarily strong; what must it be on the one
who from the first is below par? The vast
experience that culminated in the Mendelian
theory enables us to predict, almost with
certainty, the relative fitness or unfitness of
the progeny of a given marriage. The re-
sults of an analysis of pedigrees of insane
subjects, and rules of theoretical expectation
as propounded by Mendel, and known as
the Mendelian laws, are as follows—
	1—Both parents being neuropathic, all
children will be neuropathic.
	2—One parent normal, but with neuropa-
thic taint from one parent, and the other par-
ent being neuropathic, half the children will
be neuropathic and half will be normal but
capable of transmitting the neuropathic
make-up to their progeny.
	3—One parent being normal and of pure
normal ancestry and the other parent being
neuropathic, all of the children will be normal
but capable of transmitting the neuropathic
make-up to their progeny.
	4—Both parents being normal, but each
with the neuropathic taint from one parent,
one-fourth of the children will be normal
and not capable of transmitting the neuropa-
thic make-up to their progeny, one-half
will be normal but capable of transmitting
the neuropathic make-up, and the remaining
one-fourth will be neuropathic.
	5—Both parents being normal, one of pure
normal ancestry and the other with a
neuropathic taint from one parent, all the
children will be normal, half of them will be
capable and half incapable of transmitting
the neuropathic make-up to their progeny.
	6—Both parents being normal and of pure
normal ancestry, all the children will be nor-
mal and not capable of transmitting the
neuropathic make-up in their progeny.
	What better inheritance could you wish
from your parents than a pure normal hered-
ity without taint of any kind? Can lands
and houses, and the cattle on a thousand
hills compensate for it? True it is that oc-
casionally a defective is born to normal par-
ents, but the relative infrequency of such an
occurrence only emphasizes the law of hered-
ity. The child born to a heritage of unrest
may not “outgrow it” regardless of the fa-
ble to the contrary so often quoted. He may
be trained out of it but he will “outgrow”
very few things but his cradle and
his clothes.
	The problem of caring for the unfit is
becoming more and more grave with each
succeeding year. When we must face it
squarely is only a problem in procrastina-
tion. We know that the vast majority of

[Column 2]
degenerates are the children of parents who
were insane, feeble-minded, epileptic, alco-
holic, or too closely related by blood. If this
statement holds good, it stands to reason that
the more profound the parental taint the
worse, the degeneracy. Shall our defective
population be allowed to increase without
restraint? Shall we continue to procrasti-
nate? Shall children be born who have very
little chance of life and almost none of real
usefulness? It seems only too true that “the
forests are protected, the hogs inspected and
the children neglected.”
	In your capacity as advisers in case of
sickness you can assist many whose oppor-
tunities have been limited or those over
whom fortune has spread a cloud that seems
to have no silver lining.
	If you could at the same time discourage
the marriage of the unfit—remembering that
only from healthy parents can healthy off-
spring result—you will accomplish a work
that future generations will remember with
gratitude. I know full well that very many
people select their life-mate with much less
care than would be bestowed on a new suit
or hat. It will be said that I would take
from love all the poetry—perhaps—yet I
would like to substitute good common sense
for poetry. The so-called Indiana Child
Creed that every child has the inalienable
right to be born free from disease, free from
deformity, and with pure blood; every child
has the inalienable right to be loved; to
have its individuality respected; to be
trained wisely in mind, body and soul: to be
protected from disease, from evil influences
and evil persons, and to have a fair chance
in life, holds good in this and every state.
We say that all rules are broken in case of
sickness, and the comforts of the well must
give way to the sick, yet I have known the
expectant mother to receive less considera-
tion and care than is bestowed on the lower
animals. Is it ignorance? Is it thought-
lessness? Or worse—is it a question of dol-
lars and cents? If she requires intelligent
care after a new life is ushered into the
world, she certainly does before. In the
present generation women underclad, under-
fed, overworked, cannot bear infants en-
dowed with an organism fit to stay; factory
children, with no light, no air, no resistance,
grow up, if at all, to ages in which they are
permitted to procreate their kind, only poor-
er (Jacob). Thus children are starved or de-
fectives before they are born, infected with
hereditary diseases, and destined to be born
only to perish.
	In the education of the mother, in the care
of herself and her baby, we have the strong-
est weapon for fighting infant mortality. The
prevention of infant mortality forces the
problem of tuberculosis into second place.
The United States government report for
1908 gives the mortality from every form of
tuberculosis, in the registration area, as 78,-
289: whereas the number of infant deaths
under one year of age total 136,432!
	A baby comes into the world with less
chance to live one week than an old man
of ninety, and less chance to live a year
than one of eighty. Is it not true that there
are too many short coffins sold in the United
States? More than 1000 infants, under one
year of age, die annually in this state. Dr.
Hope of Liverpool reports that the deaths
among children under three months of age
either wholly or partially fed on artificial
foods are fifteen times as great as they are
among an equal number of infants fed on

[Column 3]
breast-milk. Ten bottle-fed babies die to
one that is breast-fed. Partially from statis-
tics and partially from estimates 1,500,000
persons die annually in the United States,
630,000 of these from preventable diseases.
	The fundamental causes of infant mor-
tality are mainly the result of three condi-
tions—poverty, ignorance, and neglect.
	The head of the United States government
Children’s Bureau (Miss Julia Lathrop) has
this to say regarding infant mortality:
	“Most of us are very much shocked to
find that in the ten years from 1910 back to
1900, there came into the world at least two
and one half million children who died be-
fore they were one year old, slipping into
life and out again, unknown and unnum-
bered in any public way.”
	And yet is there not something worse than
infant mortality — disgraceful and waste-
ful as it is? The moral degenerates that fill
our prisons, the insane that fill our asylums,
all have to be reckoned with. If improper
marriages, vicious living, ignorance and pov-
erty pad the death roll, do they not also
leave us a certain class of human beings
characterized throughout life by certain pe-
culiarities, eccentricities, originality in some
useless way, oddities of manner, speech or
dress, non-conformity to the rules that gov-
ern the great body of mankind, these things
not amounting to true insanity, and yet being
usually by heredity closely allied to it, or by
evolution ending in it at last? No one has
lived long in the world without meeting
many examples. Some of these examples
are strikingly original, a genius in some
lines of work or thought, yet in the ordinary
affairs of life wanting in those traits that
bring success, and capable of transmitting
to posterity an unstable mentality and a
vitiated heredity. The poor you have al-
ways with you, the mentally defective like-
wise; in the one case a distinctive hereditary
taint as a causative factor and at the same
time preventable, in the other a misfortune.
You can neither legislate nor educate pover-
ty from the world, but you can educate
hereditary mental defectives out of exist- 
ence.
	In your labors of love among the poorer
classes there is a vast field open to you, a
field that has not received the attention it
deserves, a field that by its newness begets
opposition in the ignorant, and yet a field of
work that will amply pay for all your time
and labor, and the result will be—what?—
Better children born and unborn.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


SOCIETY OF THE UTAH HAND-CART
			PIONEERS.
	Three years ago today, April 14, 1910, a
little company of Hand-cart Pioneers and
daughters met at the home of Sister Hannah
Lapish in response to an invitation published
by her in the daily papers that all Hand-cart
Pioneers and daughters meet at her home.
Quite a number responded to the invitation,
and upon arriving were told by Sister Lap-
ish the object she had in view when she
called them together. She felt it was time the
Hand-cart Pioneers were recognized for the
part they took in the early history of our
Church and State. She had visited Presi-
dent Joseph F. Smith, told him her desires
and received his approval. He blessed her
and told her to go ahead and organize and
he would do all he could to help her.
	Having received the sanction and blessing


[Column 1]
of President Smith and being urged by
Maria Y. Dougall, Zina Y. Card and other
daughters of the Pioneers, Sister Lapish de-
cided to call the Hand-cart Pioneers to-
gether and organize. Accordingly, she with
the help of Sister Dougall, who was present
by special request, proceeded to organize the
society to be known as the Utah Hand-Cart
Pioneers and Daughters. Mary A. Van
was chosen temporary chairman and Pearl
Woodmansee temporary secretary. Because
this society owes its origin to Hannah Lap-
ish, she was given the title of Founder Gen-
eral and was chosen President, with Sarah
Swift first counselor, and Emily Beebe sec-
ond counselor, Mary A. Van, secretary,
Pearl Woodmansee, corresponding secre-
tary, Anna Livingston, assistant secretary,
Jennie Careless, historian, Sarah Kedding-
ton, treasurer, Hannah Saunders, chaplain,
Mary S. Houtz, registrar, together with 39
charter members, and M. Y. Dougall, an
honorary member.
	Constitution and by laws were formed and
a committee, with Frances Armstrong as
chairman, appointed to read and report.
They were approved by the committee and
adopted by the society, whose object was to
commemorate and perpetuate the memory of
the sublime faith and heroic courage of the
Utah Hand-cart Pioneers, who for the sake
of their religion, journeyed on foot, draw-
ing their belongings in two-wheeled carts,
a distance of from one thousand to twelve
hundred miles, over barren country, inhabi-
ted only by wild animals and Indians.
	It shall be the purpose of this society to
collect and preserve an accurate history of
those memorable journeys with their indes-
cribable sufferings, hardships, and priva-
tions, and a display of more than ordinary
fortitude. And it is the subject of this soci-
ety to preserve the names of all those who
started to come to this then Territory by
hand-carts, whether they reached their desti-
nation or perished by the way.
	For this purpose we feel that a monument
should be erected in a public place, and all
these names inscribed upon it. Having this
object in view, a small bank was purchased,
and presented to the society by Sarah Swift,
and at each meeting it has a conspicuous
place on the table where members drop do-
nations from time to time.
	This being the third anniversary of our
society, it seems only fitting that we should
refresh our memories upon a few of the
things that have transpired during that time.
We have met on the 14th of each month,
and have had many interesting talks
from those who crossed the plains with
hand-carts; also words of encouragement
from Prest. Joseph F. Smith, John Henry
Smith, Seymour B. Young, and many
others. We have met twice each year with
the Hand-Cart Veterans, where interesting
times were had, and have had excursions
to Wandamere and many pleasant parties
at different homes.
	During all our little existence, we have
been fortunate in having the beautiful home
of our loved President Frances Armstrong
for our meeting place, and have received
many courtesies and pleasant surprises from
her hands. At the expiration of two years,
she was unanimously chosen our second
President, with Alicia Arnold first counsel-
or, and Jennie Careless second counselor.
Sister Lapish had served us well, and we
still feel she is the “back-bone” of the soci-
ety.

[Column 2]
	We have chosen red as our color, typical
of the flower called “Indian Paint Brush”
which grew in abundance at that time. Our
society pin is made of enamel in colors. We
also have a banner, hand painted by our
Founder-General, Hannah Lapish. This
also portrays the pulling of hand-carts
across the plains and was proudly carried
by our society in the big parade on the 24th
of last July.
	Since our organization people have
awakened to the fact that there was such a
thing as a Hand-cart Pioneer and many
inquiries have come from all over the state,
showing great interest in the organization.
While we know we are still in our infancy,
yet we feel we will, as Sister Dougall said
we would, come to the front and be a lead-
ing society in Utah. We have come to the
front, and are being recognized. This was
proven at our recent conference, when we
were called together by our honored Presi-
dent Joseph F. Smith and counselors, and
entertained by them, and such men as Elders
Francis M. Lyman, Orson F. Whitney,
James E. Talmage, Bishop Heber S. Iver-
son, the Old Folks Choir, and others.
	We are proud of our society and the fact
that our pioneers and parents pulled hand-
carts across the plains. When we sing our
hand-cart song, “Some Must Push and 
Some Must Pull,” we feel, with Brother
Heber C. Kimball, that we have pushed and
pulled our way to Zion. Now we must keep
pushing and pulling to get into the Kingdom
of God.
					MARY A. VAN
							Secretary.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
	RELIEF SOCIETY REPORTS.
		BEAR RIVER STAKE.
	Bear River Stake Relief Society confer-
ence was held in the Garland Social Hall,
Jan. 16th, 1913, at 10 and 2 o’clock.
	President Margaret W. Manning, presid-
ing.
	There were present, First Counselor of the
Stake Presidency, P. M. Hansen, five High
Councilors, two bishops, one member of
high priests’ quorum, five members of the
Y. M. M. I. A. and Y. L. M. I. A. stake
boards, two ward teachers, two members of
Primary stake board, and one member of
Stake Religion class; Sisters Julia Farns-
worth and Elizabeth McCune of the General
Board, ten stake board members and a very
good representation of officers and members
from the various wards.
	Singing, Now Let us Rejoice.
	Prayer, Bro. J. P. Christenson.
	Singing, Sweet is the work, My God.
	Pres. Margaret W. Manning made the
opening remarks. Felt very grateful for the
good attendance at our conference and
thought the sisters had a great deal of faith
to enable them to get out so early and come
so far to conference; felt the Lord would
certainly bless them for so doing; felt grate-
ful that so many of the Priesthood were
able to meet with us; was more than pleased
to announce that we had two sisters of the
General Board with us, Sisters Julia Farns-
worth and Elizabeth McCune. They had ex-
pressed a desire to hear the local presidents
report the different societies, which was very
ably done.
	Duet. Sisters Lucetta Thompson and
Mame Shumway.
	Sister McCune: There is an excellent

[Column 2]
spirit in our midst; had lived in the country
and knew the extra effort the sisters had
to make to come so far. The Lord will
certainly bless us for all the efforts we put
forth to do our duty. Was very much
pleased with the reports that had been given
on the storing of grain and other Relief
Society work; encouraged the sisters to
continue in gathering grain; advised them
not to sell all of it at once, but felt we
should keep some always on hand; had
worked in the Temple and was very much
interested in work for our dead. President
Joseph F. Smith gave the General Board as
a mission, the making and keeping of tem-
ple clothes on hand; urged Relief Society
presidents to keep two or three suits on
hand in case of emergency. Mothers should
teach their daughters to come to the temples
properly dressed; felt it would be lovely for
girls to make their own dainty little temple
suits to be married in, instead of spending
so much time on their silks and satins.
	Sister Julia Farnsworth: Had enjoyed
the spirit of conference; thanked the sisters
for the warm welcome they had given them;
felt the gospel brings us nearer together
and makes us more or less acquainted.
Mothers should not neglect homes, hus-
bands, and children for public duties; urged
the sisters to do all they could in temple
work.
	Pres. P. M. Hanson made encouraging
remarks, and commended the sisters for
their faithful and energetic work in Relief
Society.
	Singing, The Happy Day has Rolled on.
	Prayer, Bro. J. E. Dewey.
	During the noon hours a wholesome lunch
was served to all present by the Garland
Relief Society sisters, the same having
been prepared by sisters from the different
societies.
	Afternoon session.
	Singing, Now let us Anew.
	Prayer, Bro. David Manning.
	Sister Julia Farnsworth: Thought the
attendance very good for so small a stake;
had been a member of the Relief Society for
thirty-seven years; read a sketch of the life
and mission of the Prophet Joseph Smith;
referred back to the council in heaven, and
mentioned the many incidents down through
the ages of men that were necessary for the
restoration of the Gospel, and the coming
of the Prophet Joseph Smith; portrayed
beautifully his youth and first vision; pic-
tured on through the struggles of life, how
he was fitted to fill his glorious mission.
	Sister McCune said: When talking of
the prophet it always brings a good spirit;
spoke of genealogical classes that were be-
ing organized in the Church; urged all to
join that could; encouraged family reunions,
so they could organize and help each other
in doing work for their dead. It is wonder-
ful what can be accomplished if we only
put forth our efforts; spoke of temptations
menacing us, especially the young. Parents
must be ever on the alert watching and
help to keep them clean and pure; spoke of
the evils in cities where young people went
to supper after dances and theaters, the
temptations there awaiting them.
	Sister Farnsworth said if we would be
loyal to the Exponent it would be printed
in magazine form and contain and outline.
	Ester C. Evans: Felt we had had a splen-
did conference; felt grateful for the visit-
ing sisters that were with us; said we had
made progress in our work and study in the


past year; felt anxious that we continue on
through the new year, and knew we would
if we would do our part and work unitedly
together. Prayed the Lord’s blessings on
us all.
	Conference was then adjourned for six
months.
	Singing, Abide with Me.
	Closing prayer, Hannah E. Loveland.
				EFFIE I. WELLING,
						Sec. Pro Tem.

		ST. JOSEPH STAKE.
	The Semi-Annual conference of the Re-
lief Societies of the St. Joseph Stake con-
vened at the meeting-house in Thatcher,
Sunday March the 9th, 1913, beginning at
10 a. m. We had no visitors from the Gen-
eral Board. President Elizabeth Layton and
counselors and all members of the Stake
Board present; also Stake President An-
drew Kimball and Counselors, members of
High Council, Patriarchs Claridge and Bar-
ney, and a number of Bishops from the dif-
ferent wards, which was quite an encourag-
ing feature. The morning singing was con-
gregational and the time spent in executing
the business part of the program.
	First Counselor Selena Phillips, who was
in charge, welcomed the sisters; hoped they
would gain renewed courage and interest in
attending our conferences, for there is so
much we can do to build ourselves up in the
work of the Lord. Spoke of utilizing our
spare moments in reading and informing
our minds on the various subjects which will
fit us to perform our parts in life.
	Reports, written and verbal, from several
of the wards were given, and a solo “At
Last” was beautifully rendered by Sister
Effie Montierth. Reports resumed, all
showing fairly good work done along the
lines of Relief activities.
	Sister Mary K. Taylor spoke by way of
encouragement to the teachers, explaining
the duties of this high calling, and urged
that they magnify their work along this
line. Said they should seek to understand
the principles of the Gospel, for we cannot
give what we do not possess. Said they
should seek the Lord in earnest prayer to be
inspired as to what is most needed in the
homes they visit. Their work is also to
relieve the needy, to comfort the mourners,
and in all things seek to do that which
tends to better and higher conditions in life
among the Saints. Said we must put forth
every effort to have all the married sisters
enrolled in the Relief Society.
	Sister Josephine Kimball, who recently
visited the Franklin Ward, which is about
sixty miles away, reported the Society in a
very prosperous condition, the sisters feeling
well in their work, their meetings resumed
after a siege of sickness, and a determina-
tion on their part to do better and more
efficient work. Had held a re-union and
entertainment by which they had replenished
their funds. All felt well and an enjoyable
time was had.
	The Stake Relief Society officers were
presented and unanimously sustained. Sis-
ter Alice Pace was added to the Board.
Meeting was adjourned until 2 p. m.
	Singing, “Zion prospers, all is well.”
	Benediction was pronounced by Laura
Barney.
	Conference was resumed at 2 p. m. The
session was held in connection with the reg-
ular Sabbath services. The choir sang some

[Column 2]
beautiful hymns and anthems, and a solo
was given by Sister Lottie Larson. Invoca-
tion was offered by Elder A. Barney, and
benediction was pronounced by President
John F. Nash. After the administration of
the sacrament, the meeting was given over
to the sisters and conference was resumed.
	The statistical and financial report for
the year of 1912 was read, showing an in-
crease in attendance at the meetings.
Through the efforts of the sisters a new
steel elevator has been built, with a capacity
of one thousand bushels, and in which they
have stored four hundred and fifty bushels
of wheat. A great deal of charity work
has been accomplished, not alone among our
own people, but the refugees from Mexico
have been gladly assisted in many ways.
	President C. M. Layton advised that, if
possible, the wheat now stored be disposed
of for cash, and when next harvest time
comes, fill the elevator with wheat of a su-
perior quality. Felt that the sisters were
doing a good work, and that the attendance
was equal to or better than the attendance
of any organization in the Stake. Urged
young mothers to join the society, and get
the good from it which is given in the dif-
ferent subjects taught. We should not
slacken our efforts to make our societies the
best in the world.
	Second Counselor Frances Kimball gave
a talk on the education of our girls—the
true education—wherein they might be
taught their high and holy mission which
they were sent on earth to perform, that of
the sacredness of motherhood. Deplored
the fact that the shrinking from this respon-
sibility was gaining ground among our Lat-
ter-day Saint girls. Showed the sin of this
evil and strongly urged mothers to teach
their daughters that no mission could equal
that of bringing into the world those spirits
held in reserve in the spirit world, which
God has ordained to assist in the consumma-
tion of this great latter-day work. Thought
we should be more conservative in our dress,
amusements, food, etc.
	Sister Emma B. Coleman spoke of our
several duties as Latter-day Saints, as mem-
bers of the Relief Society, and as mothers
in Israel. Said if we taught our children
to live and love the Gospel while they are
young, when they grow up, though they
might possibly stray away for a time, they
will have something to remember and event-
ually come back to, while those who have
been neglected, have nothing to remember
with love, and may never come back.
	A recitation, “What has she done? and
the reply,” was given by Sister Emma Mer-
rill.
	President Andrew Kimball read an “Anti-
cigarette bill,” now being discussed in the
Legislature and likely to become a law, and
made comment thereon. This has been got-
ten up mostly through the influence of the
Parents’ Class of the Sunday School, and
hoped that the efforts put forth would not
be in vain. Spoke of tithing and obedience

[Column 3]
to the Priesthood. Said no Board has a
right to change, add to or take from their
officers without the advice and consent of
those placed over them. Each Society
should have credit for their public work,
for waiting on the sick and attending to the
dressing of the dead. Advised that there be
a sufficient number on the Board to visit
every Society once a month or often-
er. Prayed God to bless all engaged in Re-
lief Society work.
	President E. W. Layton made closing re-
marks, thanking all for their attendance and
the choir for their beautiful singing. An-
nounced that conference would be adjourned
for six months.
		JOSEPHINE CLUFF KIMBALL.
						Stake Secretary.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


IN MEMORIAM.
ANN OLIVER YOUNG, A HANDCART VETERAN.
	Mrs. Ann Oliver Young, widow of the late
“Uncle” John Young, eldest brother of Presi-
dent Brigham Young, died Dec. 24, at 9:30
a. m. at the home of her brother, Samuel Oliver
in East Mill Creek.
	Mrs. Young was born Sept. 26th, 1832, at
Whaddon, Cambridgeshire, England. She be-
came a member of the Church of Jesus Christ
of Latter-day Saints in 1851; in 1856, she emi-
grated to Utah in one of the hand-cart com-
panies of that year, arriving here in Septem-
ber. In 1857, she was married to John Young
and continued to reside in Salt Lake City until
1873, when she moved to East Mill Creek and
took up her residence with her brother, Samuel
Oliver. “Aunt” Ann was a consistent Latter-
day Saint and proved faithful to every obliga-
tion. Of a modest and retiring disposition,
she rarely spoke of the trials and hardships
through which she passed during the early set-
tlement of Utah. Her passing removes an-
other of the hand-cart veterans, whose heroic
fortitude is an inspiration to those who are
acquainted with this portion of the history of
the Latter-day Saints.
	Mrs. Young had no children of her own, but
has been a mother to the seven children of
her brother, Samuel Oliver, who were deprived
of a mother’s care at an early age. These, with
the nine grandchildren, have been her constant
care.


“THY WILL BE DONE.”
’Tis true in life we oft must yearn—
	Wail o’er an infant’s empty cot;
Some go away that ne’er return.
	But O, the home is ne’er forgot.
We weep when God our loved one calls,
	’Mid falling tears we yield him up;
We tremble when the cold sod falls
	And we must drink the bitter cup.
Some at the altar we must leave,
	Yield unto man the love we prize;
And pray they ne’er may rue or grieve,
	While tears that blister fill our eyes.
Why cannot we be more content—
	Our God gave, He may take away;
Disguised sometimes are blessings sent,
	O say “Thy will be done” alway.
					LYDIA D. ALDER.

	An Exile’s Prayer...............................	57

AN EXILE’S PRAYER.
		(Inscribed to F. L. J.)
Father in heaven, hear the exile’s prayer,
A prayer of thousands scattered where, oh
	where.
Our feet are weary, in strange paths we roam,
Our hearts are aching for the dear old home.
We long to see the morning sun arise
Painting in rainbow tints the deep blue skies;
The bright rays glancing through the green
	pine trees,
Their sylvan music floating on the breeze.
Just as we’ve watched and lingered many a
	year
To see a new day dawning, bright and clear,
Dispelling gloom, and every shade of night,
As in the morning of creation’s light.
We long for each loved valley, mount and plain
To hear the children’s voices there again;
The hazy moonlight, and dear evening star,
They do not seem the same, so far, so far,
From the home shrines our willing hands have
	reared
Hearts all undaunted, that no danger feared;
And Thou hast blest us, and we trust Thee
	still,
Schooling our feelings to Thy righteous will.
We long to climb the hills and gather flowers
Blooming in nature’s own sweet sunny bowers;
We long for mountain peak, and grassy plain;
We long to hear our own wild birds again.
Once more to till the soil, to sow and reap,
To cheer the hearts that sorrow now, and weep,
To gather home again our scattered band,
And help reclaim our desecrated land.
We fain would clasp each hand, and hear the
	song
Of jubilee ring out deep, clear and strong,
To gather ’neath our church’s shadowy wing
To hear our choir the songs of Zion sing.
There rest our loved ones free from every care
Beneath the waving grass, and flowers fair;
Sacred the spot, so dear to every heart;
These, too, are of our lives and souls a part.
Here by the wayside, our beloved sleep;
By the lone grave we sorrow still, and weep;
Our Father whisper peace and heal our pain,
Help us to live, until we meet again.
Fond memories cling around those days, we
	never can forget,
Where e’er we are, where e’er we roam until
	life’s sun is set;
For deep heart throbs were woven into that
	web of life,
Where mingle threads of anguish, pain and
	joy and strife.


Hearts still will throb and tremble, when love's
	fingers touch the strings;
Sweet music blend with sorrow, where a lin-
	gering cadence rings;
Our mountains, rivers, valleys cast a halo on
	the soul,
That will touch the hills eternal, when life's
	billows cease to roll.
						S. E. R.,
April 28, 1913.		Mesa City, Arizona.


[Column 1]
		JULIA CRUSE HOWE.
	“Sweet thoughts are mirrored in her face
	And every motion is a grace.”
	Sincerely indeed, will these words find re-
sponse in the hearts of those readers whose
privilege it has been to come within the 
charmed circle whom Sister Howe calls
friends. To the writer, she has always 
seemed the embodiment of gentle dignity in
which refinement and culture and natural 
grace were combined.
	Having just completed her ninetieth year,
she is one of the few who are left to us
from among that group of close friends
which included Eliza R. Snow, Zina D. H.
Young, Bathsheba W. Smith, Elmina S.
Taylor, Presindia L. Kimball, M. Isabella
Horne, Sarah M. Kimball, Hannah T. King,
Elizabeth J. Stevenson, Maria W. Wilcox,
Elizabeth Howard, and our dear “Aunt
Em” Wells, most of whose names are found 
inscribed with loving tributes in the auto-
graph album of Sister Howe.
	To one born in Utah, after the pioneer
stage was passed, the life story of a woman
like Sister Howe is full of peculiar interest,
although much must of necessity be omitted
in one brief sketch.
	Julia Cruse was born at Boxford, near
Newberry, Berkshire, England, on the sev-
enteenth of June, 1823. Her parents were 
James and Mary Joyce Cruse, and to them
were born six girls and one boy, of which
children Julia was the youngest.
	A religious influence pervaded their
home. The children were taught to read

[Column 2]
the scriptures; family prayer was conducted
morning and evening, and the parents lived 
up to the best light and knowledge they
possessed. In spite of this condition, Julia
was not satisfied. “She to higher hopes
was destined,—in a finer mould was
wrought, and tempered with a purer flame.”
	The teachings in the Church of England
concerning infant baptism and hell were
responsible for her state of mind, and she
could not see the justice in condemning
pure little children to everlasting punish-
ment merely because parents failed to have
them christened. The death of a beloved 
companion who had not been christened
was also a great source of sorrow to Julia
because of this belief.
	While grieving for her friend and think-
ing she had gone to hell, Julia had a dream 
in which she went in a beautiful chariot to 
a city whose streets were paved with gold,
and all else was in keeping. As a jeweled
door swung open for Julia to enter one of
the mansions, she awoke with the thought
that she had visited Heaven and her ideas
were so changed thereby that she grieved
for her friend no more.
	Owing to ill health, several years of the
young girl’s life were spent with a married
sister, on the Isle of Wight.
	She became engaged to a young man of
good character and returned home to pre-
pare for her marriage.
	She found the household in confusion and 
her mother in tears because an older sister
had “disgraced” the family by joining the
“Mormons.”
	Her father being spiritually minded, said
to the weeping mother, “He that judgeth a 
matter before hearing it, is not wise.”
	Being thoroughly familiar with the
scriptures, but a short time was needed to 
convince Julia that her sister’s belief was in
accordance therewith, and soon she became
converted and was baptized into the Church
of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints on April
6th, 1849, and afterward both her parents 
joined the Church.
	An estrangement now having come be-
tween herself and her betrothed, Julia and
her sister left England for America in June,
1849.
	Before they left England, her father
blessed the daughters and to Julia, gave 
the promise that she should live to do a 
work of great importance, that her parents
would not live to accomplish. Upon arriv-
ing in America the Cruse sisters located in
St. Louis, Missouri, where they passed
through many interesting and some trying 
experiences, one being a fire which burned
almost everything they had, but through
the assistance of warm friends and their
own efforts they were soon made comfort-
able again.
	While in St. Louis, Julia met Amos
Howe, a skilled mechanic who was identi-
fied with the local branch of the Church.
In June, 1850, she became his wife, and for
fourteen years the Howes lived in St. Louis
and New York, where Mr. Howe was en-
gaged in construction work involving great


[Column 1]
responsibility, and where his superior abili-
ties along this line were highly appreciated.
	During these years five children were
born to them. The eldest two died in in-
fancy, leaving three sons, Edgar, George,
and Charles.
	They kept open house for the Elders and
had the pleasure of entertaining many dis-
tinguished members of our Church, among
them being Apostles Erastus Snow, George
A. Smith and Joseph F. Smith.
	In 1864 they came to Utah, locating in
Salt Lake City, where they found a warm
welcome awaiting them by the many whom
they had so hospitably entertained. Mr.
Howe became one of the strong business
men of the community.
	Sister Julia has been an indefatigable
worker in the various Church organiza-
tions, almost from the time they were be-
gun, until failing health and advanced age
necessitated quiet and rest.
	She acted as a teacher and afterward as
treasurer of the Seventeenth Ward Relief
Society. She organized a sewing class to
assist the Relief Society, which was called
the Junior Retrenchment association, and
this afterward became the Mutual Improve-
ment Association, of which Sister Howe
was President, for quite a number of years.
When the Primary Associations were estab-
lished she was made President of the Ward
association, and she served also as an aid
in the Relief Society Board of the old Salt
Lake Stake. She was a Sunday School
teacher for many years, and quite a num-
ber who were associated with Sister Howe
in Sunday School, Mutual, or Primary
work, and who were taught by her have
since attained prominent positions in various
Church organizations. Among these might
be mentioned Sister Maria Y. Dougall,
Clarissa S. Williams, Ann D. Groesbeck,
Elizabeth C. McCune, and Ida Felt; also
Brother George Albert Smith and others.
Her testimony of the gospel was always
most fervent and convincing.
	It was Sister Howe’s custom in years
gone by to entertain the widows and or-
phans and others in the ward who were
less fortunate than herself. She was most
generous in her contributions, spiritually,
physically and financially, and both she and
Brother Howe were ever ready to give of
their substance at all times to assist in the
work of the Lord.
	In these labors of love, Sister Howe en-
shrined herself in the hearts of hundreds
who have been blessed and benefited by her
wise counsels, her kindly admonitions, and
above all, by the beautiful example of her
daily life.
	“A spirit pure as hers, is always pure,
even while it errs, as sunshine broken in a
rill, though turned aside, is sunshine still.”
The prophetic promise of her father has
been fulfilled, not alone in the faithful per-
formance of these public duties, but in her
unceasing efforts to obtain genealogical data
and carry on her Temple work. For the
purpose of obtaining more complete records
she visited her birthplace in 1886 and was
highly successful in her quest. It is worthy
of note that she has lived to have the work
done for every one of the hundreds whose
record she was able to obtain. And now
at the age of four score years and ten she
rests quietly in her comfortable home await-
ing almost anxiously for the Father’s call.
	Perhaps no more fitting close could be

[Column 2]
given to this imperfect little sketch than to
quote the words written on a page of Sister
Howe’s album  by one of her “girls.”
	“The beauty of holiness beaming through
your life is an energy of moral suasion
greater than precept, command, or warn-
ing. The seen but silent beauty of holiness
speaks more eloquently of God and duty
than the tongues of men and angels.”
			MAY BOOTH TALMAGE.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
			IN MEMORIAM.
MARY JANE WHITE HINMAN, RELIEF SOCIETY
	PRESIDENT OF ALBERTA STAKE.
	On Sunday, April 20th, were held the
funeral services over the remains of Mary
Jane White Hinman. The tabernacle
at Cardston was almost filled with sympa-
thizing friends. As the large number of
relatives and fellow workers entered the
building, the congregation arose en masse.
Following immediately behind the sorrow-
ing husband and children, were the fellow
workers in the Relief Society, representing
most of the wards in the stake. Nearly all
were dressed in white and bearing the many
and most beautiful floral offerings, the scene
was of solemn grandeur and one long to be
remembered by all present. The services
throughout were most impressive. Symp-
thetic and comforting remarks were made
by Elders Johannes Anderson, Josiah A.
Hammer, Ammon Mercer, David H. Elton,
James T. Brown, President Edward J.
Wood, Patriarch H. M. Brown, and Sister
Sarah B. Davis of the stake board of Relief
Society. Elders Thomas Duce and Sterling
Williams made the opening and closing
prayers respectively. Beautiful music was
rendered by the choir and a quartet, consist-
ing of Mrs. Grace Cazier, Mrs. Ida Brown,
and Frank Layne and A. B. Cure. The
soloist for the occasion was Mrs. Mary
Leadbetter.
	Mary Jane White Hinman was born at
Farmington, Utah, April 5, 1853, where she
lived with her parents until her marriage
with Henry L. Hinman Nov. 18, 1874. She
continued to make her home here until she
moved to Canada in June, 1890. There were
born to her in Farmington three sons and
three daughters, and after her arrival in
Cardston two more sons were born. Jan-
uary, 1892, she was chosen counselor to
Zina Y. Card in the Y. L. M. I. A., which
position she held until August, 1894, when
she was appointed president of the Y. L.
M. I. A. in the Cardston ward. Two years
later, on Aug. 24, 1896, Aunt Jane was
chosen as second counselor to Mary L.
Woolf in the Relief Society in the Alberta
stake, which position she held until Nov. 14,
1909, when she was appointed president.
This position she held up to the time of her
death. She was a true, faithful Latter-day
Saint, and was ever ready to defend the
faith. She was loved by all associated with
her. She was one of the few who could
always be depended upon and whose word
was seldom if ever broken. During the past
two years she has suffered greatly. Sister
Hinman died April 13th at the Latter-day
Saints hospital, Salt Lake City, following
an operation, surrounded by sorrowing
friends and loved ones. A funeral service
was held in the undertaking parlors, about

[Column 2]
forty Canadians being present, among them
both of her counselors and the stake Relief
Society treasurer. The body was then
shipped to Cardston, and was met at Great
Falls by her sons Scott and Willard. As
the train which bore the remains drew near
the band played “Nearer My God to Thee,”
in the presence of friends who had gathered
there to do honor to the departed sister.
	The sympathy of the whole community is
with those who mourn, especially with the
bereaved husband who is in his seventy-sixth
year, and who for the past twenty-three
years has been cared for by his devoted wife.
			JANE W. BATES, Stake Sec.

		ELLEN THUESON.
				Monroe, April 6, 1913.
	In memory of Sister Ellen Thueson, an old
and esteemed member of the Relief Society,
of the Monroe South ward, who departed this
life March 10, 1913.
	As God has seen fit to take from us our
beloved sister, be it
	Resolved, That we the officers and members
of the Monroe South Ward Relief Society, do
acknowledge the hand of God in all things, and
extend our heartfelt sympathy to the bereaved
family of the departed.
	Resolved, That we follow the example she
has left us, in trying to live a life as near to
hers as possible.
	Resolved, That a copy of these resolutions
be given to the family, and a copy be sent to
the Woman’s Exponent.
	Sister Ellen Thueson was blessed and set
apart as second counselor to Sister Christina
Sorenson, at Orson Magleby’s, then acting
bishop, on the fourth day of December, 1898.
	She held that position faithfully for a period
of ten years. She was always a faithful mem-
ber and a guiding star in all our Relief Society
work, until the sickness that caused her death.
	May we follow her example in God’s noble
cause.
							JANE NAY.
			To the Family.
	Dear friends and bereaved relatives: In be-
half of the Monroe South Ward Relief Society,
I will read a few lines in love and sympathy
with the bereaved, and in fond remembrance of
Sister Ellen Thueson; no one, only those
who have lived by and learned to love her, will
know how we will miss her loving presence
and wise counsel, from every nook and corner
of the Relief Society work.
	God has called a noble spirit,
		One whom we all shall miss,
	With her wise advice and counsel
		And her face of cheerfulness.
	One could feel the Holy influence
		Of a spirit filled with love,
	She has earned a crown of glory,
		An eternal home above.
	Her life was not a path of roses,
		But how patiently she tread,
	Heeding not the thorns that pierced her,
		Nor life’s sorrows as they fled.
	In her long life of sacrifice
		She heeded not the strain
	Nor marked the slowly ebbing tide
		Of life’s enduring pain.
	As mother daily toiled she on
		What sweeter thought to you,
	Embracing all that’s pure and good
		Your friend, both tried and true.
	Mourn her not too deeply,
		Time alone we know can heal,
	The loss of her loving presence,
		Which you now so deeply feel.
	Live a life both pure and noble,
		Let her be your guiding star,
	For she loves and will await you
		In her heavenly home afar.
						MRS. JANE NAY.
	Monroe, Utah, Mar. 12, 1913.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


EVENING.
	This poem recently won a gold medal award-
ed by the London Poet’s Club.

Beyond all poesy, sublimity of song.
Sweet eventide, when mellow shadows throng
The valleys, and the slow, reluctant day,
On purple sandals, gliding, steals away
Into the gloaming by the sleeping streams,
A pensive spirit passing unto dreams. 
It is the hour when woods enchanted glow,
And gentle winds with dying odors blow.
From tree to tree faint pipes of evening call,
The bat sweeps circling by the ivied wall,
A lark drops fluttering to his lowly nest.
And drowsily the ringdove croons of rest.
The moan of kine has ceased, the drone of
	bees,
But ever a little stream among the trees
Speeds lightly on, and singeth as it goes,
Songs that a child at evening might compose,
Now the sun's flight is finished in the west,
Where far the great clouds veil his flaming
	crest,
The shepherd pens his weary flock away,
Safe folding laggard little ones that stray,
Bidding them browse awhile ere darkness steep
All things that move, in deep, embrowned
	sleep. 
High uplands as at early morning shine,
Sacred the light that gilds the day's decline,
As when the dawn with holy eyes appears
And opening blossoms sparkle with her tears,
For gross, foreboding day, which climbs the
	east, 
Calling the world to work, and fight, and
	feast,
The cunning day, the fierce, insistent hours
Of human strife with Nature's dreadful pow- 
	ers,
The groaning of a being chained to earth,
Although ablaze with vision from his birth.
But, come, the evening calls us, let us go,
We must not sorrow because earth hath woe.
A passion lingers in this serene air,
A passion void of triumph or despair.
Empty of storm, and hushed to calm delight,
We turn our eyes to greet the coming night.
The stars are streaming up the boundless hills,
The stars which smile at men's inconstant
	wills,
And in an opal radiance, crescent-wise,
The moon peers coldly from the limpid skies.


[Column 1]
	AN INTERESTING LETTER.
	An interesting letter from the recent
flooded district:
				Peru, Ind., May 15, 1913.
My Dear Grandma:
	Now that conditions are getting back to
normal I know you will be interested to hear
from me regarding the recent flood. As
perhaps you know, we had a rainy Easter
Sunday and not until seven o’clock Monday
morning did it stop, then the sun came out
and it looked as though the rain must be
all past. I went to town to do my market-
ing, and hearing that the Wabash river was
rising, I with a friend went down to see it.
About noon we returned to her home and
had luncheon, planning to go about a mile
up stream in the afternoon to the mouth of
the Wississinewa river, as we could hear
the roar of the water, and wanted to see the
sight. However, it started raining again
and we decided to wait, but at five o’clock
it had not stopped so we gave it up and I
returned home. All night the rain came
down and when I tried to phone my friend
Tuesday morning “central” told me the
wires in that neighborhood were down and
there was a foot of water around her home.
I hurried to town and when I reached the
Court House corner one block away I could
see the river was over its banks and already
one block of the business district was in-

[Column 2]
undated, and the water still rising. Every
one was talking about the high water mark
having been passed and as our city is nearly
level and I then was only about two squares
from the water, I thought I better get in 
some provisions. You see I was all alone
as Gene left Monday morning for Fort
Wayne. I first bought a pair of rubber
boots, then walked over to my friend’s house
or rather to within a half block of it, for
then I was in water up to my boot tops.
Their house is on a very high foundation
and she and her family were all right, as
they have an upper story. Her house is
farther from the river than mine, but that
part of the city is lower. I went back to
town and bought a big stock of canned
goods, oil stove, lamps and some alcohol. In
the store they were so busy I had to grind
my own coffee (eight lbs.) and carry all my
purchases home. I bought the largest
washtub I could and caught it full of rain
water, besides filling everything in the
house. The city water by this time was unfit
for use. I worried considerable about Gene
at first until I was told Fort Wayne was
higher, and then I felt better. We were
cut off from the outside world and I did not
know that he, too, was in a flooded country.
Tuesday night he heard about Peru and
tried to get word from me, but could not
nor could he get back here. Still he felt I
was safe as our apartment is on the second
floor and is built of brick. Then we are
only one block west of the court house, and
that is the highest point. We were both
mighty glad and thankful when we found
each other safe and well, Friday night. He
had to walk from Chili, about ten miles
north of here. I took in eight refugees and
the apartment opposite being vacant, we
fitted that up with spare things. The men
carried furniture from down stairs, and in
all we had about twenty-three to cook for
on a little alcohol stove. By this time we
had bought more canned goods, and we ate
mostly cold things, saving the alcohol to
make hot coffee. Everyone had their own
dishes, as washing them was impossible,
water was too valuable. The gas, water and
heat were now all off and we all had to
hang around the oil stove to keep the chill
off. Some of us crowded around that while
others slept, but I could not have slept for
the roar of the water was maddening. My
telephone still was working and the chief
operator and the other girls talked to us to
keep up their courage. They had nothing
to eat so I made a bucket of coffee and
boiled some eggs and took this, together
with some canned goods, over to them. The
exchange is a square away, but with my
boots I made one trip all right, later I sent
it over in a boat each time. Wednesday it
snowed, and this added to the suffering.
Most of the refugees were crowded in the
court house and library, and they had no
heat. Thursday the water started to go
down, and as three of the men in our party
were connected with the Brown Auto Truck
factory, on the north side where the water
was deep, they got a boat and went over to
investigate. While they were in the factory
some one took their boat. They sent word
to us by way of “central” and we took an-
other out, and I went along. The sights I
saw on this trip I will never forget. Still I
have since seen worse in South Peru, where 
nearly everything was wrecked.
	Everything considered, we have much to

[Column 3]
thank our Lord for. Hoping this finds you
well and happy, I am always
	Your loving grandchild,
				ELEANOR SEARS.


THE UTAH CHAUTAUQUA.
	One of the busiest and most helpful places
in Utah during the middle of July will be
on the grounds of the Utah Chautauqua As-
sembly at Ogden from July 17th to the 27th.
	During the ten days over eighty different
lectures, entertainments, or concerts will be
heard. Of special interest to women will be
the work of Mrs. Ida B. Cole and Professor
McKeavor.
	Mrs. Cole is the official representative of
the Chautauqua institution, and is a delight-
ful speaker. She will give two lectures on
art, which will be illustrated by life-sized
reproductions of some of the masterpieces
of the art of all times and all countries. One
lecture on home furnishings will be illus-
trated by large models of houses which are
furnished by the art committee of the Gen-
eral Federation of Women’s Clubs.
	Professor McKeavor of the Kansas Ag-
ricultural College, who is well known in
Utah, will conduct a parents’ class conven-
tion during the session which will be very
valuable.
	Pioneer Day will be celebrated under the
direction of Apostle D. O. McKay and in
the evening the “Mid-Summer Night’s
Dream” will be presented by Miss Maud
May Babcock and the Dramatic Club of the
U. of U.
	Among the other lecturers might be men-
tioned Dr. Charles Zueblin of Boston, Dr.
Ira Landrith of Nashville, Col. Bain of
Kentucky, and Bishop McConnell of Den-
ver.
	Good music will be furnished by the
Brahms, a mixed-quartet, the Imperial Male
Quartet, the Salt Lake Symphony Orches-
tra and others.
	Ample provision is made for the children.
They have lots of fun all day long with their
organized plays, athletics, swimming classes
and clubs.


AUNT LENORA MORLEY.
	SISTER OF ELIZA R. SNOW.
			A SKETCH.
	I opened the EXPONENT and the picture
of Cordelia Morley Cox struck me very
forcibly, as it resembled our Aunt Lenora.
It took me back in thought to my school
days, when we attended Aunt Lenora’s
school. If we were not studying diligently
we would receive a very gentle thump on
the head with her thimble. The first ex-
ercise in the morning was a chapter from
the Bible. In the afternoon we were taught
fancy work and sewing, of different kinds.
Aunt Lenora made a great number of bed-
spreads. They were made out of un-
bleached cloth which she would put on the
snow to bleach. When the cloth was bleached
she would embroider the spreads and they
were just as white as need be. I have one
of them now which I greatly prize. Aunt
Lenora was very industrious, always work-
ing. She lived around with the different
families. She was a mother to all. I know
felt her loss and wondered how Aunt
Eliza could control her feelings in order to
speak at her funeral, we were all crying.
She was honored and loved by all.
