SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH,	-	DECEMBER, 1913


THE UTAH WOMAN’S PRESS CLUB.
	The Utah Woman’s Press Club, at
their annual meeting held an election of
officers and discussed informally the coming
year’s work. Mrs. Amanda Done was elect-
ed president, and Mrs. May W. Cannon,
vice president; Mrs. Cora Leaver, secretary
and treasurer.
	The loan fund which is to be used to as-
sist in the publishing of books by the mem-
bers, is steadily increasing and the collection
of books for the library was also reported
favorably.
	An interesting feature of the meeting was
the proposal by Miss Kate Thomas, that
the society endeavor to get Aunt Em to
publish her long promised book, “Great
People I Have Known.” This proposition
was followed by a discussion which resulted
in the decision that for each meeting during
the year a chapter of the book be prepared
and read by the author. Certainly the many
friends of Mrs. Wells will be delighted with
this idea and we trust it will be carried out.
	The new officers are full of zeal for their
work, and everything points to a most suc-
cessful and profitable year.


AN ARRAIGNMENT OF PRESIDENT 
			WILSON.
	The Rev. Anna H. Shaw created quite a 
sensation at the national suffrage associa-
tion meeting when she declared President
Wilson had fallen short of the greatest op-
portunity he ever had or would ever have
in failing to say a word in behalf of wom-
an’s suffrage, when he read his annual mes-
sage to Congress.  She said no other Presi-
dent has ever had the opportunity of speak-
ing a word which might ultimately lead to


the enfranchisement of a large part of the
human family. Even Lincoln, who by a
word freed a race, had not this opportunity
to release from bonds one-half of the hu-
man family. I feel I must make this state-
ment as broad as it is for the reason that
we at Budapest, last year, realized woman-
kind throughout the world looked to the
United States to blaze the way for the ex-
tension of universal suffrage. President
Wilson has missed the one thing that might
have made it possible for him never to be
forgotten.


THE RELIEF SOCIETY OUTLINE. 
	A general outline for work in the Relief
Society has been prepared by a committee
appointed by the officers and directors of the 
General Board. The outline will be ready
for distribution to the several stakes by the
first of the year.
	It embraces many important and interest-
ing subjects and can not fail to be most
beneficial to the members of the organiza-
tion.
	Much attention is given in the outline to
genealogical work, to which the officers of
the different stakes are urged to give their
early attention. Some stakes have already
had organized classes for this study and
have done some splendid work, others are
just adopting it, while still others have not
yet taken it up. It is the desire of the general
officers that this coming year all the societies
willl have completed this course and thus
instigated a desire among the people of bet-
ter, more systematic and increased temple
work.
	It is likely that some of the subjects on
home ethics have already been studied in the
societies, and in such cases the repetition of
such lessons is left to the discretion of the
presiding officers to determine.
	Where societies have not completed the
outline work that has been provided for
them by the respective stakes, that also is
optional with the local presiding officers to
decide, whether to finish the work already
started and then take up the new outline or
to drop the work and begin with the general
outline as soon as it is received.
	The subject of a general outline for the
Relief Society has been much discussed the
past few years, in fact ever since the moth-
er’s work was introduced as departmental
work, and there still remains the question
whether the most benefit can be derived
from the whole body studying the same sub-
jects at the same time, or from each stake
planning its own work. After long delibera-
tion on this matter the general officers have
decided to issue the new outline.
	That it will be gratefully and joyfully re-
ceived by the members and officers through-
out the different wards and stakes is certain
and much pleasure and advantage will
doubtless be derived from its study.
	The outline has been prepared by a most
efficient and capable committee and in its
preparation much time, study and thought
have been given.
	The work will go forth to the sisters of


the Relief Society with the hope that every
lesson will bring splendid results, and awake
a new interest in the great work in which
we are engaged.


THE NATIONAL COUNCIL.
	The executive session of the National
Council of Women was held in New York
City Nov. 21, 22.
	Mrs. Emmeline B. Wells and Miss Sarah
McLelland of the Relief Society, Mrs. Mar-
tha H. Tingey, and Mrs. Edith Lovesy of
the Y. L. M. I. Association, were in attend-
ance at the session. It was quite important
that all the affiliated societies be represented
wtih their full quota as much important
business was transacted, not the least of
which was the election of officers. Next
year, 1914, the quinquennial will be held in
Rome, when all the great organizations of
women throughout the world will send rep-
resentatives.
	There have been only four gatherings of
the International Council, the first held in
the United States in the city of Washington
when Mrs. May Wright Sewall was elected
president, the second was held in London
and Mrs. Sewall was re-elected, the third in
Berlin when the Duchess of Aberdeen was
chosen president, the fourth in Montreal
when the Duchess was again elected. The
discussions at the triennial in New York
were in anticipation of the business which
will be transacted next year in Rome and
dealt largely with social legislation and mat-
ters pertaining to the status of women in
social and political life. The Utah women
who were in attendance not only took part
in the discussions but also received impor-
tant appointments on committees and were
the recipients of marked attention by the
women of the council. Mrs. Emmeline B.
Wells was elected chairman of the advisory
committee of the Council, Mrs. Clarissa S.
Williams of the Relief Society was elected a
delegate from the United States to the quin-
quennial, to be held in Rome in 1914, and
Mrs. Ida B. Smith, who is at present in
England with her husband, President Hy-
rum Smith, was elected an alternate.
	After the adjournment of the council the
Utah delegation visited friends and relatives
in New York, attended the Sunday services
at the mission headquarters and then pro-
ceeded to Washington, where they spent
Thanksgiving day. The influence gained in
the world by association with women of
note and ability who devote their lives to
causes of humanity is helpful to our women,
and the Latter-day Saint women yield an
influence that is beneficial to others. Much
prejudice has been allayed through this affil-
iation and the true position and condition of
the "Mormon" women has been made plain.
Falsehoods and slanders have been refuted
and the truth been told concerning the peo-
ple of Utah.
	The presence of Latter-day Saint women
at these great gatherings has led to many
newspaper comments, and their dignity, in-
telligence, and benevolence have been sub-
jects of much praise. The officers of the
council have found the Utah women most
capable, and often advise with them on im-
portant matters. Such recognition is much
appreciated and we trust will always as now,
be well deserved. The Utah delegation re-
turned home safely after an absence of two
weeks, feeling that their mission was the
means of much good.


NATIONAL SUFFRAGE ASSOCIATION.
	The meetings of the National Suffrage
Association, this month at Washington, are
considered the most important ever held
by that association. The suffrage women
have held annual gatherings in the different
cities of the United States, meeting every
alternate year at the national capital for the
past forty years. The results in gaining
recruits to the suffrage ranks, removing
prejudice, advancing social legislation for
the benefit of the woman laborer, and many
other achievements have marked the on-
ward trend of the work, but the fact that
in all that time only ten states have granted
equal rights to women, is not considered
sufficient progress and that the rest of the
women of the nation might enjoy the fran-
chise the pleading, petitioning and waiting
period is about over; patience is exhausted;
and the women now propose to make a de-
mand from the law-givers for more atten-
tion to this subject. The papers state that
the scene resembled a charge on a citadel,
when more than a thousand women marched
from the suffrage headquarters to the cap-
tol. The women moved in regimental
formation, in quick step order, and crowded
the hearing room long before the committee
appointed to hear them arrived. The re-
quest made by the women was that a House
committee on suffrage be appointed, equiv-
alent to a similar one in the Senate. The
request is made at every session of the asso-
ciation for an amendment to the Constitu-
tion in behalf of the enfranchisement of
women, and with special committees in each
branch of Congress the day seems not so
far distant for this result.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


A NATIONAL ACADEMY OF LITERA-
	TURE AND ART FOR WOMEN.
	It is with great satisfaction that we note
the wonderful advance in thought among
great people concerning the status of women
in art and literature. While some of the
greatest writers have been women, it is still
a fact that public acknowledgment of wom-
en’s talents has seldom if ever been given,
save perhaps to those of histrionic ability;
now, however, an American has taken the
initiative and the word has gone forth, and
another great step has been taken in human
progress. Professor Brander Matthews of
Columbia University, president of the men’s
national institute, in an address before the
academy proposed a national institute for
women.
	Professor Matthews said that women had
never been given an opportunity to become
members of the national institute. He said
the subject had never come up, it has never
been considered. It is quite proper, how-
ever, that women should have an institute
and academy of their own. If such writers
as Mary Wilkins Freeman, Edith Wharton,
Margaret Deland, and Ida M. Tarbell, and
such painters as Martha Caisatt and Mary
Beilitt, would join to initiate such a move-
ment the national institute would be glad to
co-operate with them in any way possible in
the formation of the women’s organization.
	The gold medal of the academy was pre-
sented this year to Augustus Thomas as a
dramatist. Previous awards have been in
sculpture to Augustus St. Gaudens, in his-
tory to James Ford Rhodes; in poetry, to
James Whitcomb Riley, and in architecture,
to William B. Mead. Why have a separate
institute for women? Rather let these noted
women, mentioned together with others,
unite in persuading Professor Matthews to
interest himself in the admission of women
to membership in the academy and institute.


[Column 1]
		AUNT BETSY’S LEGACY.
	Main street in the town of Lind consisted
of one store, where general merchandise was
sold, a drug store and post office combined,
one one side of the street, while on the other
was a plain square board building known as
“The Lind Hotel,” a stable with yard where
teams could be boarded for a day or so, and
hay and grain were sold, an amusement hall,
and a little shop containing patterns and ma-
terial for art needle work, where lessons
were given in all kinds of embroidery,
crochet and knitting. 
	It is needless to say in such a town there
were no paved streets nor electric lights, nor
other modern improvements, on the con-
trary, the wild grass fringed the edges of
the sidewalks, and narrow creeks of running
water took the place of curbing between the
streets and the walks. The settlers of Lind
had reserved one large square for city pur-

[Column 2]
poses where a rather pretentious city hall
was erected from native stone, in the cupola
of which hung a massive bell, this with a
less pretentious building on the same square
which served for a school room during the
week, and on Sundays a house of worship,
constituted the only public buildings in the
town. It is with the little art shop on the
corner, however, that this story has to deal.
In new settlements out on the western
prairies one seldom expects to find culture
and refinement. The very hardships of pio-
neer life and the circumstances which lead
a family out into such places would rather
lead one to expect a rough and fortune-
seeking community. The town of Lind
was not settled either by fortune seekers or
rangers, but its settlers were a part of that
wandering people who, driven from their
beautiful homes in the east, had sought and
found a refuge in the heart of the western
desert. The Lind people had not left with
the first exiles, nor had they followed them
to their destination, but had stopped midway
and established a trading post, expecting
later to continue the journey. This, how-
ever, never happened and the little town
became a permanent home for the few
weary travellers, increasing but slowly as the
years rolled on, and never assuming a thriv-
ing community. In such a place the men
have generally the most amusement, at least
time hangs less heavily on their hands, for
with the work of subduing the soil, trading
horses and raising cattle, such as is always
the business of these prairie towns, their
minds are well occupied, but the women
fare less kindly. The homes are generally
widely separated and the tasks are less in-
teresting. It happened, therefore, that the
one bright spot in the town of Lind for the
women folks was the little corner store kept
by the dearest of old ladies known to every-
body as “Aunt Betsy.”
	Perhaps some day you may happen to see
on the walls of some modern library or
drawing room an old-fashioned sampler or
ottoman cover worked in red and yellow
wool roses, or a buckskin gauntlet, em-
broidered in silk floss; if you do, it will re-
call Aunt Betsy and her little store where
she taught the women to fashion all kinds
of wonderful designs with the needle and
crochet hook. With her brother’s family
Aunt Betsy had come to America in the
early 60s from a pleasant English home,
and with them had remained by the way-
side. It was not her nature, however, to
be a dependent on her brother nor a nurse
for his children, and having brought with
her a splendid box of material for needle
work and some little means of her own she
soon established herself in business making
her home in the back end of the room, di-
vided only from the store by a large curtain
of blue denim.
	Nothing ever happens in a country town
but what everybody knows every detail and
the corner grocery, post office or village
store, is the great dispensary of news, but
for once in the town of Lind the people were
dumb and not a soul could explain the mys-
tery when one Christmas morning Aunt
Betsy found on her doorstep a wee baby
girl. In a city, such a circumstance might
not create any consternation, at least not
for long, but in an open prairie village miles
from everywhere, in a town where every-
body knew everybody, and there never had
before been a mystery, this happened to be
more than a nine-day wonder, in fact it

[Column 3]
never ceased to be a wonder until many
years later when—but wait, this comes not
now. When Aunt Betsy picked the bundle
off her doorstep in the early morning it had
a light covering of feathery snow, for the
snow had been falling all night and this
slight dusting showed that it had not lain
there very long; sufficiently long, however,
for any tracks of vehicle or footprints to be
covered, and it seemed as though it had
dropped down from the skies with the 
shower of flakes that covered it. Such a-
theory, indeed, Aunt Betsy was willing to
accept, for it was better for her to look upon
the little thing as a gift from heaven and
immediately try to give it a mother’s care,
than to strive to fathom a mystery desired
to be kept, and perhaps bring upon its head
disgrace and sorrow. After many surmises
and unnecessary questionings into each
others’ affairs the village folk resigned
themselves to Aunt Betsy’s philosophy, “If
it didn’t come down from heaven with the
snow, probably the Lord knew where it did
come from, and when he is ready would let
them know too; I only knew it was here
and I intend to take care of it and ask
no further questions.” She did not say,
however, that on its little body was a tiny
shirt, made of soft linen on which some
three weeks previously, she believed she had
stamped a small embroidery scallop, and
which now she had laid carefully away for
further developments.
	When the tiny thing was old enough to
have a name all the women in the town
wanted to assist in the selection, and many
an interesting conversation was held over
the counter about the baby’s name. “She is
so pretty,” said one mother. “I should
think you would call her June or Rose or
Pearl or some fine name like that.” “She is
such a little thing,” said another; “why
don’t you call her Fanny or Minnie or
Mattie.” “She almost belongs to all of us,”
said still another; “why not name her Lindy
for the town.” This seemed to suit most
of the women folk, but Aunt Betsy was a
long time deciding. In the meantime, how-
ever, she was making most wonderful clothes
for the approaching day of the christening;
embroidered flannels and dress, while a cloak
of white cashmere done in blue forget-me-
nots was the principal gift of this loving
foster mother, and not until the second
Christmas, when the little thing was begin-
ning to talk, did she receive a name.
	“I’d like to please you all,” Aunt Betsy
said, “but you have so many different opin-
ons. Now I never but once in my life had
a chance to hear or see any great personage
and that was when my father took us all up
to London to hear Jenny Lind sing, think-
ing perhaps as her name was the same as
ours we might be some relation, and it’s a-
good thing to be loyal to your own family,
so I think I’ll just name this little might of
a thing for that great singer. Lind being
the name of this town named for my brother,
who was the first town officer, I guess it
will suit you all pretty well anyhow.”
	What Aunt Betsy didn’t tell was that the
little embroidered shirt so carefully laid
away had been stamped for some singer
girl, who a few weeks before had responded
time and time again to their applause in the
town amusement hall. So Jenny Lind, un-
der Aunt Betsy’s tutelage and care became
quite a girl in the little community. She
was trained in all things, which her good
foster mother knew, and though her accom-


[Column 1]
plishments were few, she was the leader
among the young people in every social
affair and a great favorite with all.
	Aunt Betsy gave the girl great freedom
at all times, except when some straggling
troupe of players stopped on their way and
entertained the villagers for a night or two.
At such times she was more than watchful
and never let her out of her sight, and at
just such times Jenny herself was more anx-
ious for her freedom. The dull little town
always seemed so much duller after these
visits. The novelty of entertaining actors
even of mediocre ability was quite exhilarat-
ing, and Jenny’s feet always seemed to want
to dance much faster when she heard the
music, so fast indeed that poor Aunt Betsy
was afraid she would dance into their crowd
and disappear as suddenly as she had come.
	Perhaps she would have joined some of
the troupes had she followed her own in-
clination, but there was a magnet greater
even than her love for her foster mother
which held Jenny from leaving home. The
youth of the village had looked with admir-
ing eyes for a long time on this young girl.
More beautiful and something different to
the other girls, she had unwittingly won the
hearts of the young men, but by none of
these had her heart been touched. It was
not love for anything human that held Jenny
at home, but a fascination for a piece of
needle work of her foster mother’s over
which she marveled and questioned and pon-
dered many hours. As time went on Jenny
assumed most of the duties of the little home
and business, leaving Aunt Betsy more time
for her teaching and embroidery. This par-
ticular piece of work, which fascinated
Jenny, was never worked upon when others
were around, only at night and early morn-
ings, and the only satisfaction Jenny ever
received for her questions was “some day it
will explain to you a mystery.” The thought
that perhaps it had something to do with
her birth was enough to keep the girl near
her kind benefactress who, though she had
been all that a mother could be, had never
been called other than Aunt Betsy, “leaving
the sacred name of mother to be given only,
if the good Lord ever allowed, to her who
had the right”—so said the kind old soul
when upbraided by the girl for not allowing
her to call her mother. “You were my most
precious Christmas gift, Jenny, and some
day, I hope, we will have the chance to give
on that holy day, an offering in return for
the joy you have brought to me.”
	It was not easy to obtain always the
proper colored silks for the fine workman-
ship of Aunt Betsy’s wonderful picture, and
when sent for, it took a long time to get the
articles by mail, and so like Penelope’s
famous needle work it seemed as though it
never would be done, but as Jenny grew to
young womanhood the tapestry took on a
more finished form. On the Christmas when
Jenny was eighteen it was her gift from
Aunt Betsy.
	A work the dear old lady had wrought
throughout the eighteen years; a work the
tired brain had pondered over and the clever
hands had at last been able to accomplish.
	The picture was a young girl like Jenny in
all respects save complexion, for Jenny’s
hair was dark, while the picture girl had
hair of burnished gold. Jenny’s eyes were
that unknown color between the brown and
violet which sometimes seem black and
sometimes blue—hazel the poets call them

[Column 2]
and the painter calls them grey. This had
been the difficult part in making the picture,
for with, Jenny’s ever-changing eyes in front
of her and the memory of those soft, brown
eyes when she sold the little pattern, Aunt
Betsy was much puzzled, however, with the
touch of a soft brush to help with her needle,
she as though by inspiration, caught the soft,
dreamy expression and the work was fin-
shed. That her memory served her well is
not remarkable, for in all the years she had
been striving to recall the details of dress
and figure of the girl she had spoken to, but
once, and her work was not alone a work of
art but a memory and an inspiration.
	To what purpose had she thus employed
all the years, and now what use could this
specimen of handicraft be? She knew not
herself, but she gave it to her little girl with
admonition to display it where possible but
to never part with it, for in its lines were
written all she knew of Jenny’s mother.
	The town of Lind never grew beyond the
village dimensions, though the railroad came
within a few miles and a branch line was
run down to the town where a cattle guard
was built, and not far away a great city grew
out of the plains, to which the village folk
took their produce and where they some-
times went for entertainment.
	Before another Christmas Aunt Betsy had
fallen into her last sleep, leaving besides her
picture legacy, all her earthly possessions to
her adopted daughter. Jenny very soon dis-
posed of her property, having no natural
taste for those things which had been a part
of her existence and with only a few cher-
shed articles sought the more exciting life
in the near-by city. She had some little
means and considerable common sense, so
was soon established in a good home and
began her pursuit of occupation. Perhaps
it was fate, perhaps fancy, which led her
direct to the manager of an opera house to
apply for a position on the stage before she
had ever had any schooling in dramatic art,
dancing or musicc other than the church
sociables of her native town afforded. She
was not without the knowledge, however,
that she had the gift, for never in all her
life had she been able to keep still when she
heard the sound of music. It was not her
voice, though she was something of a singer,
that responded to the strains, it was her feet,
and many a time in her rambles for wild
flowers out on the prairie had she whiled
away hours, dancing alone to the music of
her own voice. So when the manager asked
her how long she had taken lessons in danc-
ing she answered, “All her life.” He gave
her a trial and she swept before him with
such weird grace and agile step as to at once
win his favor and obtain employment. He
related to his wife at dinner the story of the
new girl who had applied for a position and
how beautifully, yet wildly she danced, and
that she had no conception of costumes or
stage requirements, yet he had decided to
engage her, and told his wife, who was
herself a retired actress, to be at the re-
hearsal on the following day and take the
new girl in hand.
	Jenny was somewhat amazed when she
appeared for rehearsal, with the appearance
and chatter of the bevy of chorus girls and
other people who assembled to do over their
parts before the evening performance. Her
heart just about failed her and she had de-
termined to quietly withdraw when the man-
ager’s wife accosted her. Mrs. Watts knew

[Column 3]
instantly the girl, she was so different in
every way, and she saw first the hesitancy,
then the resolve in her face to quit, so she
speedily intercepted Jenny. Mrs. Watts was
not an unkind woman but had given untold
aid and help to many struggling girls. She
had seen hundreds come to the stage led—
most of them—with an idea that it was a
quick way to fame and fortune, only to find
it the quickest way to an opening to the
way to shame and sorrow, and her heart
had often led her to advise girls to keep
away from the footlights and seek other em-
ployment. But this was her husband’s busi-
ness and she, as far as her conscience would
permit, assisted him in his work. It is a
hard thing to be manager of theatrical con-
cerns and be above blame.
	The profession itself makes girls liable to
temptation, the late hours, the stage ap-
parel, the necessary contact with all kinds
of people, the freedom of speech, and the
generally unsatisfactory wages are factors
which every girl should consider before
adopting a stage career, and if she be pure
minded should ask herself “Am I strong
enough to place art first and with all my
might struggle against every temptation and
live alone for that art?” Few consider and
those generally who do, find themselves not
so strong as they believed.
	To Jenny the first rehearsal was a perfect
nightmare. She was not asked to join the
girls just at first but to watch them; prob-
ably had she begun the dance with them, the
music would so have lured her on that she
would have overcome her timidity. Some-
thing in the face of Mrs. Watts held her
almost spellbound. When that lady finally
asked her if she was ready to join the girls
in their second try of the dance she said,
“No, I must go, but you, I must see again,
think I have seen you before.” Her slow,
strange speech mystified the lady and she
was preparing to let her go when she sud-
denly caught a look in Jenny’s face which
brought back some forgotten hour. And
the two women stood before each other as
though trying to recall some past acquaint-
ance. Presently Jenny, who though the
younger was after all the stronger, said come
with me I have something for you to see,
and the two left the theatre together[-]
	After a half hour ride on the car they
were in Jenny’s room and she had unpacked
Aunt Betsy’s legacy and together they were
trying to unravel its mystery. At first Mrs.
Watts thought the girl must be mad to think
she had any connection with just a piece of
embroidered tapestry, but suddenly her eye
caught the scalloped pattern worked on the
girl’s bodice. Then she remarked, “I used
to do some little needle work; I believe I
have done that pattern on the waist.”
	With this Jenny flew to her trunk and
brought forth the little linen shirt Aunt
Betsy had treasured for so many years, and
then—they both knew.
ANNIE WELLS CANNON.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


Miss Josephine Baker, as chief of the
Division of Child Hygiene of the New York
City Health Department, is concerned with
the well-being of over 825,000 children.
There are 135,000 babies born in New York
each year and during the four years of Dr.
Baker’s administration infant mortality has
been reduced by thousands annually.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
			MOTHERHOOD.
	In considering the subject of Motherhood,
shall do so under several different head-
ings: first, what we owe our mothers; sec-
ond, what our mothers owe us; third, the
conjoint responsibility of father and mother,
in rearing a family.
	James Whitcomb Riley says: “The
heaven that lies about us in our infancy, is
motherhood, and no matter how exalted, or
how depraved we may become, we are al-
ways attended by the grace of a mother’s
love.” Every great man has glorified a
great mother A great Jewish rabbi says:
“God could not be everywhere, so He made
mothers.”
	Is there any limit to the debt of gratitude
we owe to the one who, since giving us
birth, has stood like a wall of granite be-
tween us and the ills of childhood, the
temptations of youth, and the sorrows of
maturity, until age has whitened her hair, or
perhaps the summons has come and she has
passed over the silent river, leaving a va-
cancy that no one else on earth can fill; no
one else so willing, so sympathetic, so end-
less and untiring" in devotion. 

	“Nobody knows the work it takes
	To keep the Home together,
	Nobody knows the steps it takes.
	Nobody knows, but Mother.”

	Who shall say when the debt we owe her
is paid? and in what way it shall be paid?
No one but the child himself. Bulwer says:
“The best monument a child can raise to the
memory of his mother, is that of a clean,
upright life, such as she would have re-
joiced to see her son live.” As children we
cannot escape the responsibility of our ac-
tions, and they most truly reflect credit or
discredit upon ‘the memory of the one we
love most, our mother. One mother, in
writing to her son, said:

“Do you know that your soul, is of my soul
	such part
That you seem to be fiber and core of my
	heart?
None other can pain me as you dear, can do,
None other can please me or praise me as you,
Remember, the world will be quick with its
	blame,
If shadow or shame ever darken your name,
‘Like mother, like son is a saying so true,
The world will judge largely of ‘mother’ by
	you.”

	Truly then, until we too cross over and
join her on the shore of the hereafter, the
debt and responsibility we are under to
mother, is never paid.

WHAT A MOTHER OWES HER CHILD.
	Martin Luther has said: “The prosperity
of a country depends not upon the abund-
ance of its resources, nor on the strength of
its fortifications, nor on the beauty of its
public buildings, but upon its number of en-
lightened and cultivated citizens, its men of
character and worth; here are to be found
its true interests, its chief strength, its real
power.” Upon whom does the responsibility
rest, of giving such men to the world, if not
upon the mothers of men?
	No greater blessing was ever given by
our Heavenly Father to man and woman,
than parenthood. The custody of a little
child, fresh from our Father’s presence, is
the greatest trust confided to mankind.

[Column 2]
“They are idols of hearts and of households,
	They are angels of God in disguise.
His sunlight still sleeps in their tresses,
	His glory, still gleams in their eyes.”

	Having an assurance of this, how anxious
we should be as parents to so order our
lives, that the tender litttle plants should
grow upright and tall, and straight, that the
fruit-time of their lives might be all that
the blossoming promised.
	No more ennobling and worthy desire can
fill the heart of any woman than to be thus
abundantly helpful to these little children,
not only in her own home, but in every
home to which her influence may be ex-
tended.
	The four corner-stones upon which char-
acter should be builded are: a loving faith
in God, truthfulness, obedience, and self-
denial. The ideals which fill the mental
horizon of youth color all after years; they
are the well-springs which feed the “thought
garden” of the soul. Let us see to it then
that these ideals are lofty, filled with the
love of God, and charity to mankind. “That
which we make our children desire, is of
more importance than that which we make
them learn.”
	Most of the crime and misery of the world
are due to the early influence of home. In-
fancy is neither vicious nor virtuous it is
simply innocent, and is susceptible alike to
good and bad influences. Its safety depends
alone on the watchfulness of its guardians.
	Parents, your child is a bound volume of
blank paper, upon which is to be written the
history of your own actions.	The poet
Burns said:

“O, wad some pow’r the giftie’ gie us
To see oursel’s as ithers see us!”

	My friends, we need go no farther than
the confines of our own homes, to have this
wish gratified. No mirror ever more faith-
fully reproduced an object held before it,
than do the acts of our children represent
the lives of their parents. Of all human in-
fluences those of the home are the most far-
reaching in their results. It then remains
for us as parents to make our homes as per-
fect as we can, that our children may find
within their walls a joy and contentment
against which the allurements of the amuse-
ment hall will have no influence. I believe
one of the most potent factors in making a
home thus perfect is music. You know we
are told a song of praise is a prayer to God.
There is no perfect home without a musical
instrument of some kind. There is no tie
that will withstand the ravages of time and
absence, like the memory of the good old
home songs. Just last week, in the state
prison of California, a prisoner about to
suffer the death penalty for the crime of
murder, was asked by the warden what his
last request would be. He was a native of
Italy, and with tears in his eyes, he asked,
that a woman from his own country, with
a sweet voice, be found and allowed to come
and sing to him the songs of his fatherland.
Hand in hand with music is the literature of
the home. We cannot be too careful what
books and magazines we place within the
reach of a child. Henry Ward Beecher said:
“Books are the windows through which the 
soul looks out; a home without books is like
a house without windows.” We should pro-
vide a diversity of reading in order that
the child’s mind, through the medium of
books, may be given a knowledge of every-

[Column 3]
thing that will fit him for future usefulness;
but always with one great aim in view, to
bring the mind in sweet accord with the
will of God. There is one book that will
never fail us in our hours of pleasure or sor-
row—the Bible. When Sir Walter Scott lay
dying in his great library in Abbotsford, he
said to his son-in-law, “Reach me down the
Book.” “Which book,” asked Lockheart,
viewing the shelves, filled with thousands of
volumes. “There is but one Book,” cried
Scott, and he died clasping the Bible in his
arms. I believe we do not, as a people,
make sufficient study of our sacred books.
If we did, we would be better fitted for the
responsibilities we have to meet, as parents
and members of the Church to which we
belong.

CONJOINT RESPONSIBILITY OF PARENTS.
	Much has been said about the responsi-
bility of motherhood. We have it sung into
our ears from pulpit and press. I would like
to impress upon those present, if possible,
the unity of the father and mother in the
rearing of a family of children. Neither
parent, no matter how zealous, can alone
make a perfect home. It takes the united
wisdom and effort of both to successfully
cope with the difficulties that arise. How
often we see fathers who seem to take no
farther interest in their child, than to see
that it is well housed, well fed, well clothed.
When mother says the boy, or the girl,
should go away to college, he stands ready
to meet all accounts for board, etc., that
accrue therefrom. He leaves all the train-
ing—physically, mentally, spiritually and
morally—to the mother, teacher, or any one
who will assume the responsibility. Now,
all this I hold to be wrong. The father owes
it to himself as well as the child, to be its
companion and confidant. I have not much
fear for the future of a child whose father
nightly calls the family together and, in a
few well-chosen words, tells them of life,
and the temptations they will meet, and then
with a short earnest prayer, sends them to
bed with a heart filled with gratitude for
God’s goodness. Long after that father has
answered the summons from above, will his
humble prayer be a strong factor for good,
in the memory and life of that child. I be-
lieve we should live more with our children
and not so much for them, and I believe we
will profit equally with them in the com-
panionship. One writer thus speaks of chil-
dren:

“These truants from home and from heaven
	They have made me more manly and mild,
And I know now how Jesus could liken,
	The kingdom of God to a child.”

God help us parents all to live aright,
And may our homes all Love and Truth enfold,
Since life for us no loftier aim can hold,
Than leading little children to the light.

				MRS. GEORGIA HATCH.
Metropolis, Nevada, March 17, 1913.


INDUSTRY.

	The following essay was given at a Relief
Society meeting in 1856, by Sister Elizabeth
McLelland.
	I have chosen the subject of “Industry”
to speak upon this evening. Industry,
whether it be hard labor, or the exercise of
the mind, is that by which the great and


good of our sex have been distinguished.
Indeed, it is by the application of this prin-
ciple to their lives that they have become
truly good or truly great. It is said of Jesus
that He went continually about doing good,
hence we see industry was a prominent part
of His character, and no one can truly say
he is a faithful follower of Jesus, unless his
faith is made manifest by his works.
	I can bear testimony myself that when I
have the Spirit of God upon me, I am led
to be industrious, and my pursuits in life
though in a humble sphere, give me much
comfort and happiness. My home, or rather
my husband’s house, is my heaven, and 
when I can make it comfortable and neat,
I feel I am improving my heaven.
	It is in this sphere that the faith of those
of my sex is made manifest, for here are
their works seen.
	And how true it is that by their works
or fruits ye shall know them. No one can
hide himself, for all have their sphere to
act in. And therein will their works make
their character manifest.
	Industry in the domestic circle makes
home happy. And I consider that women
require intelligence to guide their efforts
and direct their energies in their sphere as
much as men. I do not know that I can
express the importance which I feel belongs
to their duties when we take in considera-
tion that the infant soul, mind, and body is
committed to their care, I would ask where
is there greater responsibility? I believe
nowhere. And like all other responsibilities
it requires the constant enjoyment of the
Spirit of God to enable us to discharge our
duty faithfully and acceptably. In this ca-
pacity a woman is president. She is called
to act as a physician and a law-giver to her
little flock or charge. She is all this in con-
nection with her husband.
	By woman’s industry a great deal can be
accomplished in the furtherance of the
works of her husband. If she is faithful
and industrious she lightens his load; bears
part of his burden; anticipates his wants and
is truly what was designed to be a help-
mate. I know by past experience, they can
do a great deal by their industry. Many of
us recollect the second year of our settle-
ment here, how eager we were to raise
crops, but this could not be done without
industry. We had brought with us from
St. Louis two hens; from these we raised
60 chickens. Notwithstanding our scarcity,
this I have not been able to do since, though
we have had plenty. We had four quarts of 
potatoes for seed, and by carefulness and
industry we raised from them, 25 bushels of
potatoes. I know I am not the only one
who could tell similar cases of the effects
of industry.
	I pray God to bless us and enable us to
be industrious, that we may fill the measure
of our creation, that in the end our works
may be done and well done to His accept-
ance, in the name of Jesus. Amen.

[Column 1]
	RELIEF SOCIETY REPORTS.
		NEBO STAKE.
	Minutes of Nebo Stake Relief Society
conference held Sept. 19, 1913, at Santaquin
ward, President Eliza J. Dixon presiding.
	After the opening exercises by singing,
Henrietta Teitjen, President of Relief So-
ciety of Santaquin ward, gave a loyal wel-

[Column 2]
come to all who came to conference, and
President Dixon responded to the welcome
given.
	A brief report of the work done in the
last year was given by Councilor Martha S.
Kapple.
	Sister Sarah J. Cannon of the General
Board, addressed the conference. She espe- 
cially urged the sisters to take more interest
in coming to their meetings and to make
their reports read better every year; spoke
of genealogical work; she made many good
and ecouraging remarks.
	Sister Elizabeth S. Wilcock of the Gen-
eral Board addressed the sisters in her pleas-
ing way; said she had known most all the
presidents and leaders of the Relief Societies
throughout the Church. The mission of the
Relief Society is to build people up and help
them, and to be prayerful; also spoke of the
power of the rPiesthood and how we are
blessed every day of our lives by this su-
preme power.
	Bishop Halliday of Santaquin ward said
these Relief Society conferences always
brought some blessing with them, and the
sisters always bring sunshine with their
presence also spoke of many good things
the Relief Society had done in the Santaquin
ward.
	President Dixon gave a short talk.
	Singing, Lord dismiss us with thy bless- 
ing.
	Benediction, by Lars Ahlien.
	Conference adjourned until 1:30 p. m.

President Eliza J. Dixon presiding.
	Singing, Jesus Lover of My Soul.
	Prayer, Bishop Halliday.
	Singing, Count your many blessings.
	Minutes of afternoon session read and
approved.

subject “The Little Things That Count.”
She spoke very emphatically on our duty
to our children.
	Sister Sarah J. Cannon wished to en-
dorse all that had been said by Sister Wil-
cox, and commented on how the Relief So-
society membership when first organized
numbered only 18 members, and now the
membership is 45,000; spoke very emphat-
ically on Temple work and getting work
done for the dead.
	President Eliza J. Dixon wished that the
Relief Society presidents of each ward
would send a representative to join the
Nurses’ Class of Iinstruction held at Salt
Lake City.
	Clara R. Evans felt to thank the Lord for
the many blessings and was so thankful
that the visiting sisters were here with us
today; she also spoke of the temptations of
our young people.
	Brother Jerman felt well paid for coming
to conference, and wished to assist them in
any way.
	President Dixon thanked the good people

[Column 3]
of Santaquin for their kindness and hospi-
tality, and thanked all who had taken part.
	After benediction, conference adjourned
for three months. 
				ELIZA A. HAWKINS,
						Cor. Sec.


THE STAR OF BETHLEHEM.
Wise men discerned a change among the stars 
	In distant lands they had a wonder seen,
	An orb most luminous had come between
	The shining heavenly worlds, what could it
		mean, 
Its light a-streaming through the heavenly
		bars?

But as they watch the star in brilliance grow,
	It moves, and they, to learn its destiny,
	Prepare to follow where so e’er it be,
	To other lands or far beyond the sea, 
The stranger keep in sight where e’er it go.


O’er Bethlehem the guiding star stands still,
	And there in Mary’s arms they find the
		Child;
	Their hearts o’er fill with rapture wild,
	When kneeling round he on them smiled—
This Babe who came to do his Father’s will.

While shepherds wait without the manger door,
	Lo! Eastern kings make princely offering
	Of precious spices, such as camels bring
	Across the desert, to the new born King— 
Significant the gifts those wise men bore

The royal birth declared by angels’ voice,
	Brought love to all mankind, where e’er
		they be,
	Which thrills our hearts e’en though we may
		not see, 
	The blazing star in earth’s fair canopy,
Nor join the shepherds’ song when they re-
		joice.


Our hearts are touched with pathos of Christ’s 
		word;
	“How oft I would have gathered you,” he
		cried,
	And grieved that they companionship denied,
	Rejecting him and from him turn aside.
As if his pleadings they had never heard.

Yet now his natal day brings songs of love,  
	The Christmas bells with happy, noisy chime,
	Ring down the ages to remotest time
	E’er wakening chords of music most sub-
		lime— 
The song the angels sang in vaults above.
						LYDIA D. ALDER.

LIFE IS WORTH THE LIVING.
Oh life is worth  the living, from the cradle to
	the grave,
With all its light and shadow, if the spirit
	is but brave;
For the mystic web it weaveth, touched by
	memory’s magic spell,
Are pictures in soul chambers where the spirit
	loves to dwell.
Oh life is worth the living, for the sunshine
	and the dew,
The diamond dew that sparkles, like a sweet
	thought pure and true,
For the little warbling songbirds—the tree
	tops soaring high,
For the music of the breezes so softly pass-
	ing by.
Oh life is worth the living, for the little
	baby’s smile,
It’s loving touch and prattle, the lingering
	hours beguile;
For the glow and warmth of youth time, life’s
	mystic fairy-land
For the pearls and gems that sparkle along its
	golden strand.
Oh life is worth the life, for fond parental
	love,
Type  of that higher parenthood, in our dear
	home above,
For the silver threads of friendship twining a
	cable strong
For the touch of sweet humanity, in life’s un-
	numbered throng.
Oh life is worth the living, to know God live[-]
	on high,
That the spirit is immortal, and can never
	never die,
To know that His great kingdom, is buil[-]
	upon the Rock,
To stand against the wildest, the tempests’
	fiercest shock.
Oh life is worth the living, for the wrecks
	along the shore.
Where the wild waves dash upon them, and
	the ships return no more;
For they are safely anchored, in the Heaven
	of the blest,
Where the wicked cease from troubling, and
	the weary are at rest.”
			Sarah E. Russell (Hope).


Take joy home, and make a place in thy
great heart for her, and give her time to 
grow, and cherish her. Then will she come, 
and oft will sing to thee when thou art work-
ing in the furrows; aye, or weeding in the
sacred hour of dawn. 
	It is a comely fashion to be glad; joy is
the grace we say to God.—Jean Ingelow.


TO THE RELIEF SOCIETY.
Beloved Sisters:
	As we are now just entering the New
Year, 1914, it seems an appropriate time,
as it certainly is a pleasant duty, to wish
you a happy and prosperous year, coupled
with the hope that you, with us, will try to
make it the very best year that has ever
yet transpired for the Relief Society, as
well as for ourselves, our families and our
kindred.
	We are keenly sensible of and grateful
for your faithfulness and diligence in the
years that are past. But we also know that
we learn by doing; and we believe that
your past experiences have given you such
knowledge of the work that you are now
better qualified than ever before to attain
to excellence in all that pertains to our
great calling. These experiences qualify
you for improvement, in the greater under-
takings that now loom up before the society.
These are of such value and magnitude
that we cannot afford to let them pass.
Rich opportunities are offered for us to put
forth our best efforts; we are encouraged
to strive for the advancement of all that is
noblest and best, and should exert the
utmost diligence in the wide field of spir-
itual culture, thus making sure of the satis-
faction that comes from ministering to those
in need of kindly deeds and the sweet char-
ity that never faileth. The Savior is our
Pattern and Guide; and if we follow His
example and teaching we cannot fail to
meet with His favor and approbation.
	The education our sisters recieve in the
Relief Society is valuable beyond price. It
embraces many things not taught in col-
leges. We know whereof we speak when
we assert that the Relief Society, even back
in the past long before we had the many ad-
vantages we have at present, was a source
of higher education and benefit to those
who were faithful in its service and in the
duties and obligations connected with it,
than any or all other agencies within their
reach. The Relief Society has given to its
members a lofty sense of honor and a true
conception of the duties pertaining to the
activites of life and its value—all in such
sweet simplicity that every one could com-
prehend, through the spirit of our religion,
pure and undefiled. And we maintain that
no school of philosophy could have taught
so successfully as has our society, the science
of true charity and good fellowship to
women of different nations and different
temperaments, or could have created the
love and union that have existed in the so-
ciety throughout the Church of which our
sisters in this organization are for the most
part faithful members.
	We trust our sisters will not think we in-
dulge in a spirit of boasting. We have no
such thought. But having been acquainted
with the movement for many years, and
having personally known and labored with
all the former leaders who championed
Relief Society work from its very begin-
ing, we feel impressed by the dignity of

[Column 2]
this, the first woman’s organization in the
Church, and would like to awaken those
who are indifferent to its significance as a
school of spiritual and moral advancement.
As the years roll on, still greater achieve-
ments will be reached by those who shall
be identified as members with this society.
We feel perfectly sure that this will come
to pass. The assurance of its solidarity is
the harmony that has existed and does ex-
ist between its members in whatsoever land
they may be, or whence they may have come.
The material growth and enterprise mani-
fested during the past year seem in our esti-
mation to have surpassed the showing of any
year in our history. But we must not be
content with the progress made, we must
strive for more. We must each of us seek
for an increase of faith in our Heavenly
Father, and in the Gospel that has been re-
stored in this day and age. We must be
careful of our example in all that pertains
to our conduct and to our conversation and
lives. We must not only look to the ma-
terial well-being of our own households, but
must see to it that in the manners and cus-
toms of our homes and families we may be
examples worthy of imitation. There is
much to look forward to in the coming year.
Let us never lose sight of the high calling
to which we have been chosen, and set apart.
Let us seek to excell in all the virtues that
pertain to our work in charity, benevolence,
purity and uprightness, in our own lives, in
order that we may worthily seek out the
needy and unfortunate and stand firm
as the “Rock of Ages” for the truths
we have embraced--setting an example in
all things great and small, of which we
never need be ashamed.
	And now, beloved sisters, in closing, accept
this expression of our love and commenda-
tion for the great and good work you have
accomplished, not only in the past year, but
in all the years you have labored. Rest as-
sured that you will in no wise lose your re-
ward. May our Father in Heaven bless you
and yours, and prosper your endeavors in
whatever you undertake in the interest of
the society, and the enlightenment and bene-
fit of those with whom you labor.
“Cast your bread upon the waters, and
it will return after many days.”
				Faithfully yours,
		EMMELINE B. WELLS,
		CLARISSA S. WILLIAMS,
		JULINA L. SMITH.


THE NOBEL PRIZE WINNER.
	The Nobel Prize for literature for 1913
was awarded to Rabindra Nath Tagore, a
Bengali poet. The man thus honored is of
a notable Bengal family, famous both for
wealth and intellectual attainments.
	Born in 1860, Tagore’s first few years of
childhood were spent at Calcutta and in the
vastnesses of the Himalayas. At seventeen
he was sent to England, where he thorough-
ly acquainted himself with the English lan-


[Column 1]
guage and English literature. After return-
ing to the Orient he wrote a great deal for
magazines, but though this work held a
strong fascination for him, his remarkable
versatility early marked him as a poet, and
also showed his powers as an educator and a
philosopher as well as a journalist.
	Tagore’s early poetry shows marked ro-
manticism, probably caused by the influence
of the English romanticists, Byron and
Shelley. This sensualism became so great
in some cases that several of his early works
were discountenanced by the more conserv-
ative Bengali. We are told, and this is
probably one of the causes for his extreme
romanticism, that he returned from Europe
distinctly Epicurean; that he lived, for a
time, sumptuously, in the proverbial Ori-
ental magnificence, until the awakening of
his riper, greater powers revoked the sen-
sualist and called forth the higher spirit-
ualist.
	Tagore’s poetry, unlike the works of other
Oriental philosophers in his conception of
the Deity and Eternal love, is still more un-
like the works of any European or Amer-
ican poet. The lyric beauty and utter in-
dividuality of some of his lines reminds one
somewhat of our own Walt Whitman. But 
such high-mindedness, such grace and 
majesty, we find more suggestive of the
works of Dante and Milton. It may be re-
marked here, that judging from the sketches 
and paintings of Tagore, his face bears no
little resemblance to that of the great Italian
master, Dante.
	He is a born poet; a study of his lines re-
veals deep, meditative serenity. His words
are delicate and expressive with a charming
abundance of suggestiveness. His deep pa-
triotism is shown in these lines:
	“To thee, my motherland, I dedicate my
body, for thee I consecrate my life, for thee
my eyes will weep, and in thy praise my
music will sing.
	“Though my arms are helpless and power-
less, still they will do the deeds that can
only serve thy cause; and though my 
sword is rusty with disgrace, still it shall
sever thy chains of bondage sweet mother
of mine.”
	The MacMillan company has recently
published the works of Rabindra Nath Ta-
gore. These include “Gitanjali” (Song
Offerings), a collection of prose translations
made by the author from the original Ben-
gali; “The Gardener” (Poems of Youth) ;
“The Crescent Moon” (Child Poems); and
“Sadhana: The Realization of Life,” a vol-
ume of essays.
	In the following selections from the
Gitanjali we note a Psalm-like dignity, a
rich majesty suggestive of that other Ori-
ental poet, Omar Khayyam, a hint that
these works may be the key that shall un-
lock untold treasures of unwritten Oriental 
literature:

“ *	*	* The onrolling flood of the love
	eternal
Hath at last found its perfect final course.
All the joys and sorrows and longings of the
	heart, 
All the memories of the moments of ecstasy,
All the love lyrics of poets of all climes and
	times
Have come from thee everywhere
And gathered in one single love at thy feet.”
	“Where the mind is without fear and the
head is held high; 
	“Where knowledge is free;

[Column 2]
	“Where the world has not been broken up
into fragments by narrow domestic walls; 
	“Where words come out from the depth of
truth; 
	“Where tireless striving stretches its arms
toward perfection; 
	“Where the clear stream of reason has not 
lost its way into the dreary desert sand of dead
habit; 
	“Where the mind is led forward by thee into
everwidening thought and action—
	“Into that heaven of freedom, my Father, let
my country awake”
							—C. W. C.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
			A SKETCH.
	[The following article was read by the
author before the Utah Woman’s Press
Club, Jan. 7, and by unanimous resolution
of the members present, its publication was
requested in this issue of the EXPONENT.]

	The maples on the mountain sides are
turning golden, the great Artist of the sea-
sons is arraying in a carnival of colors the
landscapes for their autumn holidays, be-
fore they shall be clothed in their winter
robes of snow.
	And this calls to mind the maples of far
away on the rugged, ocean-voiced coast of
the Atlantic, where lie the New England
States, where Prophet and Patriarch, also
many of the first born of the Gospel of Jesus
Christ of Latter-day Saints, were born and
baptized into His Church.
	There, on February 29th, 1828, at Peters-
ham, Worcester County, Massachusetts,
was born Emmeline B. Wells, the subject
of this sketch, amid the grandeur of ocean
and mountain scenes, amid the environ-
ments of patriotism, religious influences,
cultured associations, hereditary endow-
ments and highest ideals of home and wom-
anhood.
	With her spiritual eyes fixed upon the ex-
emplers of her progenitors, and the awak-
ening of new ideals possible in the near fu-
ture, grew to maidenhood this gleaner of
sheaves of knowledge, which should become
her principal and bear interest of intellec-
tual riches and influence in the years to
come; also a queenly place and power in the
kingdom yet to be; that kingdom yet a
dream of the future to all earth’s wisest
scholars and worshippers; a hope and dream,
to all the world, save to the youthful
Prophet—Joseph Smith.
	It was in July, 1859, that I first met Sister
Emmeline B. Wells, at the old Wells House
where the Templeton now stands. At that
time I had no idea that the introduction
meant a future acquaintance and associa-
tion; it seemed merely a social formality,
for in the days when I was a young lady,
such as I, were supposed to profit by listen-
ing to the conversations of their elders.
However, having been acquainted with a
mutual friend, Mrs. Ellen McGary, first in
San Bernardino, California, and later in
Beaver, Utah, who had often spoken of her
old friend Emmeline, her personality, and
abilities, I felt somewhat acquainted, even
at the respectful distance I occupied. So
several years passed by and although I be-
came better acquainted with some of the
daughters of General D. H. Wells, and
their mothers, it was not until Sister Emme-
line B. Wells was called to occupy the edi-
torial chair of the WOMAN’S EXPONENT
that I was drawn nearer to her through my .
labors as secretary of the Y. L. M. I. A.,

[Column 2]
Relief Society, and the semi-monthly meet-
ings in the Fourteenth Ward, and an occa-
sional contribution to the WOMAN’S EXPO-
NENT. 
	From the beginning of our acquaintance
(after it became such) I have felt a partic-
ular special attraction in her personality, her
face and figure (I’ve never seen a photo-
graph or a painted portrait that did her jus-
tice), her type of mind, practical versatility,
her fund of information and her history
with the Church; but—all of these not ex-
pressing certain qualities which make the
woman, I mean as she is to me personally,
I can sum it up best thus: It’s the New
England of it. You may study and under-
stand it, if you are New England born (as
I was: in Boston).
	No matter where you were born, dear
reader, you know that the spirit of your
native land still claims its kinship with you
wherever else you may hereafter dwell, or
what cause you may espouse; and though
the Gospel makes of us all strangers hereto-
fore, one family of one Father.
	When I heard of the accession of Emme-
line B. Wells to the presidency of the Relief
Society of the Church of Jesus Christ of
Latter-day Saints, throughout the world, I
said: That is as it should be. There is no
one else so well qualified to fill that posi-
tion. Sister Wells has been with the Relief
Society as member and later as secretary
(you might say historian) through its up-
ward growth till she knows every branch of
its genealogical tree, and all the fruits it
has borne; she could give you the figures.
	Was she not beside “Aunt Eliza,” “Aunt
Zina,” and “Sister Bathsheba,” ever ready
with her invaluable aid? Did they not know
her thoroughly, her reliability and accuracy
in every detail? Her promptness to serve
and rejoice in serving? And did they not
love her? Did she not share the exodus
with them and bury her dead as they did,
outside their beloved Nauvoo, and traverse
with them that memorable march from civ-
ilization to the shores of the Dead Sea of
America? Fitting it is, that Emmeline B.
Wells should unveil the Sea Gull Monu-
ment, for she was here when they, the
heaven-sent birds, halted the impending
famine.
	Has she not, through the WOMAN’S EX-
PONENT, given voice to her sisters and kept
record of their progress?
	Though her ideal in journalism would
have been a magazine of high character, she
has faithfully labored with the lesser publi-
cation because it was needed, and the “sis-
ters’” resources were limited to this at the
present.
	Some of America’s great men have been
compared to the “mighty oak,” “the tower-
ing pine,” etc.
	Because Emmeline B. Wells was New
England born, and because she was born in
Massachusetts, where, in the days of her
girlhood, the sugar maple was perhaps of
more importance than any other tree (to
the mothers), in my own heart I select the
sugar maple as her emblem. Is it not ap-
propriate?
	Did it not give the sweetening that was
needed to the Puritan housewives? And it
was not passed under government inspec-
tion to prove its purity; and who cared then
what other countries made sugar, or if they
made it cheaper and wanted to send it here


on the “free list” and upset all our home
sugar factories?
	No, each neighbor owned his own sugar
plant on his own farm, and had his “sugar
making” time undisturbed and unlimited in
supply, and what an occasion sugar making
was, and what other sugar or syrup can
taste better? It needed no protecting “trade
mark,” the color and the odor verified its
individuality among the sweets.
	What other tree has such autumn leaves,
that everyone wants to gather and press,
and keep, or to decorate the home with, as
the sugar maple? Of course, other states,
north and south, have the sugar maple, but
it is of the trees of her state and herself that
I am writing.
	Sweetness from its heart in the early
spring time (She was born just before those
camp fires in the woods were lighted, and
men and maidens bore the wooden buckets
of maple sap to the huge kettles that were
kept boiling day and night.)
	Wealth of color and beauty in its falling
leaves in autumn, its latest gift to rich and
poor alike, it is like her. Has she not given
the sweetness of her best years and now, in
the autumn of her life, are not her leaflets
of thought bright and treasured?
	O you artists of gloomy canyons, torrid
deserts, ocean sunsets and moonlights, and
every other kind of picture—who has ever,
who is going to immortalize himself by a
picture of a ‘sugar camp” among the maple
trees and the nut trees, while the snow may
still be seen in the untrampled background?
It is one of America’s chief assets, and dear
to so many hearts. There are the mighty,
protecting forest branches overhead, the fire
lights and the faces, the industry itself with-
out the beauty? destroying smoke stacks and
noise of the machinery, in the primeval for-
est.
	It is grand to have been born there, and
of those ancestors. Blended in her fragile
women is the heritage of those men and
women who counted no sacrifice as too
great for religious and political freedom;
who had braved the rigors of climate, the
Indians and the perils of the Atlantic coast,
for the lives of those who went down to
the sea in ships has been closely interwoven
through the character of all who dwelt in
the New England States.
	Why, then, should she not be the woman
that she is, with the Gospel to lead her? 
	Who among us, though, is better pre-
pared like her, to meet the representative
women of the world, no matter what their
great work or station?
	Emmeline B. Wells, as ambassadress of
the women of Zion, would be bearer of
credentials, the highest that could be be-
stowed, because she is in the service, not of
any earthly potentate, but in the service of
the Church and kingdom of our Father and
His Son Jesus Christ. 
	May her example be a stimulus to the
daughters of Zion to attain unto the call of
the future to each and every one of us in
our place and calling; and may her pres-
ence be among us still for many years to
come.
	With love and respect,
		Your Sister in the Gospel,
		AUGUSTA JOYCE CROCHERON.
	Erda, Oct. 4th, 1913.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
REBECCA E. STANDRING.
	On Friday, the 19th of December, oc-
curred the death of Sister Rebecca E.
Standring, one of the staunch and devoted
women of the Relief Society. While for
some time past Sister Standring has not
been in good health, it was only a month
before her death that she was honorably
released from the position of President of
Alpone Stake Relief Society.
	Sister Standring was born February 20,
1828, at Northampton, England. She was
the daughter of William and Charlotte Ford
Smith, one of eleven children, two brothers
and two sisters now living. She joined the
Church in February, 1851, and emigrated to
America four years later, remaining in New
York and Dayton, Ohio, two years before
coming to Salt Lake City, where she ar-
rived in September, 1857. At the time of
the “move” she went to Springville, and in
1859 was married to Edwin Standring at
Lehi where she has ever since lived.
	At this time Sister Standring became
quite a leader among her associates both in
religious and social circles. She was a mem-
ber of the Lehi choir and the first lady
teacher in the Sunday school of that ward, a
position which she faithfully filled for thirty
years. She was a member of the Home
Dramatic Club and in many roles displayed
marked ability. For ten years Sister
Standring was secretary of the Lehi ward
Relief Society, and in 1879 became ward
president. At the organization of the Al-
pine stake 1901, Sister Standring was elec-
ted to be stake president of the Relief So-
ciety, in which position she was most effi-
cient and much beloved. When the Relief
Society was incorporated in 1891, Sister
Standring was elected on the direstorate and
served in that position until 1901. She at-
tended the business meetings of that body
at her own expense most faithfully, and was
considered a valuable member of the general
board both on account of her good counsel
and executive ability, but owing to the more
frequent meetings and the many calls for
the members she with others living at dis-
tances, were released. She gave her time
and service to the Lord’s work, and was one
of the most devoted, and untiring laborers
in the Relief Society cause. She has worked
for the living and the dead in the St.
George, Manti and Salt Lake temples. Sis-
ter Standring was not blessed with children
of her own, yet she had been a devoted
mother to her adopted children. Many will
always remember her and cherish that mem-
ory in their hearts.
	Her call to labor in higher fields and her
release from earthly chains came at the ad-
vanced age of nearly 86 years after a long
life of loving. The associate members of the
Alpine Stake Relief Society Board pay the
following beautiful tribute.
	During the past twelve years that we have
been working together as a Board, she has
been the guiding spirit of the almost num-
berless efforts for the advancement of the
Sisters in the Alpine Stake. Has she ever
failed in doing her part? ask those with
whom she has labored. Ask those who
have heard her voice raised in defense of 
truth; and prayer to God. We cannot for-
get that she has set before us the noble ex-

[Column 2]
ample of a pure and blameless life, and has
guided her throne with all womanly virtues.
She has shown her excellency in her public
duties, in her religion, and in the advance- 
ment of woman kind. She has on many oc-
casions inspired us to put forth our utmost
efforts to live a nobler and purer life. What-
ever is best in womanhood is exemplified in
her life. We will remember her best for
those qualities which every woman can pos-
sess. It is what one does and what one
really is that counts in the end. The long
and beautiful life of our Dear President,
will ever be a living example for all the sis-
ters who knew her in this Stake of Zion. Her
genuine good qualities, her devotedness to
duty, and her noble example, will be as a
beacon light to us all. No more fitting
words of her life could be quoted than is
expressed in one of the proverbs, "She has
stretched out her hand to the poor, yea
she has reached forth her hand to the needy,
she has opened her mouth with wisdom,
and in her tongue was the law of kind-
ness, she looked well after the ways of her
household, and ate not the bread of the 
idler.
	We, her sisters are delighted to honor
her and her memory for ever. On account
of ill health she was released as President
of the Alpine Stake Relief Society by
the sisters at a conference held at the Amer-
ican Fork First Ward, October 19, 1913.
And was officially released at a quarterly
conference held at Lehi, October 26, 1913.
She passed from this life December 19.
1913, at the age of eighty-five years, nine
months, and twenty-nine days. She leaves
two adopted children, Alice Standring 
Royle, and John Standring. A whole com-
munity and a large circle of friends to
mourn her loss. She died as she had lived, 
a true and stalwart, faithful, Latter-day
Saint. 
	Sister Standring’s funeral was held
Monday December 22, of which event, Mrs.
Julia P. M. Farnsworth furnishes the fol-
lowing:
	Sisters Cannon and Julia P. M. Farns-
worth were appointed by President Wells,
to go to Lehi and attend the funeral of
Mrs. Rebecca Standring, and represent the
General Board. The services were held
in the Lehi tabernacle, and conducted by
Bishop J. H. Gardner of the Second Ward. 
	The singing of the choir was beautiful,
and appropriate. Mrs. Clifford Young and
Mrs. F. F. Kirkham gave solos. There
were many lovely flowers. The speakers
were, President James H. Clark and Able
John Evans of the Alpine Stake presidency,
T. R. Cutler for many years, bishop of
Lehi, Mrs. S. L. Chipman counselor of
Sister Standring read a fine sketch of
Mrs. Standring’s life.
	The building was well filled, and all who
addressed the meeting testified of the noble
life work of Sister Rebecca Standring;
Mrs. Cannon spoke of working with her in
the Salt Lake temple and on the General
Board; Mrs. Farnsworth spoke of her
womanly qualities, as a home maker and
gentle woman, as an efficient director and of
the love and high esteem in which she was
held by our president, Sister Wells and all
her associates, William Southwick, a life
long friend dedicated the grave.
