SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH	-	JANUARY, 1914


HUMANITY VS. MILLINERY.
	To “my lady” who decks her hat in gay
plumage, the following interesting paper on
the above subject, given by Miss Linda
Jessup of this city at a recent gathering of
fashionable women, will be somewhat in-
structive in fact, the subject will doubtless
be of interest and information to all women,
for while Miss Jessup handles the matter of
birds and their plumage for adornment, in
a somewhat humorous vein, yet she does not
fail to show the seriousness of the matter
as well. It is to be hoped that the women
who desire moderation in all things and can
wield an influence for good among their
kind, will heed the lesson taught by the
perusal of this article and use it for hu-
manity’s sake:
	“We have admired and then copied our
distant cousins, living closer to nature than
we, who with the daring of the simple life
appeared at the great ceremonials clad in
nature’s true garments, their birthday
clothes, but topped always by a magnificent
headdress. Not to be outclassed we have
wares that engulf us of Pocohontas trim-
mings, a Zulu top feather, a Navajo war
bonnet, or Black Hawk feather piece.
	“If by some great act of misfortune one
of our number found herself alone on Bird
island, foodless, clotheless, bonnetless, even
as the Indian woman of early tradition,
whom the histories tell us was found on one
of the Santa Barbara channel islands after
a desertion of twenty years, her modern
sister’s ability to fashion a garment from


[Column 1]
birds’ feathers, a needle from a slender
bone, and food from the same source, would
be consistent and applauded around the
world, with a contract drawn for a series
of moving pictures. But only a few of us
can be candidates for a Crusoe existence.
	“We do, however, crave the coverings
that nature has given the natural inhabi-
tants of the Bird islands, and do not scruple
to use every means to transfer that covering
from their bodies for our personal adorn-
ment. If it were only bad for the com-
plexion or caused horrid lines and blemishes
to wear dead birds, like so many scalps,
there might then be an immediate remedy
for this wretched fashion, but the aigrette
is beautiful and becoming in its airy grace,
and so the massacre goes on, or rather, this
statement should be qualified and used in
the past tense. In the passage of the
plumage bill that is a part of the new tariff
law, the ornithologists, agriculturists, scien-
tists and friends in general of the birds with
bright or dull plumage sincerely hope they
see the beginning of the end, but, as our
state game commissioner says all new laws
have to be tried out, it is still too early in
its passage to have noted great results. We
have incurred the wrath of incoming trav-
elers, who, through ignorance of the new
law, or otherwise, have failed to declare
those delicate bits of adornment—the
aigrettes. One opera singer screamed out
her wrath when ordered to give up $1,000
in aigrettes that she had brought to this
country for stage purposes, and was loud in
her denunciation of this free land, suggest-
ing to the custom inspectors that the Statute
of Liberty be removed from its pedestal.
This new law reads:
	“ ‘Aigrettes or other bird plumage, whose
importation is forbidden by the new tariff
law, must be removed from the hats of in-
coming travelers and turned over to the
custom authorities before the travelers
leave the wharf. Secretary McAdoo today
so ruled because of reports that an aigrette
had been snatched from the hat of a woman
at New York.’
	“ ‘That the importation of aigrettes, egret
plumes or ‘osprey’ plumes, and the feathers,
quills, heads, wings, tails, skins or parts of
skins of wild birds, either raw or manu-
factured and not for scientific or educational
purposes, is hereby prohibited ; but this pro-
vision shall not apply to the feathers or
plumes of ostriches, or to the feathers of
domestic fowls of any kind.’ Think what
it will mean for the whole of the United
States by only six lines of national law to
end the disgraceful bird slaughter that is
going on in response to the demands of the
traders in American fashions.
	“Our dictators of fashion declare three
feet of lonesome trimming dangling from
the rear of our head coverings the correct
style for winter, and, with the assurance of
the feminine, we accept the crazy style, ap-
pearing in the street, in places of worship,
the ballroom, yea, even in Mr. Harriman’s
autos, do we assist those public servants in
polishing spots in the car windows. Truly
our sympathy goes out to the long suffering
individual in the crowded car, on being
sweetly addressed by the lady standing in
front of him, with, ‘Does my feather annoy
you,’ This after frequent punctures over
his countenance and a pair of inflamed eyes.
‘O no madam, I cut it off ten minutes ago.’
	“There will come a certain reaction, and
the season of 1914 may find the demure

[Column 2]
Quaker bonnet or even the calico sunbonnet
minus even the tail feathers from the barn-
yard fowl. A little extract from a paper
read at the annual convention of the New
York state federation by Mrs. May Riley
Smith will give a slight idea of the obstinacy
with which the gentle sex has fought against
every advancement by those hoping to save
the remnant of bird life. Extract: ‘Have
you read and did you shiver as we did over
the action of the forestry committee of
women in St. Louis, to whom the state fed-
eration referred the audubon pledge? Utter-
ly blind to, and apparently ignorant of the
economic relations between the insect, the
bird and the tree, which it is supposed to be
the object of a forestry committee to pre-
serve, the secretary civilly remarked: ‘I
must refuse to subscribe to that sort of
thing, because if I want to wear an aigrette
I shall certainly do so, and my conscience
won’t hurt me a particle.’ And the chair-
man agreed that ‘We must refuse to ad-
vocate the pledge, for I intend to wear one
of those very things on my hat.’ And the
newspapers that chronicled this decision
added: ‘The clubwomen of St. Louis will
not uphold the forestry committee in this
action.’
	“It is the manner of this decision, the
defiance of public sentiment by women that
hurts most in this occurrence. It was a
happy moment to turn to the delightful re-
port of ‘The Bird Masque’ given the latter
part of the summer in New Hampshire near
President Wilson’s summer home. Great
interest was aroused, not alone because of
the interest and principal parts taken by the
President’s daughters, but the beauty of the
play, its settings, out of doors and the pop-
ularity of its author, Percy Mackaye.
	“The case is far more serious than the
world at large suspects. But for the profits
the birds would be unmolested, and no un-
protected wild species can long escape the
demands of commerce. From the trackless
jungles of New Guinea, round the world
both ways, to the snow-capped peaks of the
Andes no unprotected bird is safe. Amer-
ca’s share in the destruction of the birds
for adornment purposes is 150,000,000—
Europe the same, and England 30,000,000
yearly. Millions each year!
	“Do you not marvel that Uncle Sam sat so
comfortably in his big arm chair with a bird
on one arm (and we, as a nation, not even
holding that bird sacred), complacently
waving the stars and stripes with the other
hand over his almost birdless fields? The
brilliancy of the plumage or the brightness
of the song does not argue that the feath-
ered creature is useless.
	“One pair of rose breasted grosbeaks can,
in a week, destroy all of the potato bugs on
an acre of potatoes and then keep the vines
clean for an entire season.
	“Whereas, two boys with a bucket of
paris green at a salary of one cent for every
hundred slain bugs, cannot even approxi-
mate that record. Chemistry has brought
its past breakers to great proficiency, but the
insects continue to work too insidiously and
too quickly for man adequately to cope with
them. It is for you to remember, when you
come to select your new hat, that the insect
rises as the bird falls; $200,000,000 losses
to agricultural interest is the record of the
insects in the United States, and this does
not include damage done to ornamental
shrubbery, shade and forest trees.
	“The robin has also been marked for

[Column 3]
wholesale slaughter. One small town in
Tennessee sends each year to the market
enough to bring in $500, with the price at
5 cents a dozen—125,000 robins. The fact
that a particular bird is not wanted for the
time being, is no proof of its safety. As in
the case of the grebe, it may suddenly be
declared fashionable, and the grebe was
killed off by the thousands around the lakes
in Oregon.
	“Some birds are brought into the market
as an experiment. They are killed, not be-
cause there is a demand for them, but on the
chance that the demand may be created
This shows the danger in which every spe-
cies of finely plumaged bird stands until
legislation interferes. The question that
thousands of working people would be
thrown out of employment if the fancy
feather trade were taken from them has no
foundation.
	“This material gives less labor to the
working class than probably any other kind
of trimming that could be or would be em-
ployed in its place. The profit does not go
to pay the wages of a large number of
hands. It goes to the few firms who con-
duct the business. When the wild birds can
no longer be obtained, ostrich and poultry
feathers remain, and there can be no doubt
that the use of artificial flowers and berries
and of ribbon and fancy ornaments would
increase and give more employment in the
labor market than is now given by the wild
bird plumage. Mr. McClain, in his speech
before the senate in August, refers to the
persistency with which the milliners’ asso-
ciation circulated the idea of the artificial
osprey or egret plume. The feathers were
no longer to be labeled real. Assistants were
instructed to assure lady customers that
these delicate sprays were manufactured by
the millions out of quills, horse hair, and
other material, by an army of workers who
earned their living by this pleasant and ar-
tistic work.
	“The fraud flourished until 1908, when it
became evident that the force of mere repe-
tition which had proved so successful with
an uncritical public would not stand investi-
gation before a serious tribunal.
	“The invention of artificial ospreys was
discarded for that of molted feathers. The
sprays were admitted to be real, but it was
no longer cruel to wear real plumes. They
had simply been picked out of the nests
after the birds had raised their young and
left them. That is a myth. A few torn,
badly frayed plumes are occasionallly picked
up from the swampy breeding grounds that
have been shed after nesting, but these are
too poor to command any price. These
plumes or aigrettes are in effect the mar-
riage wing of the snowy heron, or egret.
They are sometimes called ‘the love feathers’
of the birds. The story of the aigrette trade
deals with the slaughter of the innocents by
the slow process of starvation, a method
which history shows has never been followed
by even the most savage race of men deal-
ing with their most hated enemies.
	“This war of extermination which has
been carried forward unchecked for years
could mean but one thing, namely, the rapid
disappearance of the egrets of the United
States. As nesting birds they have disap-
peared from New Jersey, Maryland, Vir-
ginia and also those states of the Mississippi
valley where at one time they were to be
found in great numbers. The efforts for the 
preservation of the egrets and herons are


[Column 1]
now being pushed with greater vigor than
ever before. These aigrettes, torn from the
back of the bird during the nesting season
only, now sell for twice their weight in gold.
From $32 to $80 an ounce. Remember, one
aigrette costs five lives—the mother bird
and the four nestlings. Robbing hen
roosts grades as a respectable occupation
compared with plume hunting. One
commercial plume hunter boasted of hav-
ing killed 125,000 birds. All the collec-
tions in this country, caged and stuffed, do
not count as many skins as are sold for mil-
linery in one London auction, and there are
several auctions in a year. The imports of
millinery feathers to this country alone are
valued at $11,000,000, while the valuation of
the diamond imports at the last census was
about $12,000,000.
	“In the southern part of our country a
few years ago there must have been 500
varieties of our daintiest bits of animate
feathered life, the humming bird. Only a
few varieties are seen now. Many winter in
Cuba or Mexico and along our western
coast. In Cuba, as well as in the United
States, the humming birds have been killed
off by the thousands to swell the London
feather trade. They sell from a fraction of
a cent to 2 cents apiece, and some unfortu-
nate with $10,000 too much money for his
own good paid that price recently in Paris
for a cloak made of humming-bird skins.
	“The King of the Cannibal Isles who re-
quested that humming birds’ tongues on
toast be served him for breakfast had noth-
ing over the wearer of the cloak. The an-
cient Mexicans looked upon the humming
bird as emblems of the soul, as the Greeks
did the butterfly, and held that the spirits
of their warriors who had died in defense
of their religion were transformed into these
most brilliant of living creatures, and yet
in later years these birds were marked out
for such slaughter. In Oregon only a short
time ago there were more Chinese pheasants
than there were in China itself, and yet in
Oregon recently 50,000 of these pheasants
were killed in a single day. The hunter has
pronounced the doom of the woodchuck,
and the milliner has sentenced the bluebird.
The passenger pigeon, once so plentiful, is
now absolutely extinct. If the rumor that
an Oregon university professor has discov-
ered the nesting places of a group of these
birds in her state be true, there will be great
rejoicing. The one live specimen in the Cin-
cinnati zoo died last summer. They used to
be butchered in their breeding places by the
wagon loads. It afforded plumage and
made rare sport.
	“On the Bahama islands and in Florida
were formerly large rookeries of the flamin-
go which are now nearly extinct, and great
numbers of the white ibis, which shared in
the popularity of its delicate plumage with
the egret.
	“If only we had had sufficient sentiment
to hold this bird sacred as the early Egyp-
tions did! Two thousand years ago it was
to commit murder to kill one of these birds
in Egypt. They symbolized the soul of a
departed.
	“This superstition of the Egyptians was
so strong that burial grounds were laid out
for the ibis, their bodies were embalmed,
wrapped in costly wrappings and placed in
pottery coffins. The jackal shared in the
reverence with the bird, as he was delegated
to accompany the soul of the departed on its
journey across the great desert to the final

[Column 2]
oasis, the jackal being the only creature
capable of finding its way across that great
trackless plain. Before the ibis and the
jackal start out across the desert, the soul
of the departed is weighed against an os-
trich feather, and if not found wanting in
the balance he is permitted to start on his
hunt for the oasis.
	“The government of India made the ex-
port of skins and plumes from India illegal,
and yet naturalists and others interested in
the matter saw that in spite of this prohibi-
tion the feathers of the birds peculiar to the
East Indies continued to be offered for sale
in London.
	“It was then learned that many cases were
imported as cow hair or horse hair, a layer
of horse hair covering perhaps thousands
of skins.
	“Yet back of the feather merchant and
plume hunter, who supply the skins, are the
men who make a fashion for women and
then impose it upon them subtly, irresistibly
	“The rebellion of Audubonists against
wanton waste of the prettiest thing in nature
ought to begin, it would seem, not in the
rookeries, not in boudoirs, not in milliners’
shops, not in feather dealers’ warehouses,
but in those lofts in Paris where modes of
headgear are designed and, by authority
more autocratic than a czar’s, more respect-
ed than a pope’s, are enjoined upon a civil-
ized woman as ‘the latest things.’
	“Who did kill Cock Robin?	Nobody
clamors for the distinction:
		“‘Not I,’ said the plumer,	
		‘It’s nothing but rumor.’
		‘Not I,’ said the birdman,
		‘I’m half brother to Pan.’
		‘Not I,’ said the seller,
		‘It’s some brutal feller.”
		‘Not I,’ said the hatter,
		‘See Paris in the matter.’
		‘Not I,’ said the cat,
		‘Would I ever do that?’
		‘Not I’ said the collector,
		‘I’m partly protector.’
		‘Not I,’ said the dame,
		‘’Twas dead when it came.’”


PRESIDENT SMITH’S VISIT TO ARI-
			ZONA.
	The recent visit of President Smith and
party to the stakes in Arizona was a most
delightful occasion to the people of that part
of Zion. One purpose of the visit was the
dedication of the twin chapels at Mesa, but
many other matters of interest were at-
tended to.
	There are many of the Saints who were
forced to leave their homes in Mexico
among the people visited and to them the
wise counsel and comforting words of their
loved leader was much appreciated. A most
touching and beautiful incident of the trip
was when seventy-five children passed in
line and each one presented the President
with a rose—one for each year of his life
—one little girl saying, “for our dear
Prophet, because we love you.”
	Accompanying President Smith were
Mrs. Smith and two of the President’s
daughters, President Anthon H. Lund, Pre-
siding Bishop C. W. Nibley and Mrs. Nib-
ley, and Elder George A. Smith of the
Council of the Twelve. Later the party
was joined by President Francis M. Lyman
and Elder Joseph F. Smith, Jr., of the
Council of the Twelve, and Superintendent
Horace Cummings of the Church schools.
	The visit of the Church authorities was
a rare treat to the Saints in Arizona and
one long to be remembered. The assem-
blies both at stake conferences and schools
were so large that many could not gain en-
trance. That much good will accrue from
this visit is certain.


ELIZA R. SNOW.
	On the 21st of the current month occurs
the 110th anniversary of the birth of that
great and honored woman, Eliza Roxy
Snow Smith. She was a peer among women,
and as a leader of the women of Zion may
her name be ever remembered.
	Like Miriam of old she cheered the wan-
dering children of God with songs along the
dreary journeying way and lent aid, com-
fort and counsel to the faint-hearted and
the weary.
	With great fortitude and mastery of self
she forsook bright prospects of literary and
social attainments, and cast her lot with a
people persecuted because of their faith.
Yet in the hearts of that people she found a
place of refuge. Her hymns sung in the con-


gregations of the Saints have immortalized
her name among the younger generations,
still we do not forget that her greatest work
was achieved, not so much through the ex-
ercise of her loved gift as in the broader
field of organization. From north to south,
from east to west, she traveled; not in the
ease of Pullman cars, as we today may go,
but in all kinds of vehicles and every kind
of weather, organizing branches of the Re-
lief Society, and teaching words of truth and
wisdom to the women of Zion.
	The women of the Relief Society should
sometimes recall the names and works of
those great women of the past to whom so
much is due.


[Column 1]
		SARAH E. RUSSELL.
	The harvest of the Great Reaper this
past year o’erflows with names of the great
and worthy. With sighs and tears we scan
the long roll, noting here and there a friend
and co-laborer in the great work of the
kingdom of God. It may be in different
lines, some as educators, some ministers,
some social service workers, others writers
and poets and among the last, we read the
name of Sarah E. Russell known to the
many readers of the Exponent as “Hope.”
Her beautiful poems have graced the pages
of this paper for many years and like the
soul of the author, breathe simplicity, hu-
mility, love, forbearance, charity and faith.
	The many friends of Miss Russell were
shocked to learn of her demise which oc-
curred Sunday the 28th of December, at
3 o’clock in Tucson, Arizona. One thing
which makes the death seem particularly
sad is the fact that in all probability, Miss
Russell was one of the victims of the Mexi-
can exodus. She, with her family were
very reluctant to leave their homes in Mex-
ico and notwithstanding the many and ur-
gent requests from friends and relatives in
Utah to give up their Mexican holdings
and come back they positively refused to
leave until forced to do so by the Mexican
outrages, only then going into Arizona with
full purpose of returning to Mexico when
conditions became more settled.
	Sarah E. Russell was the daughter of
Isaac and Mary Walton Russell, and was
born at Far West, Missouri, Aug. 12, 1840.
The family of Isaac Russell, with those of
John Taylor and Joseph Fielding, were con-
verted to the Church by Parley P. Pratt, in
the spring of 1836. John Taylor met Parley
P. Pratt at the home of Isabella Russell
Walton a sister of Isaac R. Russell, in Tor-
onto, and later, Mrs. Walton took Elder
Pratt to her brother Isaac, who resided on
his farm at Charletton’s settlement, now
Downsview, a few miles northwest of Tor-
onto. Isaac Russell and Joseph Fielding
owned adjoining farms in this village. They
both joined the Church under the ministry
of Elder Pratt, and were among the first
missionaries to Great Britain in the follow-
ing year, 1837, when the British mission
was opened at Preston, where Joseph Field-
ing had relatives. The first public sermon
in England was preached at the Cockpit at
Preston in July on the first Sunday after the
arrival of the elders in Great Britain. The
first baptisms were performed in the river
Ribble at Preston. Many converts were
made, and the Church was established in
the British Isles.

[Column 2]
	Isaac Russell’s family resided at Kirtland
during his absence and upon his return,
joined the Saints at Far West, where Sarah
E. Russell was born. The father died at
Richmond, Missouri, in 1884, at the age of
39, leaving his widow, Mary Walton and
six young children. The family remained
at Richmond until 1861, when they came
to Utah and settled in the Fifteenth ward of
Salt Lake City, where Sarah E. Russell
formed many, dear and lasting friendships.
In 1882, Miss. Russell accompanied her
younger sister, Isabella M. Johnson, to
Mesa, Arizona, and thence to Mexico. They
were residing in the valley of Chuichupa,
when the present revolution in Mexico com-
pelled them to abandon their homes, and they
came as refugees into Arizona, where they
have since resided.
	Sarah E. Russell is survived by her
youngest sister Isabelle M. Johnson, who
was with her at the hour of her death and
had been her inseparable companion in life;
and by the children of her deceased broth-
ers and sisters, George W. Russell, Mary
W. Gray, Samuel Russell and Ann Jane
Howley.
	It is the regret of her many friends in
this city that they have been separated
from her in the latter years of her life and
they could not be near her in the hour of
her departure. To them she will be of hal-
lowed memory.
	The last poem written by Miss Russell
(Hope) for the Exponent was descriptive
of the condition of the Mexican refugees
and most pathetic, however, one of her
most inspired poems we think is the one
on the front page of the present number,
which we reprint from an early paper so
like it is to her, whom we now mourn.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


IN SICKNESS.
	It is quite surprising how few young
women avail themselves of the opportunity
to learn anything about the care of the sick
and yet most of them expect to become
mothers and have the care and training of
little children. Probably ignorance has been
the cause of more suffering and death among
children than disease, and in this day there
is little excuse for such ignorance. Nearly
all the magazines contain articles for young
mothers and from the last number of
“Woman’s Magazine’’ we cull a few instruc-
tions prepared by a trained nurse. She
writes:
	“The dangers of contagious disease are
much increased by possible complications
and the serious effects that often follow. 
The early symptoms of many contagious dis- 
eases are those of a common cold and people
are thus exposed unawares. Children should
be taught never to kiss persons suffering
from colds, never to use the handkerchief
of another nor to drink from a common cup
or glass.
	“Should a child succumb to contagion,
isolate it in a large, well ventilated room,
free from draughts and accessible to sun-
shine, place the bed out in the room shaded


from strong lights and facing away from the
window.
	“Chicken pox appears about two weeks
after exposure, usually with slight fever.
There is an eruption within the first twenty-
four hours of crops of reddish pimples,
thickest on the back, which become blisters
in a day or so and scale off in a week.
	“Keep the child quiet in bed on a light
diet while the fever lasts and isolated until
the last scale has fallen. Inflamed eyes,
which often accompany this disease as well
as measles, should be guarded from bright
light and relieved by cold compresses. Ex-
posure after the disease often results in se-
vere bronchitis or pneumonia.
	“Measles develop about ten to fourteen
days after exposure, are very contagious,
and may be carried in the clothes.
	“The disease begins with red weeping
eyes, nasal discharge, dry cough and all the
symptoms of a severe cold. It is accom-
panied by fever, loss of appetite and on the
third or fourth day irregular patches of
small dark red raised spots appear first on
face, neck and arms, and later in trunk and
legs. There is also itching of the skin espe-
cially during peeling.
	“The child should be kept in bed until the
fever is over and the eyes carefully guarded
from strong light and bathe them three
times daily with boric solution. Cleanse the
nose and throat from discharges and do not
permit mouth breathing. The bowels should
be kept open, as the disease is partly thrown
off in that way.
	“The diet for a baby should be milk, for
older children fluids during the fever and
semi-solids later on.
	“Even the mildest cases should be rigidly
cared for, since eye, ear, nose and throat
troubles often complicate or follow measles.
It is advisable whenever a rash appears to
call in a physician or some one who can
accurately diagnose the case.”


NOTES ABOUT WOMEN.
	Mrs. Lillie Devereaux Blake, a pioneer in
the cause of woman suffrage, died in a san-
itarium at Englewood, N. J., December 30,
1913, aged 80 years. She had been ill for
several years, and recently had a turn for
the worse, when it was seen that because
of her advanced age her recovery was hope-
less.
	Mrs. Blake was the author of several suc-
cessful novels in her earlier years, but de-
voted the greater part of her life to cham-
pioning the cause of woman’s rights. She
was born in Raleigh, N. C.
	Another woman Mayor has been elected
in Oregon. A dispatch from Troutville
says that Mrs. Clara Larrson has been
chosen to that office by a majority of five
over the man who opposed her.	
	Oregon’s other woman Mayor is Miss
Clara Munson of Warrenton, who was
elected by the Citizens’ ticket last year.
	The town of Kanab, Utah, has a woman
mayor and the city council is composed en-
tirely of women. The executive ability of
women is gradually being recognized not
only in the suffrage states but the recent
appointments of women to Federal positions
shows that President Wilson realized, the
fact that women of the nation should
receive some public recognition.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


BINGEN ON THE RHINE.
		(A Story Re-told.)
O Bingen, storied Bingen, a favored lot is
	thine,
There nestling by the mountains beside the
	glorious Rhine;
Where maidens oft were weeping and sighing
	“Love of mine,
Come back, come back to Bingen,” fair Bingen
	on the Rhine.

And tearful mothers praying, still say “Thy.
	will not mine,”
Though far away is sleeping the soldier of the
	Rhine.
The dying eyes fast closing saw where the
	sunbeams shine,
On that dear home in Bingen, loved Bingen
	on the Rhine.

Escapes a sigh, a heart throb, I cannot but
	repine;
I see that soldier dying who babbles of the
	Rhine,
And happy days of childhood, loves of that
	earlier time;
When fading was the picture of Bingen on the 
	Rhine.

The battle long hath ended, sad memories
	round it twine,
But ne’er will be forgotten the soldier of the
	Rhine.
He marched away in triumph, fast stepping, 
	straight in line,
But ne’er returned to Bingen, the home be-
	side the Rhine.

And other hearts have broken, crushed by the
	woes of Time
That in the hour of passing sighed “Bingen
	on the Rhine.”
O peaceful is thy beauty when sun, or moon-
	beams shine
Appears as scene elysian the gently flowing
	Rhine.

So in life’s closing moments the heart doth
	ever pine,
For childhood days in Bingen, fair Bingen on
	the Rhine;
Soft evening shades are falling, the sun’s rays
	fast decline,
But loath I leave thee Bingen, O Bingen on
	the Rhine.

There is a something whispers as if a breath
	divine,
The tales of love and parting beside the witch-
	ing Rhine;
A[-] rapture strange, mysterious, o’er fills this
	heart of mine,
And tear drops fall unbidden while sailing on 
	the Rhine.

There comes a sadness o’er me, and thoughts
	none may define,
Not known before beholding the grandeur of
	the Rhine;
And in the days far coming, will memory round
	it twine—
Bring dreams forever cherished, I dreamed be-
	side the Rhine. 
					LYDIA D. ALDER.

MADAME BERNHARDT HONORED.
	From Paris comes the news that at last
the name of Sarah Bernhardt has been
added to the roll of fame of the Legion of
Honor. Her claims have been put for-
ward several times but the influence of
 a strong element in French society pre-
vented the French actress from obtaining
the decoration.
	Among the famous women who are pos-
sessors of the red ribbon are Adelina Patti,
Mme. Curie, the Baroness de Rothschild,
Mme. Paquin, and Mme. Dutrieu; the
aviator.


MARY E. LEIGHTNER.
	Another noble and true woman whose
death it is our sad duty to chronicle is Sis-
ter Mary E. Leightner, who died Dec. 17,
1913.
	Sister Leightner was born April 9, 1818,
in Lima, New York. She joined the Church
of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints in the
fall of 1830, just six months after the
Church was organized, or at the time Oli-
ver Cowdery, Peter Whitmer and Zeba Pe-
terson came to Kirtland, Ohio,  and estab-
lished a branch of the Church. Father Mor-
ley was ordained an elder to preside over
this branch of the Church.
	Sister Leightner was one of those chosen
spirits who seemed called for the special
purpose to help in the building up of the
kingdom of God upon the earth. She
passed through all the trials and persecu-
tions of the Saints in Missouri and Illinois,
and with unfailing faith endured many
hardships. She was sealed to the Prophet
Joseph Smith before his death.
	Sister Leightner moved from Salt Lake
City to Minersville, Beaver county, Utah, in
1863, where she made her home until the
time of her death. Her funeral was large-
ly attended by friends who honored and
loved her good and noble work.
	At her funeral letters of condolence were
read from President Joseph F. Smith and
Stake President J. F. Tolton. There were
present at the funeral, twenty-one persons
who had attended school as students of
Sister Leightner. Bishop Marshall, Elders
Myers, Banks, Lee and Wood, also Sister
Sarah B. Gillins, spoke words of consolation
and told of the faithful and unselfish life
of the good woman. She lived a life it
would be well to emulate. So, one by one
are passing from earth those who knew
the Prophet Joseph Smith, and whose tes-
timonies of him and his mission are so
treasured by the Saints.


[Column 1]
	RELIEF SOCIETY REPORTS.
		BOX ELDER STAKE.
	The Relief Society Conference of Box
Elder Stake held at Willard, Nov. 15th,
1913.
	President Sarah P. Stohl presided.
	Present, besides Relief Society Stake of-
ficers: President Oleen N. Stohl, Counselor
W. C. Horsley, Bros. Burt, Cook, and
Facer of the High Council. Bishops Wright
and Hubbard and several brethren from
Willard.
	President Sarah Stohl announced this
conference was held in honor of two of our
old sisters. Sister Nancy Whitaker, a mem-
ber of the first organization at Nauvoo, and
Sister Bathia Wells, a daughter of Brother
Elijah Fordham. Said this morning’s ses-
sion would be in honor of the Prophet Jo-
seph Smith.
	Opened with singing, “Praise to the
Man,” etc.
	Opening prayer, Bishop Brigham Wright.
	Singing, “Oh, give me back my Prophet
Dear.”
	Minutes of previous forenoon conference
read and accepted.
	President Stohl was pleased to see such
a good crowd out this morning and that
the weather was so favorable; was pleased
to have the privilege of holding our confer-
ence here at Willard, and felt to appreciate

[Column 2]
the kindness and thank the sisters for their
invitation; appreciated the sisters of the
Stake Board and the work of the local of-
ficers; said we had held district conferences
in some of the wards and felt the work
was going on.
	Sister Mary Frodsham gave the 44th
chapter of the life of the Prophet, which
treats on the miraculous healing of Elijah
Fordham and others by the Prophet.
	Sister Bathia Wells, a daughter of Elijah
Fordham, bore her testimony, and gave in
detail the incident of the healing of her
father by the Prophet, said she knew the
Prophet, saw him taken to prison, and saw
his body when brought from Carthage, was
at the meeting, when the mantle of the
Prophet fell on Brigham Young, was thank-
ful to see this day, and if her testimony was
any good to strengthen the testimony of
any one, she wished to be of service;
thanked the Lord for her faith, and for His
blessings to her, and hoped she might ever
be found trying to serve the Lord.
	Brother George Harding had seen the
Prophet Joseph and remembered him quite
well; told how they used to hold meetings
in the grove, at Nauvoo; said the most im-
pressive things to him in connection with
the Prophet, was taking a message to him at
his home, and the fatherly talk, which he
gave him; spoke of his own baptism and
confirmation by Hyrum Smith; related an
incident just before the death of the Proph-
et. Bore his testimony that Joseph Smith
was a Prophet of God, and hoped we would
stand firm and steadfast to the principle
taught by him.
	The hymn, “The Seer,” was rendered by
Sister Lottie Owens.
	Sister Nancy Whitaker, one of the sis-
ters who belonged to the first Relief So-
ciety organized at Nauvoo. She told of
the work of the organization and the sew-
ing done, but on account of being rather
feeble, her age being nearly 94, her grand-
daughter, Martha Woodland, read her testi-
mony, which she had previously written
down, as she had told it.
	President Oleen N. Stohl had been very
interested in this meeting, and could not
help but say Joseph Smith was a prophet
of God; bore his testimony to the same; said
he was glad to say that those who would
accept the Gospel would have thep rivilege
of bearing this testimony; said if we have
faith and can live the Gospel we can have
the privilege given us to do all the things
required of us; spoke on the growth of this
Church, especially in the organization of
the Relief Society, and the inspiration of
Joseph Smith to organize it, said if you
want to be happy, do something in the
Church, and if you don’t accept this mis-
sion you will never feel good; encouraged
all to work and know for themselves that
this gospel is true; spoke on tithing and
keeping the Sabbath day holy, and other
laws of God, and the blessings received by
keeping them; said, Let us work for the
service of God each day, and put the best
we have into it. Bore a strong testimony
to the power of God in his own behalf, and
felt he could not appreciate the blessings of
the Lord enough. God bless you, my sis-
ters; I feel your hearts are in the work.
	President Sarah Stohl was thankful for
the presence of so many of the brethren;
said the afternoon session would be devoted
to Sister Eliza R. Snow Smith.


Sister Agnes Owens, president of Willard
Relief Society, felt to welcome all, and
wished all to stay to lunch.
	Singing, Doxology.
	Prayer, Brother Wm. Facer.
	Afternoon Session.
	Singing, ‘I’ll Serve the Lord while I am
Young.”
	Opening prayer, Sister Jane Owens.
	Sister Lula B. Call read and explained
the song, “Oh, my Father,” which was af-
terwards sung by the congregation.
	Roll call showing 260 members present,
the stake board answering, by sentiments
from Sister Snow’s poems and sayings.
Minutes of previous afternoon conference
read and approved.
	Sister Stohl hoped the same good spirit
which had been with us this morning would
stay with us this afternoon; wished to thank
the sisters of Willard for the splendid lunch,
and pleasant noon hour.
	Sister Rachel Evans gave a sketch of the
life of President Eliza R. Snow Smith.
	The hymn, “The trials of the present day,”
was sung as a duet by Sisters Eliza White
and Alice Nebeker.
	Five minute reports were given, on sew-
ing done, nights spent with sick, quilts
made, means gathered for wheat, and other
items for six months, by the secretaries of
the following associations: Willard, Perry,
Harper, Third and Fourth Wards of Brig-
ham City.
	The “Hymn, “The Lord has imparted
from above,” was sung as a solo, by Sister
Della Nuttal.
	Reports continued, Brigham City First
and Second Wards, Bear River City and
Honeyville.
	Sister Mosiah Evans of the Bear River
Stake Relief Society, was pleased to be
here today, and visit with us in the confer-
ence; said she had heard much today that
would increase her faith; thought the tes-
timonies of those old sisters was a great tes-
timony to us; felt we ought to be so thank-
ful that we belong to the Relief Society, and
may we be inspired to go on and lead bet-
ter lives was the blessing she asked for all.
	Bishop Jos. Hubbard said it had been a
day of rejoicing to him, in listening to the
program and the testimonies of the sisters.
Felt it an honor to have this conference
held here and felt to extend a glad wel-
come to all; felt this conference would do
much good in helping to create interest in
this work.
	President Stohl was sorry the time was
so short, as we could not hear from all we
would have liked to hear from; said Presi-
dent Stohl wished her to express his appre-
ciation for the welcome extended to us;
was pleased with the reports, but felt there
was room to do better. Let us put forth our
very best efforts to further this work.
Wished again to thank the sisters and may
the Lord bless all.
	Singing, “Truth Reflects upon Our
Senses.”
	Benediction, Patriarch Edwin Cordon.
		SARAH P. STOHL, President.
		ANNIE H. LITTLEWOOD, Secretary.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


OBITUARY.
	Resolutions of respect to Sister May Ash-
worth-Booth, passed by the Relief Society
Board of Utah stake, of which she was sec-
retary:


Whereas, Our Heavenly Father has, in His
infinite wisdom, taken from our midst our es-
teemed and dearly loved sister, May Ashworth-
Booth; and,
	Whereas, The intimate relation held during
a long period of time, by her, with the mem-
bers of this Board, makes it fitting that we re-
cord our appreciation of her; therefore,
	Resolved, That the wisdom and ability which
she has exercised in aid of our work by counsel
and service, will be held in grateful remem-
brance;
	Resolved, That the removal of our dear sister
from our midst leaves a vacancy and a shadow
that will be deeply realized by all members of
the Board and its friends;
	Resolved, That with deep sympathy with the
afflicted relatives and friends of the departed,
we express an earnest hope, that even so great
a bereavement, may be overruled for their
highest good.
	That we unite our sentiments with our loved
sister Aretta Young, in her following tribute:

	Oh, Friend:
Thou hast too early gone to thy long rest:
Dear one, good-bye;
We would have kept thee,
(’Twas our hearts’ desire),
Within our domain here,
To make the world more dear,
More rich in friendship’s great bequest;
But thou didst tire
’Neath mortal pain and weariness,
And God hath called thee to thy rest:
	Beloved, farewell!

In grief our hearts are still,
He knoweth best, dear heart,
Bowed ’neath the Father’s will;
Why we should part;
And since thou'st passed the "Silence drear,”
Death’s gloom hath less of awesome fear—
The lonely pathway more of light;
Sweet friend,  good night.

	Sister Booth was born September 28,
1874, to Wm. and Mary E. Ashworth, in
Beaver, Utah. She graduated from the B.
Y. A. (now U.) in 1895; was married to
A. L. Booth of Provo in Salt Lake Temple,
in June, 1900; gave birth to five children,
three of which she laid away at birth. She
is survived by husband, two little daughters,
father, mother, six brothers, four sisters,
many other relatives and friends. She was
always a faithful worker in the Church.
	Died, Oct. 10, 1913, Provo, Utah. 
				ALICE T. BLAKE,
				EUNICE S. HARRIS,
				SARAH HOLDAWAY,
							Committee.


NOTES AND NEWS.
	A band of First Settlers, in Quaker dress,
will march in the parade which the Pennsyl-
vania suffragists are planning to hold in
Philadelphia. The Friends were probably
the earliest of modern religious denomina-
tions to give women equal rights in the
church—or rather in the meeting house—
and in colonial days Hannah Penn for some
time acted as Governor of Pennsylvania.


MADAME DAVENPORT, AGE 90, AD
	DRESSES LADIES’ CLUB.
	The absolute demonstration of a new out-
look for the human race was made re-
cently at the Ladies’ Literary club in
Salt Lake City, when Madame Charlotte
de Goliere Davenport personally illus-
trated the possibility of retaining at
90 years of age the appearance, viva-
city, physical elasticity and mental vigor,
not of youth, yet of the best of an early
prime. Madame Davenport, claiming to be
in her ninetieth year, and the mother of 24
children, appears hardly 50, and moves,
speaks and talks with the charm of a girl
of 20 years.
	Her points were made with a freshness
and wit which kept her audience bubbling
with laughter at times, and interested and
enthusiastic throughout.
	“Many people are sick,” said the speaker,
“because they are too lazy to take the trou-
ble to be well, or because they selfishly cling
to their ailments, loving them too well to let
go.” She illustrated her belief with many
interesting incidents, and continued: “There
is but one class of people for whom I have
a contempt—it is the sick. I have helped
to cure many people by showing them they
should be ashamed of their ailments instead
of exploiting and boasting of them. That
is what I call complaining—it is boasting—
and by it the sick build up what they pre-
tend to want to get rid of.”
	Madame Davenport is on her way to
California to teach a class of 75 women
“how to take care of themselves.” She has
been eight times around the world; served
38 years as private teacher of hygiene to
the empress of Austria, and now holds a
pension given her by the emperor Franz
Joseph. It is at periods like that in which
she measures her life: “I taught 25 years,”
at such a place: “I studied that science for
65 years,” and so on. Madame Davenport
states that she has a son 71 years of age,
one of 11 sons out of the 24 who are still
living. She remained in the city a week,
and it was hoped would address the young
girls, in whom she declares she is especially
interested.

SORROW AND TEARS.
Out of my sorrow and mourning,
	Out of its grief and its pain;
Its sighing and sobbing, and moaning,
	The deepest, and wildest refrain
Swells forth with a melody pleading
For the heart that is stricken and bleeding.
Regrets for past promises waiting,
	And prophecies yet unfulfill’d;
The tears and the voice that is waiting,
	The strength of strong passions distill’d;
The agony past self-containing,
The grief that o’ermasters restraining.
			—Emmeline B. Wells.


[Column 1]
		AFTER LONG YEARS.
Amid the grand old woods of oak and pine.
I sit me down, ’mong mosses, flowers and
	ferns,
And thought, with magic pow’r almost divine;
Unconsciously to other days returns.
	An excess of joy must always be tem-
pered with sadness, or at least blended, as
the minor notes blend with those that are
high and shrill, and while nature revels in
its glorious beauty, and the spirit rejoices
in accordance therewith, there are also low
tones of music sweet and clear that thrill to
the very center of the human heart and
stir its deepest fountains, with a something
indefinable, but which harmonizes with the
twilight, and the soft murmur of the brook-
let, and the song of the nightingale. And
in the train of thought that comes with
twilight shadows, soft murmuring waters,
and the songs of thrust and nightingale,
there steals athwart the soul a pensive ten-
derness, that stirs the oldest memories, and
all unconsciously, we stand as ’twere in
places where our feet have trod many
“long years ago,” and vividly before us, as
by enchantment all the realisms coeval
with the very hour, live again within our
hearts ;we see the wood-path where we
wound our way through tangled vines and
broke the rotten wood, and hear perchance
the woodman’s axe again, as though its
echoes lingered in our souls these forty

[Column 2]
years, and pushing onward through the
tangled copse pierced with the briars, gath-
ering here and there a wild-flower, we feel
the conscious touch of some soft hand with-
in our own, and, blessed memory! it thrills
us now as it did then, and we might dream
of bliss like this forever, were it not that
some fierce night owl with red eyes, glares
at us, or screeches its horrid notes, and
wakes us, as it were, in terror; or a serpent
hisses beneath our feet and warns us that
life’s path’s beset with danger. Yes even in
our memory of the past, the reveries of
blissful thoughts, these horrid phantoms
glide and mock at human happiness.
	And thus in the brightest moments of
our lives we are pursued by shadows; and
tis well, for we cannot bear too much of
sunshine and graciously the shadows inter-
vene, and sadness blends with joy, as shad-
ows with the sunlight, and the stars shine
in the darkness, as hope mingles with our
sorrows; and we know all things by con-
trast; and after toil and weariness there’s
rest, and thus we learn life’s lesson, and if
we’re wise we’ll gather precious treasures
by the way, and enshrine them in our hearts,
that we may feast upon them in our lonely
hours. The harvest of good fruit sewn by
kind acts, and loving words and deeds of
simple unaffected charity. These are the
gems that will adorn the brows of those
who live to bless their fellow beings.
	How many such, who now have passed
away our memory can recall and they will
live forever; and every spring the fair
green earth shall see, shall be the sweeter,
fairer, fresher, purer and more fragrant, be-
cause of these dear ones, whose deeds of
holy love have consecrated them to God;
and in the blessed morn of the eternal
spring when heaven shall crown the earth
with that ecstatic kiss that thrilling
through every fiber shall wake the dead,
and breathe into their re-awakened souls,
the consciousness of all they are and were
and are to be ,then shall we know what
gave us this keen sense of all the beautiful,
this power to draw such perfect imagery
and call up memories that long years have
buried deep; and all the magic and un-
known, will be revealed to those whose mis-
sion has been nobly filled.
	None should sit idle while there are
flowers or gems to gather, though they
must plunge into the thicket to obtain
them, or dive into the ocean’s depths, and
if we let our fear of thorns and briars daunt
us, or even the creatures beneath; then we
must be content without the blessing, for
none can conquer but the brave. And
when the victory is won we can sit down
and count our sorrows o’er, but till that
time, we must push on, and yet there are
some pleasant pastures by the way, and
healing waters where the thirsty traveler
may drink; and if we are diligent who
knows but we may reach the everlasting
hills that tempt us in the distance, that
seem so near until we try to reach them.
					AUNT EM.


CITY PRIDE.
		Alice Merrill Horne.
	I hold as a fundamental principle of good
government that each citizen, whether rich
poor, sick, well, old or young, should be re-
quired by the commonwealth of which he
or she is a necessary unit, to do his or her
just portion of labor in making and sustain-
ing a healthful and beautiful city. Our city
has not yet come into a full understanding of
her own. She has individual moral and
physical responsibility. We ought to have
efficient municipal officers. Further than
that, we have an individual duty to give
aid, so far as we may, singly and unitedly,
in the solution of these difficult problems.
	Though we are far from the Millennium,
may we not, as a united body of women,
hasten the reign of righteousness by, a
proper use of the suffrage? Is it not pos-
sible for us to use that sacred trust so sa-
credly that no self-seeking man shall pre-
sume to rear his head before a public office?
	What would you think of an ordinance-
to compel residents or landholders to keep
out weeds? What result would follow com-
pulsory house-screening, stable-screening,
and garbage-screening for a few years?
	What follows weekly moving of garbage
and manure from premises? We look upon
each citizen as an asset in the municipal cor-
poration. The citizens constitute the mu-
nicipal corporation’s greatest wealth. She 
has no more important problem than to pro-
vide the best means for preserving the
health and thus lengthening the lives of her
citizens. Those most interested along-these
lines prescribe a clean and beautiful city.
	“Man is that he might have joy.” So
spoke the prophet, and we accept it as true
philosophy also.
	That man might have joy, the home is
made, the city is founded, municipal gov-
ernment is established, and what, tell me,
gives man more joy than a clean and beau-
tiful home, a clean and beautiful yard, a
clean and beautiful city? These indeed
make life sweet and build a perfect setting
for Godliness.
	I would like to bring to the hearts of
those present the opportunity that this won-
derful Young Ladies’ Mutual Improve-
ment Association has squarely before it,
even the privilege to organize and utilize an
enormous amount of energy (that is daily
wasted, misdrected, or lost) toward en-
riching soil, destroying weeds, placing, trees
and hedges, planting vegetable gardens, de-
signing flower beds and window boxes,
making lawns.
	We could, if we would, make of this city
a veritable garden of Eden. Let us set
aside beauty for a moment and become sor-
did. It would pay—alas that is the measure
into which everything must fit, for we are
in a Commercial process and at the com-
mercial stage. Yes, it would pay! A yard,
six rods by ten, this season produced fully
$50 in fruit and vegetables, and gave the


[Column 1]
same profit for flowers, and over one hun-
dred large bunches of flowers sent to
friends besides. So, it is a conservative
estimate that I give you when I say that
within two years from its entire setting out,
in the form of a park, a block would be sav-
ings its residents one thousand dollars per
year, and furnishing bouquets, too, for
friends.
	The residents on any block could unite.
Here we are friends, relatives, neighbors
of long standing. Let us take out the un-
sightly fences, mark our property lines with
rows of flowers, lay out the block as a park
—a combined vegetable garden, orchard,
vineyard, flower garden and lawn! It would
not only be a thing of beauty and a joy for-
ever, if it were unceasingly cared for; and
who would be content with the stale and
expensive market product after enjoying
the fresh vegetables and fruit from his own
vine and tree?
	I believe that the greatest difference be-
tween poverty and plenty, between poor
living and good living, is a home garden.
Then, just for a moment, think of that de-
graded poverty that never allows God’s
children to see even a bud unfold. Who
could have joy in such an unlovely life?
	It does cost work, it does take time. But
it brings joy! Digging is good for women,
for both old and young. Both of my grand-
mothers were gardeners of no mean order,
and both passed the eightieth mark and one
nearly reached ninety.
	There must be early rising. Say, did you
ever see a sunrise out in your own quiet
garden, where everything seemed waiting,
expectant, for the sunlight? Yes, garden-
ing pays, and in more ways than one.
	In parking a block, after the fences are
removed the soil should be fertilized by
manure and dead leaves that are available,
and must also be carted away. What a
waste to burn the leaves, and how dreadful
it makes the atmosphere. Walks should be
laid out and don’t be stingy about the
walks; make them broad and spacious. It
will add greatly to the beauty of your home.
Small, sunny patches should be filled in
with strawberries, rhubarb and asparagus,
and more shady spots will like English cur-
rants, goosberries, raspberries, sage, pars-
ley, mint, summer savory and thyme. Grapes
in the hottest soil and sunniest places, hung
on a long pergola or lattice, would be
charming. A small grove of English wal-
nuts and almonds, will pay large dividends
in good nuts, after eight years, and con-
tinue bearing many years. They are dec-
orative from the first. Mr. J. T. Harwood
has varieties of English walnuts that have
become acclimated to these parts and they
will not therefore freeze down in cold
weather, as all imported nut trees will.
	Now, right here is the ideal home of the
fruit trees. My grandfather, George A.
Smith, set out a splendid orchard of peaches,
pears, apples, apricots and plums.
	The centers of all our blocks are now
ugly weed patches; they offer a field for ro-
tating crops of corn, squash, potatoes, peas,
string beans, greens, carrots, beets, and onions.
	Now for flowers and lawns—the east side
has shown us what can be done with Salt
Lake. But before that, long years ago, oth-
ers blazed the way! Let us hark back to
when James Dwyer planted a rose garden in

[Column 2]
Salt Lake. Roses must not be planted
singly, but in rose gardens, and then we
have roses indeed.
	Have you forgotten the big lawn the Car-
rington girls made, irrigated and tended?
I think I sniff now the lovely, pure white
climbing roses on the south wall of the old
home—and there were the great red pe-
onies, too. Can you think back to the great
trees of white and purple lilacs, and the cab-
bage roses, all abloom in Grandmother Mer-
rill’s dooryard? Who has not been made
glad with a bunch of rare chrysanthemums
or roses from some wonderful flower gar-
den? On the old Irving school block, you
may find the finest General Jocks, asters and
sweet peas.
	Then there are the wonderful old moun-
tain willows—but few remain. A few re-
mained standing in the old University
grounds—now Salt Lake High school. How
I loved to pass near them, but alas the day
that I found the most beautiful ones among
them the monarchs of the grounds (and
there were many trees that were indeed
lovely) lying broken, helpless to lift their
dainty green, which always were the first
to open their buds in spring. Think of de-
stroying a half block of trees with a history
just to make a place in which boys and
girls might drill and play when a few blocks
away a hundred blocks lay empty, except of
sagebrush! Trees that, if they could speak,
could tell the story of the red men, of hunt-
ers with the flint and arrow, of dry, parched
desert places, of the entrance to this valley
of the pale face, of the first breaking of vir-
gin soil, the birth of irrigation, the hiding
of the desert’s nakedness beneath a mantle
of green—in a word, sentinels, witnesses of
all that our fathers have done. Yea, year
by year the grand old willows had lov-
ingly reached out in newer beauty, making
shadier seats under their wonderful willow
green tops—seats you and I have sought
for study periods. And now my heart
ached, my voice choked with tears when my
startled vision beheld them ruthlessly cut
down, destroyed forever. Now no re-
pentant hand could succor them or recall
the work of the cruel ax. Few remaining
trees were spared. Three stand on the
north side, just over the sidewalk, and one
on the west. They have, it is true, been
butchered, cut out of all true shape, but they
still retain the same wonderful color, the
same tender artistic feeling.
	We have but a few treasures left, a few
of the old trees, let us care for them and
make a beautiful setting for them. Shall we
then pull down the ugly fences, lay out
good walks, make lawns, plant rose gar-
dens, shrubs, flowers, orchards, vineyards,
and kitchen gardens? Let us do it now, and
make a beginning on our garden of Eden.
We have a beautiful city, by the inspiration
of God she has been set upon hills, slopes
that look out on a wonderful inland sea.
She is guarded and girded around by
mighty mountains, crowned with everlasting
snows, veiled with marvelous shades of
blue. She is blessed with clear, cooling
streams, fresh from ten thousand springs.
Her soils vary from sandy, graveled, loamy
to clayey earth and all can be made to grow
some lovely thing.
	Can we honestly lift our eyes in praise and
thanksgiving to God for these blessings and
permit the very earth under our feet, our
own inheritance, the sod encompassing our


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


THE SUFFRAGE MOVEMENT IN ENG-
			LAND.
	The phenomenon of the “Suffragette”—
generic title accorded to the representatives
of the militant movement on behalf of the
woman’s vote in England—has its organized
rival in a non-militant advocacy of the re-
form movement, whose adherents are
termed Suffragists even when respect is pas-
sive; besides these two, there is a third an-
tithetic party, frankly antagonistic to the
vote, who proclaim themselves as Anti-
suffragists. In addition, there is a surviv-
ing residue of English women whose fav-
orite policy is to evade the subject, but who,
if cornered for a definition of their views,
frown or smile, and confess to “not really
care for politics at all.”
	A dozen years ago in London, though the
term “New Woman,” exploited in a more
or less witty problem novel by Sarah
Grande, sounded somewhat stale, Women’s
Suffrage was not in the air current. What
was understood as essentially appertaining
to the matter had been discussed in courtly
fashion the foregone century: Tennyson’s
reflective intellect having bequeathed to
“sweet girl graduates” a sane analysis of
poignant complex questions in the sustained
rhythm, varied with melodic interludes, of
The Princess. To speak of “Women’s
Rights,” a score of years ago, was to recall
the domestic chaos of the Jellaby household,
if not to evoke simultaneously the shade of
Mrs. Pardiggle, whose visitations upon the
homes of the poor were inseparable with
havoc to outstanding articles wrought by
her sweeping train. If Dickens, or if Ten-
nyson, recalled to the scene of earthly vision,
could be asked to translate his impressions
of “The Signs of the Times,” as presented
by the campaign of his aggressive country-
women today, one wonders what creation of
an emancipated “type,” embodied in immor-
tal novel; what revelation of reversal to the
eternal feminine ideal, inspiring enchanting
verse, would be added to the world’s store?
	Though these return not, contemporary
literary opinion on the matter in question is
overtly expressed from time to time. Mr.
G. B. Shaw, the sardonic satirist of that
New England play, The Devil’s Disciple,
who has since, one suspects, become a satir-
ical poseur, has achieved a characteristic ex-
position of the subject in a stage-piece enti-
tled Fanny’s First Play. The acknowledged
head of the Anti-suffrage League is that
serene star of society and cultured conven-
tion, Mrs. Humphrey Ward. Miss Marie
Corelli has expressed a doubt as to the av-
erage British woman being a responsible
claimant for the imperial vote. Ouida,
whose latter volume of Critical Essays re-
vealed her to be secondary in intellectual
acumen to no predecessor amongst English-
speaking sister writers, left a word about
the matter prior to her lonely passing:—it
was a logical statement to the effect that the
assumption of' “equality” in certain senses
between the differently organized beings


[Column 1]
woman and man would result in the for-
feiture of that chivalry which is man’s defer-
ence, not to woman’s inherent weakness only,
but to her dower of delicacy and fineness,
psychal and temperamental, in the sum of
her strength.
	What, after all, is the destiny that woman
in her best development was intended by the
law of furtherance to fulfil? To achieve
submergence of her womanhood, a bur-
lesquing travesty of man, until Nature, out-
raged, brands her as a monstrous abortion,
or Time, in reactionary adjustment, disowns
her ancestry amidst a compensated genera-
tion’s laughter? Or is it for her to know
herself, complete, unique in individuality in-
deed, but in the electrical light of compar-
ison, seen decreed within herself to be, not
man’s double, but his complement, as surely
as he is hers? To recognize this is the rec-
ognize elemental truth, potentially motioned
towards infinite altitudes of the ideal in
realization. What, one ponders, can be the
attitude thereto of the militant Suffragists,
whose blatant egoism, revealed in vagaries
of ever increasing violence, has scourged the
land with lawlessness broadcast, soliciting
condonence of its crimes in pseudo-martyr-
dom?
	My introduction towards this form of suf-
fragism was at a certain stall of an exhibi-
tion, some seven years ago. Behind this stall
was a typical Suffragette, who, on my mak-
ing cursory examination of the pamphlets
and other suffrage “literature” of the stall,
advertised the opinions she was representing
in a hard, measured voice. She spoke in ex-
aggerated terms of a Miss Somebody whose
prowess appeared to consist in having in
various ways proved herself the equal of
man, in total independence of all masculine
aid, paternal or otherwise. “We” meaning
the amazonian army of the militant sister-
hood—“are prouder of her than of Joan of
Arc.”
	Joan the Maid, who spent her heroic life-
blood for a selfless realization of a dream, I
had heard of from time to time since daisy-
chain days: of the former lady never previ-
ously. The words and tone of the proclaimer
of her fame, however, brought by frictional
process to my mind the words of Tennyson:
	“Each like to each, yet like in difference.”
	“I think,” I replied, “a woman should
supplement, not substitute a man. Men
should be chivalrous.”
	Meanwhile the discussion had attracted a
little band of auditors around the stall.
Amongst them was an unmistakable French-
man, in an inverness cloak and wide-
brimmed hat; and a matronly elderly woman.
	At the latter words the Frenchman grave-
ly doffed his chapeau, while the woman said:
	“Men in England are not chivalrous as
they used to be in my younger days, and its
just the Suffragettes who are spoiling them
for us all!”
	Half a dozen years, and the Suffragette
vendor of the militant journal, Votes for
Women, has become as familiar a figure of
London streets as the Turkish bath sand-
wichman. Meanwhile, she had contrived to
contribute to the world a series of shocks.
Sudden sallies by surreptitious means into
the sacred precincts of parliament, attempted
invasion of the premier’s residence, have
been followed by wholesale smashing of
windows, and the destruction of the com-
munity’s letters in the public pillar-boxes.

[Column 2]
Churches have been burned to the ground,
priceless pictures ruthlessly destroyed;
policemen have been struck in the face in the
official discharge of duty by those Mrs.
Gamp would have been apt to describe with
certain qualifications as “female women.”
In goal for the crime of arson, when whole-
sale destruction of life has been narrowly
averted, the insurgent patriot on behalf of
women’s vote obtains a speedy release
through the ruse of hunger-striking; her re-
turn to freedom is celebrated by a banquet
at one of the most expensive hotels.
	But one day the world rings with an act
which makes the most daring deed of Don
Quixote pale before it. Not the quiet plain
which stretched around revolving wind-mills
that the Don mistook is the scene of adven-
ture, but the crowded spectacle of the royal
Derby race-course. A militant Suffragette
having evaded the barriers has thrown her-
self amongst the horses while they were
galloping by. A jockey, whose horse was
caused to stumble, escaped with a slight
injury; but in a few days the woman dies
of the hurts received. And the militant pro-
pagandists of the Vote advertise a spectac-
ular funeral. The cause has at length a
martyr.
	At the funeral of Don Quixote one would
surely weep; he sought not the applause of a
community, nor desired posthumous fame.
Save for one poor serving-man, the exploits
of his knight-errantry were performed alone.
He would not have hurt a lamb, whose iden-
tity was apparent as such. Alas! poor gen-
tleman, he was subject to illusions. But a 
woman of established sanity, in the hey-dey
of life’s prime, indued with the privilege of
intellectual culture—she had graduated from
a university with the degree of B. A.—had
thrown away her life for an act in which
not reason was justified, nor utility served.
	There is usually perceptible in a waiting
crowd the prevailing sentiment which has
gathered it together. It was a general half-
holiday, the day on which the dead Suffra-
gette’s funeral procession was to pass. The
route had been advertised, and in the bril-
liant sunshine the street-curbs were lined
with multitudes eager to be diverted by a
spectacle, possessed with sensational curi-
osity which had a certain morbid under-
current here and there. At length from the
ranks of onlookers was shouted, “Hark!
here they come!” A band was heard, and
nearer and nearer came the wailful strains of
Chopin’s Marche Funebre. Two glass
hearses appeared, containing wreaths and
other floral tributes. Then came an open
hearse, from which a crape-bound pennon
fluttered heavily; upon that, smothered in
white and purple flowers, was the coffin.
Then advanced a long procession of motley
banners. In the rear they stretched the
length of the street, high above the heads of
the crowd. Many of them rich in texture,
a few were emblematic in design; most,
however, reared a motto or scripture text in
purple letters upon a white background.
Certain of the former represented civic or-
ganizations conducted by men, whose polit-
ical opinions accord with the suffragist
movement. Most dolorous was one repre-
senting a society of women writers, the de-
sign of a rising sun in gold upon a white
background shining dimly through black
chiffon drapery. Of the texts and mottoes
held aloft, the selection or wording consti-
tuted a lurid revelation of the thought con-
fusion and the aberration of sentiment at

[Column 3]
the basis of the British Suffrage movement
as it appears in the prenomenon of “mili-
tancy.” For instance, “Give me Freedom or
give me Death,” seemed calculated to impel
a more casual mind than Pilate’s to turn
over the query, “What is Freedom?”
“Fight on, and God will give the Victory,”
the injunction on another banner, was some-
what provocative of inquiry as to the mili-
tant Suffragists’ religious creed. Certain
direct quotations from scripture had a more
painful effect upon those who hold the ideals
of Christianity in reverence. One of these,
indeed, in relation to that coffin, seemed su-
premely pitiful through the falseness of the
relevancy, “She hath done what she could.”
That the epitaph to describe the culminating
rashness of a once promising career, cut
short at its zenith? Had the act which cost
the woman graduate her life been to stop a
run-away dray, in order to save a little child,
or even a pet dog from injury, the applica-
tion might have served. But then there
would have been no pompous procession; no
banners nor bands for the diverted multi-
tude. What is it Charles Reade, in his
preface to a great book, has said concern-
ing sorrows suffered in silence, noble deeds
done without recognition, burdens bravely
borne every day by those “obscure heroes,
philosophers and martyrs that people earth?”
But a band in the rear was now passing, and
again the strains of Chopin wailed forth in-
finite lament. Well might the mournful music
wail on this bright day for the vigorous life
laid low prematurely. The woman in her
coffin was a martyr, after all; not in a great
and noble sense, but one supremely pitiful—
the martyr of a cause whose inherent blun-
derings have resulted in wild and wilder an-
archy, now at length emblazoning itself with
a vain apotheosis of its first victim unto
death. The pity of the individual tragedy of
a wasted sacrifice almost hallowed the propa-
gandists’ farce. As the music passed out of
hearing onward with the coffin towards the
station, I thought of certain lines learned
long ago by heart, which first conveyed the
idea of woman’s rights in a serious sense to
me. Perhaps the dead Suffragette had
known and felt them, too, until the octopus
of notoriety, with its fatal clutch, marred in
her the sense of the truth and fitness of
things. The lines are uttered by “The
Princess” to her fellow-seekers who would
stake the utmost for the highest:
						“Girls,
Knowledge is now no more a fountain seal’d:
Drink deep, until the habits of the slave,
Gossip and slander, die. Better not be
At all, than not be noble!”
			FLORENCE L. LANCASTER.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


FRUIT CAKE.
		(WITHOUT EGGS.)
	2 cups flour, 1 teaspoon cinnamon, 1 cup
raisins, 1 teaspoon cloves, 1 cup currants,
1 teaspoon soda, 1 cup water, 1/4 teaspoon
salt, 1/2 cup butter, 1 teaspoon lemon ex-
tract, 1 cup sugar.
	Candied peel and nutmeats may be added
if desired.
	Boil all ingredients (except flour, soda
and lemon), together for a few minutes.
When luke-warm, add flour, soda and lem-
on extract.  Mix thoroughly and bake in
slow oven, having oven hotter at first.
